### Choosing a Notetaking App: The Neural Center of Your Second Brain  
选择笔记应用程序: 第二大脑的神经中枢

The same technology that has fueled an explosion in the volume of information coming our way has also provided the tools to help us manage it.  
推动我们获取的信息量激增的技术也提供了帮助我们管理信息量的工具。

While your Second Brain is made up of all the tools you use to interact with information, including a to-do list, a calendar, email, and reading apps, for example, there is one category of software I recommend as the centerpiece of your Second Brain: a digital notetaking app.[I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-034) There are plentiful options available, from the free notes app that came preinstalled on your smartphone to third-party software you can download with exactly the features you need.  
虽然您的第二大脑由您用来与信息交互的所有工具组成, 例如待办事项列表、日历、电子邮件和阅读应用程序, 但我推荐一类软件作为您的核心第二大脑: 数字笔记应用程序。 [I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-034) 有很多选项可供选择, 从智能手机上预装的免费笔记 app 到可以下载的第三方软件, 这些软件都具有您需要的功能。

From Microsoft OneNote, Google Keep, and Apple Notes to Notion and Evernote, digital notes apps have four powerful characteristics that make them ideal for building a Second Brain. They are:  
从 Microsoft OneNote、Google Keep 和 Apple Notes 到 Notion 和 Evernote, 数字笔记应用程序具有四个强大的特性, 使其成为构建第二大脑的理想选择。他们是:

*   **Multimedia:** Just like a paper notebook might contain drawings and sketches, quotes and ideas, and even a pasted photo or Post-it, a notes app can store a wide variety of different kinds of content in one place, so you never need to wonder where to put something.  
    多媒体: 就像纸质笔记本可能包含绘图和素描、引语和想法, 甚至是粘贴的照片或便利贴一样, 笔记应用程序可以在一个地方存储各种不同类型的内容, 因此您永远不必怀疑放东西的地方。
*   **Informal:** Notes are inherently messy, so there’s no need for perfect spelling or polished presentation. This makes it as easy and frictionless as possible to jot things down as soon as they occur to you, which is essential to allow nascent ideas to grow.  
    非正式: 笔记本质上是杂乱无章的, 因此不需要完美的拼写或优美的呈现。这样一来, 您就可以尽可能轻松无阻地记下事情, 这对于让新生的想法成长至关重要。
*   **Open-ended:** Taking notes is a continuous process that never really ends, and you don’t always know where it might lead. Unlike more specialized kinds of software that are designed to produce a specific kind of output (such as slide decks, spreadsheets, graphics, or videos), notes are ideal for free-form exploration before you have a goal in mind.  
    开放式: 做笔记是一个持续不断的过程, 永远不会真正结束, 而且您并不总是知道它会通向哪里。与旨在生成特定类型输出 (例如幻灯片、电子表格、图形或视频) 的更专业的软件不同, 笔记是您在确定目标之前进行自由形式探索的理想选择。
*   **Action-oriented:** Unlike a library or research database, personal notes don’t need to be comprehensive or precise. They are designed to help you quickly capture stray thoughts so you can remain focused on the task at hand.  
    以行动为导向: 与图书馆或研究数据库不同, 个人笔记不需要全面或精确。它们旨在帮助您快速捕获杂念, 以便您可以专注于手头的任务。

All four of the above qualities are shared by paper notes, but when we make them digital, we can supercharge these timeless benefits with the incredible capabilities of technology—searching, sharing, backups, editing, linking, syncing between devices, and many others. Digital notes combine the casual artistry of a daily sketchbook with the scientific power of modern software.  
纸质笔记具有上述所有四种品质, 但当我们将它们数字化时, 我们可以利用令人难以置信的技术功能——搜索、共享、备份、编辑、链接、设备间同步等等, 来增强这些永恒的好处。数字笔记将日常速写本的随意艺术与现代软件的科学力量相结合。

Choosing which apps and tools you’ll use is a personal decision, and depends on which mobile device you use, the needs of your job or business, and even your own temperament and taste. The software landscape is dynamic and constantly changing. New apps regularly come out, and existing ones release innovative new features. You can find a free, continually updated guide to choosing your notes app and other Second Brain tools at [Buildingasecondbrain.com/resources](http://www.buildingasecondbrain.com/resources).  
选择使用哪些应用程序和工具是个人决定, 取决于您使用的移动设备、您的工作或业务需求, 甚至您自己的气质和品味。软件环境是动态的并且不断变化的。新的应用程序会定期推出, 而现有的应用程序会发布创新的新功能。您可以在 Buildingasecondbrain.com/resources 找到一份免费的、不断更新的指南, 以选择您的笔记应用程序和其他第二大脑工具。

Although you will always use many different software programs to manage information and do your work—from word processors to messaging platforms and project management tools used within your organization—your notes app is uniquely designed to facilitate personal knowledge management.  
尽管您总是会使用许多不同的软件程序来管理信息和完成您的工作——从文字处理器到消息平台和组织内使用的项目管理工具——你的笔记应用程序是专门为促进个人知识管理而设计的。

A good place to start is to look at the apps you already have and perhaps are already using. You can always start now with a basic option and upgrade later as your needs get more sophisticated.[II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-033)  
一个好的起点是查看您已经拥有并且可能已经在使用的应用程序。您始终可以从一个基本选项开始, 然后在您的需求变得更复杂时升级。 [II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-033)

Most important of all, don’t get caught in the trap of perfectionism: insisting that you have to have the “perfect” app with a precise set of features before you take a single note. It’s not about having the perfect tools—it’s about having a _reliable_ set of tools you can depend on, knowing you can always change them later.  
最重要的是, 不要陷入完美主义的陷阱: 坚持认为您必须拥有具有精确功能集的 “完美” 应用程序, 然后才能做一个笔记。这不是关于拥有完美的工具——而是关于拥有一套您可以依赖的可靠工具, 并且知道您以后可以随时更改它们。

### Remembering, Connecting, Creating: The Three Stages of Personal Knowledge Management  
记忆、联系、创造: 个人知识管理的三个阶段

As people set out on their Second Brain journey, there are three stages of progress I often observe—and even encourage. Those stages are _remembering_, _connecting_, and _creating_. It takes time to fully unlock the value of using digital tools to enhance and extend what our minds are capable of, but there are also distinct benefits at every step along the way.  
当人们踏上他们的第二大脑之旅时, 我经常观察到三个阶段的进展——甚至鼓励。这些阶段是 remembering、connecting 和 creating。完全释放使用数字工具来增强和扩展我们的思维能力的价值需要时间, 但在此过程中的每一步都有明显的好处。

The first way that people tend to use their Second Brain is as a memory aid. They use their digital notes to save facts and ideas that they would have trouble recalling otherwise: takeaways from meetings, quotes from interviews, or the details of a project, for example.  
人们倾向于使用第二大脑的第一种方式是作为记忆辅助工具。他们使用数字笔记来保存他们难以回忆的事实和想法: 例如, 会议要点、采访引述或项目细节。

Camille is the cofounder and lead designer of a start-up in Quebec, Canada. She uses her Second Brain to save excerpts from the many research reports and studies she reads as part of her work designing electric vehicle charging stations for large residential buildings. Most of these reports are published as PDFs, a notoriously inflexible and difficult-to-use format, but by importing the findings most relevant to her work into her notes, she can add as many annotations and comments as she wants.  
Camille 是加拿大魁北克一家初创公司的联合创始人和首席设计师。作为她为大型住宅楼设计电动汽车充电站的工作的一部分, 她使用她的第二大脑保存了她阅读的许多研究报告和研究的摘录。大多数这些报告都以 PDF 格式发布, 这是一种众所周知的不灵活且难以使用的格式, 但通过将与她的工作最相关的发现导入她的笔记, 她可以根据需要添加任意数量的注释和评论。

The second way that people use their Second Brain is to connect ideas together. Their Second Brain evolves from being primarily a _memory_ tool to becoming a _thinking_ tool. A piece of advice from a mentor comes in handy as they encounter a similar situation on a different team. An illuminating metaphor from a book finds its way into a presentation they’re delivering. The ideas they’ve captured begin gravitating toward each other and cross-pollinating.  
人们使用第二大脑的第二种方式是将想法联系在一起。他们的第二大脑从最初的记忆工具演变为思维工具。当他们在不同的团队遇到类似情况时, 来自导师的一条建议会派上用场。一本书中的一个有启发性的比喻进入了他们正在提供的演示文稿中。他们捕捉到的想法开始相互吸引并相互授粉。

Fernando is an oncologist at a world-renowned hospital who uses his Second Brain to organize his patient notes. He summarizes the key points from each patient’s health history with a special focus on how long they’ve had the condition, which treatments they’ve received, and key features of their tumor. Fernando uses his Second Brain to _connect_ what he knows from his training and research to the needs of his patients, to be able to treat them more effectively.  
费尔南多是一家世界知名医院的肿瘤科医生, 他使用他的第二大脑来整理他的病人笔记。他总结了每位患者健康史的要点, 特别关注他们患病的时间、接受的治疗以及肿瘤的主要特征。费尔南多使用他的第二大脑将他从培训和研究中获得的知识与患者的需求联系起来, 以便能够更有效地治疗他们。

Eventually, the third and final way that people use their Second Brain is for creating new things. They realize that they have a lot of knowledge on a subject and decide to turn it into something concrete and shareable. Seeing so much supporting material ready and waiting gives them the courage to put their own ideas out there and have a positive impact on others.  
最终, 人们使用第二大脑的第三种也是最后一种方式是创造新事物。他们意识到他们在某个主题上拥有大量知识, 并决定将其转化为具体且可共享的东西。看到如此多的支持材料准备就绪并等待着, 他们有勇气将自己的想法付诸实践并对他人产生积极影响。

Terrell is a young father of three working a demanding job at a large tech company in Texas. After taking my course, he used his Second Brain to start a YouTube channel where he shares stories and tips on parenting. For example, he’s made videos on how to travel internationally with kids and how to request paternity leave, and shared clips from weekend trips he’s taken with his family.  
Terrell 是三个孩子的年轻父亲, 在德克萨斯州的一家大型科技公司从事一份要求很高的工作。上完我的课程后, 他用他的第二大脑开了一个 YouTube 频道, 在那里他分享育儿故事和技巧。例如, 他制作了有关如何带孩子出国旅行以及如何申请陪产假的视频, 并分享了他与家人周末旅行的片段。

Being able to keep track of all the video ideas and production details outside his head has been crucial in allowing Terrell to balance his side gig with his full-time career, while still having enough time with his kids. He is using his Second Brain to express himself and _create_ the content he wants to see in the world.  
能够在头脑之外跟踪所有视频创意和制作细节对于让 Terrell 平衡他的副业和他的全职工作, 同时仍然有足够的时间陪伴他的孩子至关重要。他正在使用他的第二大脑来表达自己并创造他想在世界上看到的内容。

Each of these individuals has leveraged technology to remember, connect, and create far more effectively than if they had to do it on their own.[8](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch03fn8) They use their Second Brain in ways that complement the current season of their lives. As those seasons change, they will be able to adapt how they use their notes so that they remain relevant and useful.  
这些人中的每一个人都利用技术来记忆、联系和创造, 这比他们必须自己做的要有效得多。 [8](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch03fn8) 他们以补充当前生活季节的方式使用他们的第二大脑。随着这些季节的变化, 他们将能够调整他们使用笔记的方式, 以便它们保持相关性和有用性。

### Introducing The CODE Method: The Four Steps to Remembering What Matters  
介绍 CODE 方法: 记住重要事项的四个步骤

To guide you in the process of creating your own Second Brain, I’ve developed a simple, intuitive four-part method called “CODE”—Capture; Organize; Distill; Express.  
为了指导您创建自己的第二大脑, 我开发了一种简单、直观的四部分方法, 称为 “CODE”——捕获; 组织; 蒸馏; 表达。

These are the steps not only to build your Second Brain in the first place, but also to work with it going forward. Each of these steps represents a timeless principle found throughout humanity’s history, from the earliest cave paintings to the artisan workshops of the Renaissance to the most cutting-edge modern fields. They are flexible and agnostic for any profession, role, or career and for whatever notetaking methods and platforms you prefer. I’m even willing to bet that you’re doing them already in some form, whether you realize it or not.  
这些步骤不仅是建立你的第二大脑的第一步, 也是让它向前发展的步骤。从最早的洞穴壁画到文艺复兴时期的工匠作坊, 再到最前沿的现代领域, 这些步骤中的每一步都代表了贯穿人类历史的永恒原则。它们灵活且不可知, 适用于任何职业、角色或职业, 也适用于您喜欢的任何笔记方法和平台。我什至敢打赌, 无论您是否意识到, 您已经以某种形式在做这些事情。

CODE is a map for navigating the endless streams of information we are now faced with every day. It is a modern approach to creating a commonplace book, adapted to the needs of the Information Age.  
CODE 是一张地图, 用于导航我们现在每天面对的无穷无尽的信息流。这是一种现代的方法来创建一本平凡的书, 适应信息时代的需要。

The same way we have a genetic code that determines our height and eye color, we also have a _creative_ code that is hardwired into our imagination. It shapes how we think and how we interact with the world. It is mirrored in the _software_ code that runs the apps we use to handle information. It’s also been a _secret_ code for most of history—now it’s finally time to reveal how it works.[III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-032)  
就像我们拥有决定身高和眼睛颜色的遗传密码一样, 我们也拥有根植于我们想象力的创意密码。它塑造了我们的思维方式以及我们与世界互动的方式。它反映在运行我们用来处理信息的应用程序的软件代码中。它也是大部分历史的密码——现在终于要揭示它是如何工作的了。 [III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-032)

Let’s preview each of the four steps of the CODE Method—Capture, Organize, Distill, and Express—and then we’ll dive into the details in the following chapters.  
让我们预览一下 CODE 方法的四个步骤——捕获、组织、提炼和表达——然后我们将在后面的章节中深入探讨细节。

#### Capture: Keep What Resonates 捕获: 保持共鸣

Every time we turn on our smartphone or computer, we are immediately immersed in the flow of juicy content they present. Much of this information is useful and interesting—how-to articles that could make us more productive, podcasts with experts sharing hard-won lessons, or inspiring photos of travel destinations we might want to visit.  
每次我们打开智能手机或电脑时, 我们都会立即沉浸在它们呈现的精彩内容流中。其中大部分信息既有用又有趣——可以提高我们工作效率的操作方法文章、专家分享来之不易的经验教训的播客, 或者我们可能想去的旅行目的地的鼓舞人心的照片。

Here’s the problem: we can’t consume every bit of this information stream. We will quickly be exhausted and overwhelmed if we try. We need to adopt the perspective of a curator, stepping back from the raging river and starting to make intentional decisions about what information we want to fill our minds.  
这就是问题所在: 我们无法消耗此信息流的每一点。如果我们尝试, 我们很快就会精疲力竭和不知所措。我们需要采用策展人的视角, 从汹涌的河流中退后一步, 开始有意识地决定我们想用什么信息来填满我们的脑海。

Like a scientist capturing only the rarest butterflies to take back to the lab, our goal should be to “capture” only the ideas and insights we think are truly noteworthy. Content tends to pile up all around us even without our involvement. There are probably emails filling your inbox, updates popping up in your social media feeds, and notifications proliferating on your smartphone as you’re reading this.  
就像科学家只捕捉最稀有的蝴蝶带回实验室一样, 我们的目标应该是只 “捕捉” 我们认为真正值得注意的想法和见解。即使没有我们的参与, 内容也会堆积在我们周围。当您阅读本文时, 您的收件箱中可能充满了电子邮件, 社交媒体源中弹出更新, 智能手机上的通知激增。

It’s already there, but we tend to capture it haphazardly, at best. You might email yourself a quick note, brainstorm some ideas in a document, or highlight quotes in a book you’re reading, but that information probably remains disconnected and scattered. The insights you uncovered through serious mental effort remain hidden in forgotten folders or drifting in the cloud.  
它已经存在, 但我们最多只能随意捕捉它。您可能会通过电子邮件给自己发送一封简短的便条, 在文档中集思广益, 或者在您正在阅读的书中突出显示引文, 但这些信息可能仍然不连贯且分散。您通过认真的脑力劳动发现的见解仍然隐藏在被遗忘的文件夹中或漂浮在云中。

The solution is to _keep only what resonates_ in a trusted place that you control, and to leave the rest aside.  
解决方案是只保留在您控制的可信赖位置产生共鸣的内容, 而将其余部分放在一边。

When something resonates, it moves you on an intuitive level. Often, the ideas that resonate are the ones that are most unusual, counterintuitive, interesting, or potentially useful. Don’t make it an analytical decision, and don’t worry about why exactly it resonates—just look inside for a feeling of pleasure, curiosity, wonder, or excitement, and let that be your signal for when it’s time to capture a passage, an image, a quote, or a fact.  
当某些东西产生共鸣时, 它会在直觉层面上打动你。通常, 引起共鸣的想法是最不寻常、违反直觉、有趣或可能有用的想法。不要让它成为一个分析性的决定, 也不要担心它到底为什么会引起共鸣——只要看看内心是否有一种愉悦、好奇、惊奇或兴奋的感觉, 让它成为你捕捉一段话的信号、图像、引述或事实。

By training ourselves to notice when something resonates with us, we can improve not only our ability to take better notes, but also our understanding of ourselves and what makes us tick. It is a way of turning up the volume on our intuition so we can hear the wisdom it offers us.  
通过训练自己去注意什么时候能引起我们的共鸣, 我们不仅可以提高记笔记的能力, 还可以提高我们对自己的理解, 以及是什么让我们打勾。这是一种调高我们直觉音量的方法, 这样我们就能听到它为我们提供的智慧。

Adopting the habit of knowledge capture has immediate benefits for our mental health and peace of mind. We can let go of the fear that our memory will fail us at a crucial moment. Instead of jumping at every new headline and notification, we can choose to consume information that adds value to our lives and consciously let go of the rest.  
养成获取知识的习惯对我们的心理健康和内心平静有立竿见影的好处。我们可以不再担心我们的记忆力会在关键时刻失灵。与其跳进每一个新的标题和通知, 我们可以选择消费能为我们的生活增加价值的信息, 并有意识地放弃其他信息。

#### Organize: Save for Actionability 组织: 为可操作性而保存

Once you’ve begun capturing notes with the ideas that resonate with you, you’ll eventually feel the need to organize them.  
一旦你开始用与你产生共鸣的想法来记录笔记, 你最终会觉得有必要组织它们。

It is tempting to try to create a perfect hierarchy of folders up front to contain every possible note you might ever want to capture. Even if it was possible, that approach would be incredibly time-consuming and require way too much effort, pulling you away from what interests you today.  
尝试预先创建一个完美的文件夹层次结构以包含您可能想要捕获的所有可能的笔记是很诱人的。即使有可能, 这种方法也会非常耗时并且需要太多的努力, 让你远离你今天感兴趣的事情。

Most people tend to organize information by subject, like the Dewey decimal system you’ve probably seen at the library. For example, you might find a book under a broad subject category like “Architecture,” “Business,” “History,” or “Geology.”  
大多数人倾向于按主题组织信息, 例如您可能在图书馆见过的杜威十进制系统。例如, 您可能会在 “建筑”、“商业”、“历史” 或“地质学”等广泛学科类别下找到一本书。

When it comes to digital notes, we can use much easier and lighter ways of organizing. Because our priorities and goals can change at a moment’s notice, and probably will, we want to avoid organizing methods that are overly rigid and prescriptive. The best way to organize your notes is to _organize for action_, according to the active projects you are working on right now. Consider new information in terms of its _utility_, asking, “How is this going to help me move forward one of my current projects?”  
当谈到数字笔记时, 我们可以使用更简单、更轻松的组织方式。因为我们的优先事项和目标可能会在一瞬间发生变化, 而且很可能会发生变化, 所以我们希望避免组织过于僵化和规定性的方法。组织笔记的最佳方式是根据您目前正在进行的活动项目来组织行动。从效用的角度考虑新信息, 问 “这将如何帮助我推进我当前的项目之一?”

Surprisingly, when you focus on taking action, the vast amount of information out there gets radically streamlined and simplified. There are relatively few things that are actionable and relevant at any given time, which means you have a clear filter for ignoring everything else.  
令人惊讶的是, 当您专注于采取行动时, 那里的大量信息会从根本上得到精简和简化。在任何给定时间, 可操作和相关的事情相对较少, 这意味着您有一个清晰的过滤器来忽略其他一切。

Organizing for action gives you a sense of tremendous clarity, because you know that everything you’re keeping actually has a purpose. You know that it aligns with your goals and priorities. Instead of organizing being an obstacle to your productivity, it becomes a contributor to it.  
组织行动让你有一种无比清晰的感觉, 因为你知道你所做的一切实际上都是有目的的。您知道它符合您的目标和优先事项。组织不是你生产力的障碍, 而是成为它的贡献者。

#### Distill: Find the Essence 提炼: 找到本质

Once you start capturing ideas in a central place and organizing them for action, you’ll inevitably begin to notice patterns and connections between them.  
一旦你开始在一个中心位置捕捉想法并组织它们以采取行动, 你将不可避免地开始注意到它们之间的模式和联系。

An article you read about gardening will give you an insight into growing your customer base. An offhand testimonial from a client will give you the idea to create a web page with all your client testimonials. A business card will remind you of a fascinating conversation you had with someone who you could reach out to for coffee.  
您阅读的有关园艺的文章将使您深入了解如何扩大客户群。来自客户的临时推荐会让您产生创建一个包含所有客户推荐的网页的想法。名片会让您想起您与某个人进行的精彩对话, 您可以伸手去喝杯咖啡。

The human mind is like a sizzling-hot frying pan of associations—throw a handful of seeds in there and they’ll explode into new ideas like popcorn. Every note is the seed of an idea, reminding you of what you already know and already think about a topic.  
人的头脑就像一个由各种联想组成的滚烫的煎锅——往里面撒一把种子, 它们就会像爆米花一样爆出新的想法。每一个笔记都是一个想法的种子, 提醒你你已经知道和已经思考过的话题。

There is a powerful way to facilitate and speed up this process of rapid association: _distill your notes down to their essence_.  
有一种强大的方法可以促进和加速这种快速联想的过程: 将你的笔记提炼成它们的本质。

Every idea has an “essence”: the heart and soul of what it is trying to communicate. It might take hundreds of pages and thousands of words to fully explain a complex insight, but there is always a way to convey the core message in just a sentence or two.  
每个想法都有一个 “本质”: 它试图传达的内容的核心和灵魂。可能需要数百页和数千字才能完全解释一个复杂的见解, 但总有一种方法可以用一两句话传达核心信息。

Einstein famously summarized his revolutionary new theory of physics with the equation E=mc2. If he can distill his thinking into such an elegant equation, you can surely summarize the main points of any article, book, video, or presentation so that the main point is easy to identify.  
爱因斯坦用方程式 E=mc 2 总结了他革命性的物理学新理论。如果他能将他的思想提炼成这样一个优雅的方程式, 那么你一定可以概括任何文章、书籍、视频或演示文稿的要点, 使要点易于识别。

Why is it so important to be able to easily find the main point of a note? Because in the midst of a busy workday, you won’t have time to review ten pages of notes on a book you read last year—you need to be able to quickly find just the main takeaways.  
为什么能够轻松找到笔记的要点如此重要? 因为在忙碌的工作日中, 您没有时间复习去年读过的一本书的十页笔记——您需要能够快速找到主要内容。

If you’ve highlighted those takeaways in the flow of the reading you were already doing anyway, you’ll be able to remind yourself of what the book contains without having to spend hours rereading it.  
如果你已经在阅读的过程中强调了这些要点, 你就可以提醒自己这本书的内容, 而不必花几个小时重新阅读它。

Every time you take a note, ask yourself, “How can I make this as useful as possible for my future self?” That question will lead you to annotate the words and phrases that explain why you saved a note, what you were thinking, and what exactly caught your attention.  
每次做笔记时, 问问自己:“我怎样才能使它对未来的自己尽可能有用?” 该问题将引导您对单词和短语进行注释, 这些单词和短语可以解释您保存笔记的原因、您的想法以及究竟是什么引起了您的注意。

Your notes will be useless if you can’t decipher them in the future, or if they’re so long that you don’t even try. Think of yourself not just as a _taker_ of notes, but as a _giver_ of notes—you are giving your future self the gift of knowledge that is easy to find and understand.  
如果您将来无法破译笔记, 或者如果它们太长以至于您甚至都不想尝试, 那么您的笔记将毫无用处。不仅要将自己视为记笔记的人, 还要将自己视为记笔记的人——您正在为未来的自己提供易于查找和理解的知识礼物。

#### Express: Show Your Work 表达: 展示你的作品

All the previous steps—capturing, organizing, and distilling—are geared toward one ultimate purpose: sharing your own ideas, your own story, and your own knowledge with others.  
前面所有的步骤——捕捉、组织和提炼——都是为了一个最终目的: 与他人分享你自己的想法、你自己的故事和你自己的知识。

What is the point of knowledge if it doesn’t help anyone or produce anything?[IV](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-031) Whether your goal is to lose weight, get a promotion at work, start a side business, or strengthen your local community, personal knowledge management exists to support taking action—anything else is a distraction.  
如果没有帮助到任何人或产生任何东西, 知识还有什么意义? [IV](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-031) 无论您的目标是减肥、在工作中获得晋升、开展副业, 还是加强您当地的社区, 个人知识管理的存在都是为了支持采取行动——其他任何事情都会让人分心。

A common challenge for people who are curious and love to learn is that we can fall into the habit of continuously force-feeding ourselves more and more information, but never actually take the next step and apply it. We compile tons of research, but never put forward our own proposal. We gather untold business case studies, but never pitch one potential client. We study every piece of relationship advice available, but never ask anyone out on a date.  
对于好奇和热爱学习的人来说, 一个共同的挑战是, 我们可能会养成不断强迫自己输入越来越多信息的习惯, 但从未真正采取下一步行动并加以应用。我们汇集了大量研究, 但从未提出自己的建议。我们收集了数不清的商业案例研究, 但从不推销一个潜在客户。我们研究每条可用的关系建议, 但从不约任何人约会。

It’s so easy to endlessly delay and postpone the experiences that would so enrich our lives. We think we’re not ready. We fear we’re not prepared. We cannot stand the thought that there is one little piece of information we’re missing that, if we had it, would make all the difference.  
无休止地拖延和推迟那些可以丰富我们生活的经历是很容易的。我们认为我们还没有准备好。我们担心我们没有准备好。我们不能容忍这样的想法, 即我们遗漏了一小段信息, 如果我们拥有它, 就会有很大的不同。

I’m here to tell you that that is no way to live your life. Information becomes _knowledge_—personal, embodied, verified—only when we put it to use. You gain confidence in what you know only when you know that it works. Until you do, it’s just a theory.  
我在这里告诉你, 那不是你的生活方式。信息成为知识——个人的、体现的、经过验证的——只有当我们使用它时。只有当你知道它有效时, 你才会对你所知道的有信心。在您这样做之前, 这只是一个理论。

This is why I recommend you shift as much of your time and effort as possible from consuming to creating.[V](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-030) We all naturally have a desire to create—to bring to life something good, true, or beautiful.[9](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch03fn9) It’s a part of our essential nature. Creating new things is not only one of the most deeply fulfilling things we can do, it can also have a positive impact on others—by inspiring, entertaining, or educating them.  
这就是为什么我建议您将尽可能多的时间和精力从消费转移到创造上。 [V](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-030) 我们天生就有创造的愿望——将美好、真实或美丽的事物带入生活。 [9](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch03fn9) 这是我们本质的一部分。创造新事物不仅是 我们能做的最有成就感的事情之一, 它还可以通过启发、娱乐或教育他人对他人产生积极影响。

What should you create? 你应该创造什么?

It depends on your skills, interests, and personality. If you are highly analytical, you could evaluate the many options for camping gear and create a list of recommended products to share with your friends. If you like to teach, you could record your favorite dessert recipe and post it on social media or a blog. If you care about a local cause such as public parks, you could create a plan to lobby the city council for more funding.  
这取决于你的技能、兴趣和个性。如果您具有很强的分析能力, 您可以评估露营装备的众多选择, 并创建一个推荐产品列表与您的朋友分享。如果你喜欢教书, 你可以记录你最喜欢的甜点食谱并将其发布在社交媒体或博客上。如果您关心公园等当地事业, 您可以制定一个计划来游说市议会以获得更多资金。

All these actions—evaluate, share, teach, record, post, and lobby[VI](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-029)—are synonyms for the act of expression. They all draw on outside sources for raw material, they all involve a practical process of refinement over time, and they all end up making an impact on someone or something that matters to you.  
所有这些行为——评估、分享、教导、记录、发帖和游说 [VI](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-029) ——都是表达行为的同义词。它们都利用外部资源获取原材料, 它们都涉及随着时间的推移进行改进的实际过程, 并且它们最终都会对对你重要的人或事产生影响。

Information is always in flux, and it is always a work in progress. Since nothing is ever truly final, there is no need to wait to get started. You can publish a simple website now, and slowly add additional pages over time. You can send out a draft of a piece of writing now and make revisions later when you have more time. The sooner you begin, the sooner you start on the path of improvement.  
信息总是在不断变化, 而且总是在进行中。因为没有什么是真正最终的, 所以没有必要等待开始。您现在可以发布一个简单的网站, 然后慢慢添加其他页面。你现在可以把一篇文章的草稿发出去, 等你有更多时间再做修改。越早开始, 就越早走上改进之路。

I’ve introduced a lot of new concepts and terms, and I know at this point it can seem a little overwhelming. It may feel like you have to learn and do a lot of new things to be able to build a Second Brain.  
我介绍了很多新概念和术语, 我知道在这一点上它看起来有点不知所措。可能感觉你必须学习和做很多新事物才能建立第二大脑。

Here’s the surprising truth: you are already doing most of the work required.  
这是一个令人惊讶的事实: 您已经完成了大部分所需的工作。

You are already learning new things—you couldn’t stop if you wanted to. You already consume interesting ideas—note the dozens of tabs you likely have open in your browser. You already put in so much effort to keep track of all the information you need for your studies, your job, or your business. All you need is a slightly more intentional, more deliberate way to manage that information, plus a few practical habits to ensure it gets done.  
你已经在学习新事物——如果你想停下来是停不下来的。您已经消费了有趣的想法——注意您可能在浏览器中打开的几十个 of 选项卡。您已经付出了如此多的努力来跟踪学习、工作或业务所需的所有信息。您所需要的只是一种稍微更有目的性、更深思熟虑的方式来管理这些信息, 再加上一些实用的习惯来确保它完成。

In Part Two, I will show you how to use the CODE steps to radically expand your memory, intelligence, and creativity. For each step, I’ll share a set of practical techniques that you can implement today that will begin to yield benefits tomorrow. Techniques that don’t require any advanced technology—only the everyday devices and apps you have in your pocket and on your desk right now.  
在第二部分中, 我将向您展示如何使用 CODE 步骤从根本上扩展您的记忆力、智力和创造力。对于每个步骤, 我将分享一组实用技术, 您今天可以实施这些技术, 明天就会开始产生效益。不需要任何先进技术的技术——只需要您现在口袋里和办公桌上的日常设备和应用程序。

1.  [I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-034-backlink). Many people who follow the CODE methodology continue to use paper for their notes. Many even find that they take _more_ notes on paper once they have a way of capturing those notes digitally and saving them in a secure place. It’s not black-or-white. It’s about choosing the right tool for the job. This book is focused primarily on the potential of digital notes.  
    我 。许多遵循 CODE 方法的人继续使用纸张做笔记。许多人甚至发现, 一旦他们有办法以数字方式捕捉这些笔记并将它们保存在安全的地方, 他们就会在纸上做更多的笔记。它不是非黑即白的。这是关于为工作选择合适的工具。本书主要关注数字笔记的潜力。
2.  [II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-033-backlink). Most notes apps provide ways of exporting your notes in standard formats that can then be imported into other apps. I personally have switched platforms twice (from Microsoft Word to Google Docs, and then later to Evernote) and expect to switch to new platforms regularly in the future as technology advances.  
    二。大多数笔记应用程序都提供以标准格式导出笔记的方法, 然后可以将这些格式导入其他应用程序。我个人已经两次切换平台 (从 Microsoft Word 到 Google Docs, 然后再到 Evernote), 并希望随着技术的进步在未来定期切换到新平台。
3.  [III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-032-backlink). In a wonderful coincidence, recent research by neurophysiologists May-Britt Moser and Edvard Moser at the Norwegian University of Science and Technology indicates that the human brain remembers information using a “grid code”—a part of the brain involved in spatial reasoning. They speculate that “the grid code could therefore be some sort of metric or coordinate system” that can “uniquely and efficiently represent a lot of information.”  
    三、一个奇妙的巧合是, 挪威科技大学的神经生理学家 May-Britt Moser 和 Edvard Moser 最近的研究表明, 人脑使用 “网格代码” 记住信息——大脑中参与空间推理的部分。他们推测,“网格代码因此可能是某种公制或坐标系”, 可以“独特而有效地表示大量信息”。
4.  [IV](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-031-backlink). The word “productivity” has the same origin as the Latin verb _producere_, which means “to produce.” Which means that at the end of the day, if you can’t point to some kind of output or result you’ve produced, it’s questionable whether you’ve been productive at all.  
    四、 “生产力” 一词与拉丁语动词 producere 同源, 意思是 “生产”。这意味着在一天结束时, 如果你不能指出你产生的某种输出或结果, 那么你是否有生产力就值得怀疑了。
5.  [V](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-030-backlink). The consumerist attitude toward information—that more is better, that we never have enough, and that what we already have isn’t good enough—is at the heart of many people’s dissatisfaction with how they spend their time online. Instead of trying to find “the best” content, I recommend instead switching your focus to making things, which is far more satisfying.  
    诉。对信息的消费主义态度——越多越好, 我们永远都不够, 我们已经拥有的还不够好——是许多人对他们如何度过在线时间的不满的核心。我建议不要试图找到 “最好的” 内容, 而是将注意力转移到制作东西上, 这样会更令人满意。
6.  [VI](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-029-backlink). Other synonyms for expression include publish, speak, present, perform, produce, write, draw, interpret, critique, or translate.  
    六. 表达的其他同义词包括发布、演讲、展示、表演、制作、写作、绘画、解释、批评或翻译。

PART TWO The Method The Four Steps of CODE  
PART TWO CODE 的四个步骤
----------------------------------------------------------------

Chapter 4 Capture—Keep What Resonates  
Chapter 4 Capture——保持共鸣
---------------------------------------------------------------

> Everything not saved will be lost.  
> 没有保存的一切都将丢失。
> 
> —Nintendo “Quit Screen” message—任天堂 “退出屏幕” 信息

Information is food for the brain. It’s no accident that we call new ideas “food for thought.”  
信息是大脑的食物。我们将新想法称为 “深思熟虑” 并非偶然。

It’s clear that we need food and water to survive. What may not be so clear is that we also need information to live: to understand and adapt to our environment; to maintain relationships and cooperate with others; and to make wise decisions that further our interests.  
很明显, 我们需要食物和水才能生存。可能不太清楚的是, 我们还需要信息来生存: 了解和适应我们的环境; 与他人保持关系和合作; 做出有利于我们利益的明智决定。

Information isn’t a luxury—it is the very basis of our survival.  
信息不是奢侈品——它是我们生存的基础。

Just as with the food we put into our bodies, it is our responsibility and right to choose our information diet. It’s up to us to decide what information is good for us, what we want more of and less of, and ultimately, what we do with it. You are what you consume, and that applies just as much to information as to nutrition.  
就像我们放入体内的食物一样, 选择我们的信息饮食是我们的责任和权利。由我们来决定哪些信息对我们有好处, 我们想要更多或更少的信息, 以及最终我们如何处理这些信息。你就是你消费的东西, 这同样适用于信息和营养。

A Second Brain gives us a way to filter the information stream and curate only the very best ideas we encounter in a private, trusted place. Think of it as planting your own “knowledge garden” where you are free to cultivate your ideas and develop your own thinking away from the deafening noise of other people’s opinions.  
第二大脑为我们提供了一种过滤信息流的方法, 并只整理我们在一个私人的、值得信赖的 place 中遇到的最好的想法。把它想象成种植你自己的 “知识花园”, 在那里你可以自由地培养你的想法和发展你自己的思维, 远离其他人意见的震耳欲聋的噪音。

A garden is only as good as its seeds, so we want to start by seeding our knowledge garden with only the most interesting, insightful, useful ideas we can find.  
一座花园的好坏取决于它的种子, 所以我们希望从播种我们的知识花园开始, 只用我们能找到的最有趣、最有洞察力、最有用的想法。

You may already consume a lot of content from many different sources, but perhaps never put much thought into what you do with it afterward. Maybe you are already a diligent organizer, but you’ve fallen into a habit of “digital hoarding” that doesn’t end up enriching your life. Or, if this is all completely new to you, you may be starting at square one.  
您可能已经使用了来自许多不同来源的大量内容, 但可能从未考虑过之后如何处理这些内容。也许你已经是一个勤奋的组织者, 但你已经养成了 “数字囤积” 的习惯, 这并不能丰富你的生活。或者, 如果这对您来说是全新的, 您可能会从第一步开始。

No matter your situation, let’s start at the very beginning—how to use the first step of CODE to begin building your own private collection of knowledge.  
无论您的情况如何, 让我们从头开始——如何使用 CODE 的第一步开始构建您自己的私人知识库。

Taylor Swift is an icon of modern pop and country music and one of the best-selling music artists in history. Her nine chart-topping albums have sold over two hundred million copies worldwide and earned her a long list of awards, including eleven Grammy Awards. Not only does she appear in lists of the greatest singer-songwriters of all time, her influence has transcended music and placed her on such lists as the Time 100 and Forbes Celebrity 100.[1](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn1)  
泰勒斯威夫特是现代流行音乐和乡村音乐的偶像, 也是历史上最畅销的音乐艺术家之一。她的九张排行榜冠军专辑在全球销量超过 2 亿张, 并为她赢得了一长串奖项, 其中包括 11 项格莱美奖。她不仅出现在有史以来最伟大的创作歌手名单中, 她的影响力已经超越了音乐领域, 使她登上了时代 100 强和福布斯名人 100 强等榜单。 [1](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn1)

Over the course of her career, Swift has released five documentary films revealing her creative songwriting process. In all of them, she can be found with her head buried in her phone. As she says: “I disappear into my phone because my phone is where I keep my notes and my phone is where I’m editing.”[2](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn2) In her notes she can write down (and reread, edit, and riff off) any snippet of lyrics or melodic hook that flickers through her mind. She can take her notes everywhere, access them from anywhere, and send them within seconds to a wide network of producers and collaborators using the same device. Any feedback they send back can go right into her notes as well.  
在她的职业生涯中, 斯威夫特发行了五部纪录片, 展示了她创作歌曲的过程。在所有这些照片中, 都可以看到她把头埋在手机里。正如她所说:“我消失在我的手机里, 因为我的手机是我保存笔记的地方, 我的手机是我编辑的地方。” [2](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn2) 在她的笔记中, 她可以写下 (并重读、编辑和重复) 她脑海中闪过的任何歌词片段或旋律钩。她可以在任何地方记录笔记, 从任何地方访问它们, 并在几秒钟内将它们发送给使用同一设备的广泛的制作人和合作者网络。他们发回的任何反馈也可以直接记入她的笔记中。

In an interview about how she wrote the smash hit “Blank Space,”[3](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn3) Swift says, “I’ll be going about my daily life and I’ll think, ‘Wow, so we only have two real options in relationships—it’s going to be forever or it’s going to go down in flames,’ so I’ll jot that down in my notes... I’ll come up with a line that I think is clever like ‘Darling I’m a nightmare dressed like a daydream’ and I just pick them and put them where they fit and construct the bridge out of more lines that come up within the last couple of years... ‘Blank Space’ was the culmination of all my best ones one after the other.”  
在一次关于她如何写出红极一时的 “空白” 的采访中, [3](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn3) Swift 说,“我将开始我的日常生活, 我会想,‘哇, 所以我们在关系中只有两个真正的选择——它将永远存在, 否则它将在火焰中消失,” 所以我会把它记在笔记中...... 我会想出一句我认为很聪明的台词, 比如 “亲爱的, 我是一个穿着像 daydream', 我只是挑选它们并将它们放在合适的位置, 然后用过去几年中出现的更多线条构建桥梁......'Blank Space' 是我所有最好的一个接一个的高潮。

For Swift, writing songs is not a discrete activity that she can do only at certain times and in certain places. It is a side effect of the way her mind works, spinning off new metaphors and turns of phrase at the most unexpected times: “I’m inspired to write songs at any time of day, when I’m going through something or when the dust has settled and I’m over it. It can be anything. I’ll just be doing dishes or something, or in the middle of an interview, and I could get an idea in my head that just kind of sticks out as, ‘That could be a hook, that could be a pre-chorus, that’s a first line.’” She goes on to explain why it’s so important to her to capture those fleeting thoughts right as they appear: “I kind of have to capitalize on the excitement of me getting that idea and see it all the way through or else I’ll leave it behind and assume it wasn’t good enough.”  
对于斯威夫特来说, 写歌并不是她只能在特定时间和特定地点进行的一项离散活动。这是她思维方式的副作用, 在最意想不到的时候衍生出新的隐喻和措辞:“我在一天中的任何时候都会受到启发去写歌, 当我正在经历某事或尘埃落定, 我已经结束了。它可以是任何东西。我只是在洗碗什么的, 或者在接受采访的过程中, 我脑子里可能会冒出一个想法,‘这可能是一个钩子, 可能是一个预合唱, 这是第一句话。'” 她继续解释为什么在这些转瞬即逝的想法出现时立即捕捉对她来说如此重要:“我有点必须利用我得到那个想法的兴奋, 并一直看到它否则我会把它抛在脑后, 并认为它不够好。”

Even after all her success, even Taylor Swift needs a system to carry her ideas from inception all the way to completion. By integrating her notetaking with her daily life, she’s able to use language and analogies that are rooted in everyday feelings and experiences, forging a powerful connection with her fans who call themselves “Swifties.” Listening to her albums is like following Swift on a journey of self-discovery, each album chronicling what she was experiencing and who she was becoming in each chapter of her life.  
即使在她取得成功之后, 即使是 Taylor Swift 也需要一个系统来将她的想法从开始一直传递到完成。通过将记笔记与日常生活相结合, 她能够使用植根于日常感受和经历的语言和 类比, 与自称为 “Swifties” 的粉丝建立了强有力的联系。听她的专辑就像跟随斯威夫特踏上自我发现的旅程, 每张专辑都记录了她在人生的每一章中所经历的事情以及她正在成为什么样的人。

This story sheds light on how even the world’s most successful and prolific creatives need support systems to pursue their craft. It’s not a matter of having enough raw talent. Talent needs to be channeled and developed in order to become something more than a momentary spark. Actor and comedian Jerry Seinfeld, arguably the most influential comedian of his generation, wrote in his book _Is This Anything?_:  
这个故事阐明了即使是世界上最成功和最多产的创意人员也需要支持系统来追求他们的技艺。这不是拥有足够的原始人才的问题。人才需要得到引导和发展, 才能成为超越瞬间火花的东西。演员兼喜剧演员杰里 · 宋飞, 可以说是他那一代最有影响力的喜剧演员, 在他的书 “这是什么?” 中写道:

> _Whenever I came up with a funny bit, whether it happened on a stage, in a conversation or working it out on my preferred canvas, the big yellow legal pad, I kept it in one of those old-school accordion folders... A lot of people I’ve talked to seemed surprised that I’ve kept all these notes. I don’t understand why they think that. I don’t understand why I’ve kept anything else. What could possibly be of more value?  
> 每当我想出一个有趣的片段, 无论是发生在舞台上、谈话中还是在我喜欢的画布上, 黄色的大便笺簿上, 我都会把它放在那些老式的手风琴文件夹中...... 很多与我交谈过的人似乎对我保留了所有这些笔记感到惊讶。我不明白他们为什么这么想。我不明白为什么我保留了其他东西。什么可能更有价值?_

Think about your favorite athlete, musician, or actor. Behind the scenes of their public persona, there is a process they follow for regularly turning new ideas into creative output. The same goes for inventors, engineers, and effective leaders. Innovation and impact don’t happen by accident or chance. Creativity depends on a _creative process_.  
想想你最喜欢的运动员、音乐家或演员。在他们公众形象的背后, 有一个他们遵循的定期将新想法转化为创意输出的过程。这同样适用于发明家、工程师和有效的领导者。创新和影响不会偶然发生。创造力取决于创造过程。

### Creating a Knowledge Bank: How to Generate Compounding Interest from Your Thoughts  
创建一个知识库: 如何从你的想法中产生复利

In Chapter 2, we looked at the history of commonplace books, kept by intellectuals and writers in previous centuries. For them, the purpose of information was clear: to inform their writing, speaking, and conversation. Knowing how they were going to be putting ideas to use gave them a powerful lens for seeing which ones were worth the trouble of writing down.  
在第 2 章中, 我们回顾了前几个世纪知识分子和作家收藏的普通书籍的历史。对他们来说, 信息的目的很明确: 为他们的写作、口语和对话提供信息。知道他们将如何将想法付诸实践, 这给了他们一个强大的镜头, 让他们看到哪些想法值得费心写下来。

This practice continues among creatives today. Songwriters are known for compiling “hook books” full of lyrics and musical riffs they may want to use in future songs. Software engineers build “code libraries” so useful bits of code are easy to access. Lawyers keep “case files” with details from past cases they might want to refer to in the future. Marketers and advertisers maintain “swipe files” with examples of compelling ads they might want to draw from.  
今天, 这种做法在创意人员中仍在继续。词曲作者以编写充满歌词和音乐重复段的 “hook books” 而闻名, 他们可能想在未来的歌曲中使用。软件工程师构建“代码库”, 以便轻松访问有用的代码。律师保留“案件档案”, 其中包含他们将来可能希望参考的过去案件的详细信息。营销人员和广告商维护“刷卡文件”, 其中包含他们可能想从中提取的引人注目的广告示例。

The challenge for the rest of us is how to apply this same lens to the work we do every day. What kinds of information are worth preserving when we don’t know exactly how we’ll be putting it to use? Our world changes much faster than in previous eras, and most of us don’t have a single creative medium we work in. How can we decide what to save when we have no idea what the future holds?  
我们其他人面临的挑战是如何将同样的镜头应用到我们每天所做的工作中。当我们不确切知道我们将如何使用它们时, 什么样的信息值得保留? 我们的世界变化比以前的时代快得多, 我们大多数人都没有单一的创意媒介来工作。当我们不知道未来会怎样时, 我们如何决定保存什么?

To answer that question, we have to radically expand our definition of “knowledge.”  
要回答这个问题, 我们必须从根本上扩展我们对 “知识” 的定义。

Knowledge isn’t just wise quotations from long-dead Greek philosophers in white togas. It’s not just the teachings found within thick textbooks written by academics with advanced degrees. In the digital world we live in, knowledge most often shows up as “content”—snippets of text, screenshots, bookmarked articles, podcasts, or other kinds of media. This includes the content you gather from outside sources but also the content you create as you compose emails, draw up project plans, brainstorm ideas, and journal your own thoughts.  
知识不仅仅是身穿白色长袍的已故希腊哲学家的睿智名言。这不仅仅是由拥有高级学位的学者编写的厚厚教科书中的教义。在我们生活的数字世界中, 知识通常以 “内容” 的形式出现——文本片段、屏幕截图、带书签的文章、 播客或其他类型的媒体。这包括您从外部来源收集的内容, 也包括您在撰写电子邮件、制定项目计划、集思广益和记录自己的想法时创建的内容。

These aren’t just random artifacts with no value—they are “knowledge assets” that crystallize what you know in concrete form.[I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-028)  
这些不仅仅是没有价值的随机人工制品——它们是 “知识资产”, 将你所知道的具体形式具体化。 [I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-028)

Knowledge isn’t always something “out there” that you have to go out and find. It’s everywhere, all around you: buried in the emails in your inbox, hidden within files in your documents folder, and waiting on cloud drives. Knowledge capture is about mining the richness of the reading you’re already doing and the life you’re already living.  
知识并不总是你必须出去寻找的 “外面” 的东西。它无处不在, 就在您身边: 隐藏在收件箱的电子邮件中, 隐藏在文档文件夹的文件中, 并等待在云驱动器上。知识获取是关于挖掘你已经阅读的内容和你已经过的生活的丰富性。

Sometimes these assets are quite mundane—the agenda from last year’s financial planning off-site repurposed for next year’s meeting. At other times that knowledge is lofty and grand—your in-depth notes from a book on history that could change how you think about the world. Or anything in between. A knowledge asset is anything that can be used in the future to solve a problem, save time, illuminate a concept, or learn from past experience.  
有时这些资产非常平凡——去年场外财务规划的议程被重新用于明年的会议。在其他时候, 知识是崇高而宏伟的——你从一本历史书中做的深入笔记可能会改变你对世界的看法。或者介于两者之间的任何东西。知识资产是未来可以用来解决问题、节省时间、阐明概念或从过去的经验中学习的任何东西。

Knowledge assets can come from either the external world or your inner thoughts. External knowledge could include:  
知识资产可以来自外部世界, 也可以来自您的内心想法。外部知识可能包括:

*   **Highlights:** Insightful passages from books or articles you read.  
    亮点: 您阅读的书籍或文章中有见地的段落。
*   **Quotes:** Memorable passages from podcasts or audiobooks you listen to.  
    语录: 您收听的播客或有声读物中令人难忘的段落。
*   **Bookmarks and favorites:** Links to interesting content you find on the web or favorited social media posts.  
    书签和收藏夹: 指向您在网络上找到的有趣内容或收藏的社交媒体帖子的链接。
*   **Voice memos:** Clips recorded on your mobile device as “notes to self.”  
    语音备忘录: 在您的移动设备上记录为 “自我笔记” 的剪辑。
*   **Meeting notes:** Notes you take about what was discussed during meetings or phone calls.  
    会议记录: 您对会议或电话中讨论的内容所做的记录。
*   **Images:** Photos or other images that you find inspiring or interesting.  
    图片: 您觉得鼓舞人心或有趣的照片或其他图片。
*   **Takeaways:** Lessons from courses, conferences, or presentations you’ve attended.  
    要点: 您参加过的课程、会议或演讲的经验教训。

Look around you and notice that you already have many of these. It may be disorganized, spread around in different places, and saved in different formats, but it’s there. Just notice that you’ve already spent the effort to create or acquire it. All you need to do is gather it up and plant it as the first seeds in your knowledge garden. Soon I’ll show you how to do that.  
环顾四周, 你会发现你已经拥有了很多这样的东西。它可能杂乱无章, 散布在不同的地方, 并以不同的格式保存, 但它就在那里。请注意, 您已经付出了努力来创建或获得它。您需要做的就是收集它并将其作为知识花园中的第一颗种子播种。很快我会告诉你如何做到这一点。

As you start collecting this material from the outer world, it often sparks new ideas and realizations in your inner world. You can capture those thoughts too! They could include:  
当您开始从外部世界收集这些材料时, 它通常会在您的内心世界中激发出新的想法和认识。您也可以捕捉到这些想法! 它们可能包括:

*   **Stories:** Your favorite anecdotes, whether they happened to you or someone else.  
    故事: 您最喜欢的轶事, 无论是发生在您身上还是发生在其他人身上。
*   **Insights:** The small (and big) realizations you have.  
    见解: 您拥有的小 (和大) 实现。
*   **Memories:** Experiences from your life that you don’t want to forget.  
    回忆: 你不想忘记的生活经历。
*   **Reflections:** Personal thoughts and lessons written in a journal or diary.  
    反思: 写在日记或日记中的个人想法和教训。
*   **Musings:** Random “shower ideas” that pop into your head.  
    沉思: 突然出现在您脑海中的随机 “淋浴想法”。

The meaning of a thought, insight, or memory often isn’t immediately clear. We need to write them down, revisit them, and view them from a different perspective in order to digest what they mean to us. It is exceedingly difficult to do that within the confines of our heads. We need an external medium in which to see our ideas from another vantage point, and writing things down is the most effective and convenient one ever invented.  
思想、洞察力或记忆的含义通常不会立即清晰。我们需要将它们写下来, 重新审视它们, 并从不同的角度来看待它们, 以便消化它们对我们的意义。在我们的头脑范围内做到这一点非常困难。我们需要一种外部媒介来从另一个有利的角度看待我们的想法, 而把事情写下来是有史以来最有效、最方便的发明。

Perhaps you have some hesitation about writing down such personal thoughts in a piece of software rather than a private journal. While it’s always up to you what you choose to note down, remember that your Second Brain is private too. You can share certain notes if you want to, but by default everything inside is for your eyes only.  
也许您对在软件而不是私人日记中写下这样的个人想法有些犹豫。虽然选择记下什么始终取决于您, 但请记住, 您的第二大脑也是私人的。如果你愿意, 你可以分享某些笔记, 但默认情况下, 里面的所有内容都只供你查看。

For now, choose the two to three kinds of content from the two lists above that you already have the most of and already value. Some people favor inner sources of knowledge, some people are biased toward the outer world, but most people are somewhere in between. While you can eventually learn to capture from dozens of different sources, it’s important to start small and get your feet wet before diving into the deep end.  
现在, 从上面的两个列表中选择您已经拥有最多并且已经看重的两到三种内容。有些人偏爱内在的知识来源, 有些人偏向于外部世界, 但大多数人介于两者之间。虽然您最终可以学会从数十种不同的来源进行捕捉, 但重要的是从小处着手, 在深入研究之前先尝试一下。

### What _Not_ to Keep 什么不应该保留

The examples I’ve shared may seem so expansive that you’re wondering if there is anything you _shouldn’t_ keep in your Second Brain. In my experience, there are four kinds of content that aren’t well suited to notes apps:  
我分享的例子可能看起来太广泛了, 以至于你想知道是否有什么东西不应该放在你的第二大脑中。根据我的经验, 有四种内容不太适合笔记应用程序:

*   **Is this sensitive information you’d like to keep secure?** The content you save in your notes is easily accessible from any device, which is great for accessibility but not for security. Information like tax records, government documents, passwords, and health records shouldn’t be saved in your notes.  
    您是否希望确保这些敏感信息的安全? 您保存在笔记中的内容可以从任何设备轻松访问, 这非常适合可访问性, 但不利于安全性。 税务记录、政府文件、密码和健康记录等信息不应保存在您的笔记中。
*   **Is this a special format or file type better handled by a dedicated app?** Although you could save specialized files such as Photoshop files or video footage in your notes, you’ll need a specialized app to open them anyway, so there’s no benefit to keeping them in your notes.  
    这是专用应用程序更好地处理的特殊格式或文件类型吗? 尽管您可以在笔记中保存 Photoshop 文件或视频片段等专用文件, 但无论如何您都需要专门的应用程序才能打开它们, 因此将它们保存在笔记中没有任何好处。
*   **Is this a very large file?** Notes apps are made for short, lightweight bits of text and images, and their performance will often be severely hampered if you try to save large files in them.  
    这是一个非常大的文件吗? Notes 应用程序专为简短、轻量级的文本和图像而设计, 如果您尝试在其中保存大文件, 它们的性能通常会受到严重影响。
*   **Will it need to be collaboratively edited?** Notes apps are perfectly suited for individual, private use, which makes them less than ideal for collaboration. You can share individual notes or even groups of notes with others, but if you need multiple people to be able to collaboratively edit a document in real time, then you’ll need to use a different platform.  
    是否需要协作编辑? Notes 应用程序非常适合个人、私人使用, 这使得它们不太适合协作。您可以与他人共享单个笔记甚至笔记组, 但如果您需要多人实时协作编辑文档, 则需要使用不同的平台。

### Twelve Favorite Problems: A Nobel Prize Winner’s Approach to Capturing  
十二个最喜欢的问题: 一位诺贝尔奖得主的捕捉方法

With the abundance of content all around us, it can be hard to know exactly what is worth preserving. I use an insightful exercise to help people make this decision easier. I call it “Twelve Favorite Problems,” inspired by Nobel Prize–winning physicist Richard Feynman.  
随着我们周围内容的丰富, 可能很难确切知道哪些内容值得保留。我使用一个有见地的练习来帮助人们更容易做出这个决定。我将其称为 “十二个最喜欢的问题”, 其灵感来自诺贝尔物理学奖得主理查德 · 费曼。

Feynman was known for his wide-ranging, eclectic tastes. As a child he already showed a talent for engineering, once building a functioning home alarm system out of spare parts while his parents were out running errands. During his colorful lifetime Feynman spent time in Brazil teaching physics, learned to play the bongo and the conga drums well enough to perform with orchestras, and enthusiastically traveled around the world exploring other cultures.  
费曼以其广泛、兼收并蓄的品味而闻名。孩提时代, 他就表现出了工程学方面的天赋, 有一次在父母外出办事时, 他用备件组装了一个功能正常的家庭警报系统。在他丰富多彩的一生中, 费曼曾在巴西教物理, 学会了演奏邦戈鼓和康茄鼓以与管弦乐队一起演奏, 并热情地周游世界探索其他文化。

Of course, Feynman is best known for his groundbreaking discoveries in theoretical physics and quantum mechanics, for which he received the Nobel Prize in 1965. In his spare time, he also played a pivotal role on the commission that investigated the _Challenger_ space shuttle disaster and published half a dozen books.  
当然, 费曼最著名的是他在理论物理和量子力学方面的开创性发现, 并因此获得了 1965 年的诺贝尔奖。在业余时间, 他还在调查挑战者空间的委员会中发挥了关键作用穿梭灾难并出版了六本书。

How could one person make so many contributions across so many areas? How did he have the time to lead such a full and interesting life while also becoming one of the most recognized scientists of his generation?  
一个人怎么能在这么多领域做出这么多贡献? 他怎么会有时间过着如此充实而有趣的生活, 同时还成为他那一代最受认可的科学家之一?

Feynman revealed his strategy in an interview[4](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn4):  
费曼在 [4](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn4) 的采访中透露了他的策略:

> _You have to keep a dozen of your favorite problems constantly present in your mind, although by and large they will lay in a dormant state. Every time you hear or read a new trick or a new result, test it against each of your twelve problems to see whether it helps. Every once in a while there will be a hit, and people will say, “How did he do it? He must be a genius!”  
> 你必须让一打你最喜欢的问题不断出现在你的脑海中, 尽管它们大体上处于休眠状态。每当你听到或读到一个新技巧或一个新结果时, 针对你的十二个问题中的每一个进行测试, 看看它是否有帮助。每隔一段时间就会有一个打击, 人们会说,“他是怎么做到的? 他一定是个天才!”_

In other words, Feynman’s approach was to maintain a list of a dozen open questions. When a new scientific finding came out, he would test it against each of his questions to see if it shed any new light on the problem. This cross-disciplinary approach allowed him to make connections across seemingly unrelated subjects, while continuing to follow his sense of curiosity.  
换句话说, 费曼的方法是维护一个包含十几个未解决问题的列表。当一项新的科学发现出现时, 他会针对他的每一个问题进行测试, 看它是否能为问题提供新的线索。这种跨学科的方法使他能够在看似无关的主题之间建立联系, 同时继续追随他的好奇心。

As told in _Genius: The Life and Science of Richard Feynman_ by James Gleick,[5](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn5) Feynman once took inspiration for his physics from an accident at dinner:  
正如 James Gleick 在 Genius:Richard Feynman 的生活和科学中所述, [5](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn5) Feynman 曾从一次晚餐事故中获得物理学灵感:

> _... he was eating in the student cafeteria when someone tossed a dinner plate into the air—a Cornell cafeteria plate with the_ _university seal imprinted on one rim—and in the instant of its flight he experienced what he long afterward considered an epiphany. As the plate spun, it wobbled. Because of the insignia he could see that the spin and the wobble were not quite in synchrony. Yet just in that instant it seemed to him—or was it his physicist’s intuition?—that the two rotations were related._  
> ...... 他在学生食堂吃饭时有人向空中扔了一个餐盘——一个边缘印有 大学印章的康奈尔食堂盘子——在它飞起来的那一刻, 他经历了他很久以后认为的顿悟. 当盘子旋转时, 它摇摆不定。由于徽章, 他可以看出自旋和摆动并不完全同步。然而就在那一刻, 他似乎——或者这是他的物理学家的直觉?——这两个旋转是相关的。

After working the problem out on paper, Feynman discovered a 2-to-1 ratio between the plate’s wobble and spin, a neat relationship that suggested a deeper underlying principle at work.  
在纸上解决了这个问题后, 费曼发现了板块摆动和旋转之间的 2 比 1 的比例, 这种巧妙的关系暗示了更深层的基本原理在起作用。

When a fellow physicist and mentor asked what the use of such an insight was, Feynman responded: “It doesn’t have any importance... I don’t care whether a thing has importance. Isn’t it fun?” He was following his intuition and curiosity. But it did end up having importance, with his research into the equations underlying rotation informing the work that ultimately led to him receiving the Nobel Prize.  
当一位物理学家和导师询问这种洞察力有什么用时, 费曼回答说:“它没有任何重要性...... 我不在乎某件事是否重要。是不是很好玩?” 他在追随自己的直觉和好奇心。但它最终变得很重要, 他对旋转方程的研究为最终导致他获得诺贝尔奖的工作提供了信息。

Feynman’s approach encouraged him to follow his interests wherever they might lead. He posed questions and constantly scanned for solutions to long-standing problems in his reading, conversations, and everyday life. When he found one, he could make a connection that looked to others like a flash of unparalleled brilliance.  
费曼的方法鼓励他随心所欲地追随自己的兴趣。他提出问题并不断审视阅读、谈话和日常生活中长期存在的问题的解决方案。当他找到一个时, 他可以建立一种联系, 在其他人看来就像一道无与伦比的光辉。

Ask yourself, “What are the questions I’ve always been interested in?” This could include grand, sweeping questions like “How can we make society fairer and more equitable?” as well as practical ones like “How can I make it a habit to exercise every day?” It might include questions about relationships, such as “How can I have closer relationships with the people I love?” or productivity, like “How can I spend more of my time doing high-value work?”  
问问自己,“我一直感兴趣的问题是什么?”这可能包括诸如 “我们如何才能使社会更公平、更公平?” 之类的宏大而广泛的问题。以及诸如 “我怎样才能养成每天锻炼的习惯?” 之类的实用问题。它可能包括有关人际关系的问题, 例如 “我怎样才能与我爱的人建立更亲密的关系?” 或生产力, 例如“我怎样才能花更多的时间做高价值的工作?”

Here are more examples of favorite problems from my students:  
以下是我的学生最喜欢的问题的更多示例:

*   How do I live less in the past, and more in the present?  
    如何少活过去, 多活现在?
*   How do I build an investment strategy that is aligned with my mid-term and long-term goals and commitments?  
    我如何制定符合我的中长期目标和承诺的投资策略?
*   What does it look like to move from mindless consumption to mindful creation?  
    从无意识的消费转向有意识的创造是什么样的?
*   How can I go to bed early instead of watching shows after the kids go to bed?  
    孩子睡了以后怎么才能早点睡而不是看节目呢?
*   How can my industry become more ecologically sustainable while remaining profitable?  
    我的行业如何在保持盈利的同时变得更具生态可持续性?
*   How can I work through the fear I have of taking on more responsibility?  
    我怎样才能克服对承担更多责任的恐惧?
*   How can my school provide more resources for students with special needs?  
    我的学校如何为有特殊需要的学生提供更多资源?
*   How do I start reading all the books I already have instead of buying more?  
    我如何开始阅读我已有的所有书籍而不是购买更多书籍?
*   How can I speed up and relax at the same time?  
    我怎样才能同时加速和放松?
*   How can we make the healthcare system more responsive to people’s needs?  
    我们如何才能使医疗保健系统更好地响应人们的需求?
*   What can I do to make eating healthy easier?  
    我可以做些什么来让健康饮食变得更容易?
*   How can I make decisions with more confidence?  
    我怎样才能更有信心地做出决定?

Notice that some of these questions are abstract, while others are concrete. Some express deep longings, while others are more like spontaneous interests. Many are questions about how to live a better life, while a few are focused on how to succeed professionally. The key to this exercise is to make them _open-ended_ questions that don’t necessarily have a single answer. To find questions that invoke a state of wonder and curiosity about the amazing world we live in.  
请注意, 其中一些问题是抽象的, 而另一些则是具体的。有些表达了深深的渴望, 而另一些则更像是自发的兴趣。许多问题是关于如何过上更好的生活, 而一些问题则集中在如何在职业上取得成功。这个练习的关键是让他们成为开放式的问题, 这些问题不一定只有一个答案。寻找能够唤起对我们生活的神奇世界的惊奇和好奇状态的问题。

The power of your favorite problems is that they tend to stay fairly consistent over time. The exact framing of each question may change, but even as we move between projects, jobs, relationships, and careers, our favorite problems tend to follow us across the years. I recommend asking your family or childhood friends what you were obsessed with as a kid. Those very same interests probably still fire your imagination as an adult. Which means any content you collect related to them will likely be relevant far into the future as well.  
你最喜欢的问题的力量在于它们往往会随着时间的推移保持相当一致。每个问题的确切框架可能会改变, 但即使我们在项目、工作、人际关系和职业之间移动, 我们最喜欢的问题往往会多年来一直跟随我们。我建议问问你的家人或儿时的朋友, 你小时候对什么很着迷。这些完全相同的兴趣可能仍然会激发您成年后的想象力。这意味着您收集的与他们相关的任何内容也可能在遥远的未来也相关。

As a kid, I had a passion for LEGOs, the modular toy blocks beloved by generations of children. My parents noticed that I didn’t play with LEGOs like other kids. Instead I spent my time organizing and reorganizing the pieces. I remember being completely captivated by the problem of how to create order out of the chaos of thousands of pieces of every shape and size. I would invent new organizational schemes—by color, by size, by theme—as I became obsessed with the idea that if I could just find the right system, I would finally be able to build my magnum opus—a LEGO spaceship like the ones I saw in the sci-fi movies I loved.  
小时候, 我对乐高积木情有独钟, 这种模块化积木深受几代孩子的喜爱。我的父母注意到我不像其他孩子那样玩乐高积木。相反, 我花时间组织和重新组织这些作品。我记得当时完全被如何从成千上万的各种形状和大小的碎片中创造秩序的问题所吸引。我会发明新的组织方案——按颜色、按大小、按主题——因为我开始痴迷于这样一个想法, 即如果我能找到合适的系统, 我最终就能建造我的巨著——像那些一样的乐高宇宙飞船我在我喜欢的科幻电影中看过。

That very same question—How can creativity emerge out of chaos?—still drives me to this day. Only now, it’s in the form of organizing digital information instead of LEGOs. Pursuing this question has taught me so many things over the years, across many seasons of my life. The goal isn’t to definitively answer the question once and for all, but to use the question as a North Star for my learning.  
同一个问题——创造力如何从混乱中涌现出来?——直到今天仍然驱使着我。只是现在, 它是以组织数字信息而不是乐高积木的形式出现的。多年来, 在我生命的许多季节中, 追寻这个问题教会了我很多东西。目标不是一劳永逸地明确回答问题, 而是将问题作为我学习的北极星。

Take a moment now to write down some of your own favorite problems. Here are my recommendations to guide you:  
现在花点时间写下您自己最喜欢的一些问题。以下是我的建议来指导您:

*   Ask people close to you what you were obsessed with as a child (often you’ll continue to be fascinated with the same things as an adult).  
    询问您身边的人您小时候对什么着迷 (通常您会像成年人一样继续对同样的事物着迷)。
*   Don’t worry about coming up with exactly twelve (the exact number doesn’t matter, but try to come up with at least a few).  
    不要担心是否恰好有 12 个 (确切的数字并不重要, 但尽量想出至少几个)。
*   Don’t worry about getting the list perfect (this is just a first pass, and it will always be evolving).  
    不要担心如何让列表变得完美 (这只是第一步, 它会一直在发展)。
*   Phrase them as open-ended questions that could have multiple answers (in contrast to “yes/no” questions with only one answer).  
    将它们表述为可以有多个答案的开放式问题 (与只有一个答案的“是 / 否” 问题相反)。

Use your list of favorite problems to make decisions about what to capture: anything potentially relevant to answering them. Use one of the capture tools I recommend later in this chapter, or in the Second Brain Resource Guide at [Buildingasecondbrain.com/resources](http://www.buildingasecondbrain.com/resources).  
使用您最喜欢的问题列表来决定要捕获的内容: 与回答这些问题可能相关的任何内容。使用我在本章后面或在 Buildingasecondbrain.com/resources 上的第二脑资源指南中推荐的捕获工具之一。

### Capture Criteria: How to Avoid Keeping Too Much (or Too Little)  
捕获标准: 如何避免保存过多 (或过少)

Once you have identified the kinds of questions you want your Second Brain to answer, it’s time to choose specifically which pieces of information will be most useful.  
一旦你确定了你希望第二大脑回答的问题类型, 就该具体选择哪些信息最有用了。

Imagine you come across a blog post during your web browsing that details how a marketing expert you respect runs her campaigns. You’re hooked: this is the kind of material you’ve been looking for! Finally, the master reveals her secrets!  
想象一下, 您在浏览网页时看到一篇博文, 其中详细介绍了您尊敬的营销专家如何开展营销活动。你上瘾了: 这就是你一直在寻找的那种材料! 终于, 大师揭开了她的秘密!

Your first instinct might be to save the article in its entirety. It’s high-quality information, so why not preserve all of it? The problem is, it’s an in-depth how-to article that is thousands of words long. Even if you spend the twenty or thirty minutes it would take to consume it now, in the future you’ll just have to spend all that time reading it again, since you’ll have forgotten most of the details. You also don’t want to just bookmark the link and save it to read later, because then you won’t know what it contains in the first place!  
您的第一直觉可能是完整保存文章。这是高质量的信息, 为什么不保留所有信息呢? 问题是, 这是一篇长达数千字的深度指导文章。即使你现在花 20 或 30 分钟来读完它, 将来你也只需要花所有的时间再读一遍, 因为你会忘记大部分细节。您也不想只为链接添加书签并保存它以供日后阅读, 因为那样您一开始就不知道它包含什么!

This is where most people get stuck. They either dive straight into the first piece of content they see, read it voraciously, but quickly forget all the details, or they open dozens of tabs in their web browser and feel a pang of guilt at all those interesting resources they haven’t been able to get to.  
这是大多数人卡住的地方。他们要么直接进入他们看到的第一段内容, 如饥似渴地阅读, 但很快就忘记了所有细节, 要么他们在网络浏览器中打开了几十个选项卡, 并对所有这些他们没有接触过的有趣资源感到内疚能够到达。

There is a way out of this situation. It starts with realizing that in any piece of content, _the value is not evenly distributed_. There are always certain parts that are especially interesting, helpful, or valuable to you. When you realize this, the answer is obvious. You can extract only the most salient, relevant, rich material and save it as a succinct note.  
有办法摆脱这种情况。首先要意识到在任何内容中, 价值都不是均匀分布的。总会有某些部分对您特别有趣、有帮助或有价值。当你意识到这一点时, 答案就很明显了。您可以仅提取最突出、最相关、最丰富的材料并将其保存为简洁的注释。

Don’t save entire chapters of a book—save only select passages. Don’t save complete transcripts of interviews—save a few of the best quotes. Don’t save entire websites—save a few screenshots of the sections that are most interesting. The best curators are picky about what they allow into their collections, and you should be too. With a notes app, you can always save links back to the original content if you need to review your sources or want to dive deeper into the details in the future.  
不要保存一本书的整个章节——只保存选定的段落。不要保存完整的访谈记录——保存一些最好的引述。不要保存整个网站——保存一些最有趣的部分的屏幕截图。最好的策展人对他们允许收藏的东西很挑剔, 你也应该如此。使用笔记应用程序, 如果您需要查看来源或希望在未来更深入地了解细节, 您可以随时将链接保存回原始内容。

The biggest pitfall I see people falling into once they begin capturing digital notes is saving too much. If you try to save every piece of material you come across, you run the risk of inundating your future self with tons of irrelevant information. At that point, your Second Brain will be no better than scrolling through social media.  
我看到人们一旦开始记录数字笔记就会陷入的最大陷阱是节省太多。如果你试图保存你遇到的每一块材料, 你就有可能让未来的自己被大量无关信息淹没。到那时, 您的第二大脑将不会比滚动浏览社交媒体更好。

This is why it’s so important to take on a Curator’s Perspective—that we are the judges, editors, and interpreters of the information we choose to let into our lives. Thinking like a curator means taking charge of your own information stream, instead of just letting it wash over you. The more economical you can be with the material you capture in the first place, the less time and effort your future self will have to spend organizing, distilling, and expressing it.[II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-027)  
这就是为什么采用策展人的视角如此重要——我们是我们选择让进入我们生活的信息的法官、编辑和解释者。像策展人一样思考意味着掌控自己的信息流, 而不是让它冲刷你。最初捕获的材料越经济, 未来的自己花在组织、提炼和表达上的时间和精力就越少。 [II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-027)

Here are four criteria I suggest to help you decide exactly which nuggets of knowledge are worth keeping:  
以下是我建议的四个标准, 可帮助您准确决定哪些知识块值得保留:

#### Capture Criteria #1: Does It Inspire Me?  
捕获标准 #1: 它能激发我的灵感吗?

Inspiration is one of the most rare and precious experiences in life. It is the essential fuel for doing your best work, yet it’s impossible to call up inspiration on demand. You can Google the answer to a question, but you can’t Google a feeling.  
灵感是人生中最难得、最宝贵的经历之一。它是完成最佳工作的基本燃料, 但不可能按需唤起灵感。你可以谷歌问题的答案, 但你不能谷歌一种感觉。

There is a way to evoke a sense of inspiration more regularly: keep a collection of inspiring quotes, photos, ideas, and stories. Any time you need a break, a new perspective, or a dash of motivation, you can look through it and see what sparks your imagination.  
有一种方法可以更经常地唤起灵感: 收集鼓舞人心的名言、照片、想法和故事。任何时候您需要休息、新视角或一点点动力时, 您都可以浏览它, 看看是什么激发了您的想象力。

For example, I keep a folder full of customer testimonials I’ve received over the years. Any time I think what I’m doing doesn’t matter or isn’t good enough, all I have to do is open up that folder and my perspective is completely shifted.  
例如, 我保留了一个文件夹, 里面装满了我多年来收到的客户评价。任何时候我认为我在做什么并不重要或不够好, 我所要做的就是打开那个文件夹, 我的观点就会完全改变。

#### Capture Criteria #2: Is It Useful?  
捕获标准 #2: 有用吗?

Carpenters are known for keeping odds and ends in a corner of their workshop—a variety of nails and washers, scraps of lumber cut off from larger planks, and random bits of metal and wood. It costs nothing to keep these “offcuts” around, and surprisingly often they end up being the crucial missing piece in a future project.  
众所周知, 木匠会在车间的一个角落里存放杂物——各种钉子和垫圈、从大木板上切下的木材碎片, 以及随意的金属和木头碎片。保留这些 “边角料” 不需要花费任何成本, 而且令人惊讶的是, 它们往往最终成为未来项目中关键的缺失部分。

Sometimes you come across a piece of information that isn’t necessarily inspiring, but you know it might come in handy in the future. A statistic, a reference, a research finding, or a helpful diagram—these are the equivalents of the spare parts a carpenter might keep around their workshop.  
有时您会遇到一些不一定鼓舞人心的信息, 但您知道它将来可能会派上用场。统计数据、参考资料、研究发现或有用的图表——这些等同于木匠可能在车间周围放置的备件。

For example, I keep a folder full of stock photos, graphics, and drawings I find both online and offline. Any time I need an image for a slide deck, or a web page, or to spark new ideas, I have a plentiful supply of imagery I’ve already found compelling ready and waiting.  
例如, 我保留了一个文件夹, 里面装满了我在网上和线下找到的库存照片、图形和绘图。任何时候我需要用于 幻灯片或网页的图像, 或激发新想法时, 我都有大量图像可供使用, 我已经准备好等待它们了。

#### Capture Criteria #3: Is It Personal?  
捕获标准 #3: 它是个人的吗?

One of the most valuable kinds of information to keep is personal information—your own thoughts, reflections, memories, and mementos. Like the age-old practice of journaling or keeping a diary, we can use notetaking to document our lives and better understand how we became who we are.  
要保留的最有价值的信息之一是个人信息——您自己的想法、想法、记忆和纪念品。就像记录日志或写日记的古老做法一样, 我们可以使用笔记记录我们的生活并更好地了解我们如何成为现在的自己。

No one else has access to the wisdom you’ve personally gained from a lifetime of conversations, mistakes, victories, and lessons learned. No one else values the small moments of your days quite like you do.  
没有其他人能够获得您个人从一生的对话、错误、胜利和经验教训中获得的智慧。没有人像您一样珍视您生活中的每一刻。

I often save screenshots of text messages sent between my family and friends. The small moments of warmth and humor that take place in these threads are precious to me, since I can’t always be with them in person. It takes mere moments, and I love knowing that I’ll forever have memories from my conversations with the people closest to me.  
我经常保存家人和朋友之间发送的短信的屏幕截图。这些线程中发生的温暖和幽默的小时刻对我来说很珍贵, 因为我不能总是和他们在一起。这只需片刻, 我很高兴知道我将永远记住与最亲近的人的谈话。

#### Capture Criteria #4: Is It Surprising?  
捕获标准 #4: 令人惊讶吗?

I’ve often noticed that many of the notes people take are of ideas they already know, already agree with, or could have guessed. We have a natural bias as humans to seek evidence that confirms what we already believe, a well-studied phenomenon known as “confirmation bias.”[6](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn6)  
我经常注意到人们所做的许多笔记都是他们已经知道、已经同意或可能已经猜到的想法。作为人类, 我们有一种天生的偏见, 即寻求证据来证实我们已经相信的事情, 这种经过充分研究的现象被称为 “确认偏见”。 [6](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn6)

That isn’t what a Second Brain is for. The renowned information theorist Claude Shannon, whose discoveries paved the way for modern technology, had a simple definition for “information”: that which surprises you.[7](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn7) If you’re not surprised, then you already knew it at some level, so why take note of it? Surprise is an excellent barometer for information that doesn’t fit neatly into our existing understanding, which means it has the potential to change how we think.  
这不是第二大脑的目的。著名的信息理论家克劳德 · 香农 (Claude Shannon) 的发现为现代技术铺平了道路, 他对 “信息” 有一个简单的定义: 让您感到惊讶的东西。 [7](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn7) 如果你不感到惊讶, 那么你在某种程度上已经知道它 , 那么为什么要注意它呢? 对于与我们现有理解不完全吻合的信息, 惊喜是一个很好的晴雨表, 这意味着它有可能改变我们的思维方式。

Sometimes you come across an idea that is neither inspiring, personal, nor obviously useful, but there is something surprising about it. You may not be able to put your finger on why, but it conflicts with your existing point of view in a way that makes your brain perk up and pay attention. Those are the ideas you should capture.  
有时, 您会遇到一个既不鼓舞人心、既不个人化, 也不明显有用的想法, 但它有一些令人惊讶的地方。你可能无法理解为什么, 但它与你现有的观点相冲突, 会让你的大脑振作起来并集中注意力。这些是你应该捕捉的想法。

Your Second Brain shouldn’t be just another way of confirming what you already know. We are already surrounded by algorithms that feed us only what we already believe and social networks that continually reinforce what we already think.  
你的第二大脑不应该只是确认你已经知道的东西的另一种方式。我们已经被只为我们提供我们已经相信的东西的算法和不断强化我们已经思考的社交网络所包围。

Our ability to capture ideas from anywhere takes us in a different direction: By saving ideas that may contradict each other and don’t necessarily support what we already believe, we can train ourselves to take in information from different sources instead of immediately jumping to conclusions. By playing with ideas—bending and stretching and remixing them—we become less attached to the way they were originally presented and can borrow certain aspects or elements to use in our own work.  
我们从任何地方捕捉想法的能力将我们带向了不同的方向: 通过保存可能相互矛盾且不一定支持我们已经相信的想法, 我们可以训练自己从不同来源获取信息, 而不是立即得出结论. 通过玩弄想法——弯曲、拉伸和重新混合它们——我们不再那么执着于它们最初呈现的方式, 并且可以借用某些方面或元素用于我们自己的工作中。

If what you’re capturing doesn’t change your mind, then what’s the point?  
如果你捕捉到的东西没有改变你的想法, 那又有什么意义呢?

### Ultimately, Capture What Resonates  
最终, 捕捉共鸣

I’ve given you specific criteria to help you decide what is worth capturing, but if you take away one thing from this chapter, it should be to keep what resonates.  
我已经给了你具体的标准来帮助你决定什么是值得捕捉的, 但如果你从本章中拿走一件事, 那就应该是保持共鸣。

Here’s why: making decisions analytically, with a checklist, is taxing and stressful. It is the kind of thinking that demands the most energy. When you use up too much energy taking notes, you have little left over for the subsequent steps that add far more value: making connections, imagining possibilities, formulating theories, and creating new ideas of your own. Not to mention, if you make reading and learning into unpleasant experiences, over time you’re going to find yourself doing less and less of them. The secret to making reading a habit is to make it effortless and enjoyable.  
原因如下: 使用清单进行分析性决策既费力又压力大。这是最需要能量的思维方式。当你用了太多的精力做笔记时, 你就剩下 little 用于后续步骤, 这些步骤会增加更多的价值: 建立联系、想象可能性、形成理论和创造你自己的新想法。更不用说, 如果你把阅读和学习变成不愉快的经历, 随着时间的推移, 你会发现自己做的事情越来越少。养成阅读习惯的秘诀在于让它轻松愉快。

As you consume a piece of content, listen for an internal feeling of being moved or surprised by the idea you’re taking in. This special feeling of “resonance”—like an echo in your soul—is your intuition telling you that something is literally “noteworthy.” You don’t need to figure out exactly why it resonates. Just look for the signs: your eyes might widen slightly, your heart may skip a beat, your throat may go slightly dry, and your sense of time might subtly slow down as the world around you fades away. These are clues that it’s time to hit “save.”  
当你消费一段内容时, 倾听一种内心的感觉, 即被你正在接受的想法所感动或惊讶。这种特殊的 “共鸣” 感觉——就像你灵魂中的回声——是你的直觉告诉你某事是字面意思是 “值得注意的”。你不需要弄清楚它为什么会引起共鸣。只需寻找迹象: 您的眼睛可能会稍微睁大, 您的心跳可能会停止跳动, 您的喉咙可能会稍微干燥, 随着您周围的世界逐渐消失, 您的时间感可能会微妙地减慢。这些线索表明是时候点击“保存” 了。

We know from neuroscientific research that “emotions organize—rather than disrupt—rational thinking.”[8](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn8) When something resonates with us, it is our emotion-based, intuitive mind telling us it is interesting before our logical mind can explain why. I often find that a piece of content resonates with me in ways I can’t fully explain in the moment, and its true potential only becomes clear later on.  
我们从神经科学研究中得知,“情绪组织——而不是破坏——理性思维。” [8](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn8) 当某事与我们产生共鸣时, 是我们基于情感的直觉告诉我们这很有趣, 然后我们的逻辑思维才能解释原因。我经常发现一段内容以我当时无法完全解释的方式引起我的共鸣, 而它的真正潜力只有在以后才会变得清晰。

There’s scientific evidence that our intuition knows what it’s doing. From the book _Designing for Behavior Change:_[9](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn9)  
有科学证据表明我们的直觉知道它在做什么。摘自《行为改变设计》一书: [9](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn9)

> _Participants in a famous study were given four biased decks of cards—some that would win them money, and some that would cause them to lose. When they started the game, they didn’t know that the decks were biased. As they played the game, though, people’s bodies started showing signs of physical “stress” when their conscious minds were about to use a money-losing deck. The stress was an automatic response that occurred because the_ _intuitive mind realized something was wrong—long before the conscious mind realized anything was amiss._  
> 在一项著名的研究中, 参与者得到了四副有偏见的纸牌——其中一些会让他们赢钱, 而另一些会让他们输钱。当他们开始游戏时, 他们并不知道套牌是有偏见的。然而, 当他们玩这个游戏时, 当他们有意识的头脑即将使用一副亏钱的套牌时, 他们的身体开始表现出身体 “压力” 的迹象。压力是一种自动反应, 因为 intuitive 头脑意识到出了点问题——远在有意识的头脑意识到任何事情不对劲之前。

The authors’ conclusion: “Our intuitive mind learns, and responds, even without our conscious awareness.”  
作者的结论是:“即使没有我们的意识, 我们的直觉思维也会学习和做出反应。”

If you ignore that inner voice of intuition, over time it will slowly quiet down and fade away. If you practice listening to what it is telling you, the inner voice will grow stronger. You’ll start to hear it in all kinds of situations. It will guide you in what choices to make and which opportunities to pursue. It will warn you away from people and situations that aren’t right for you. It will speak up and take a stand for your convictions even when you’re afraid.  
如果你忽视内心直觉的声音, 随着时间的推移, 它会慢慢平静下来并消失。如果你练习倾听它告诉你的内容, 内在的声音就会变得更强。你会开始在各种情况下听到它。它将指导您做出哪些选择以及追求哪些机会。它会警告您远离不适合您的人和情况。即使您害怕, 它也会大声说出来并坚持您的信念。

I can’t think of anything more important for your creative life—and your life in general—than learning to listen to the voice of intuition inside. It is the source of your imagination, your confidence, and your spontaneity. You can intentionally train yourself to hear that voice of intuition every day by taking note of what it tells you.  
我想不出还有什么比学习倾听内心直觉的声音对你的创造性生活——乃至你的生活——更重要的了。它是你想象力、自信和自发性的源泉。你可以通过记下它告诉你的内容, 有意识地训练自己每天听到直觉的声音。

Besides capturing what personally resonates with you, there are a couple other kinds of details that are generally useful to save in your notes. It’s a good idea to capture key information about the source of a note, such as the original web page address, the title of the piece, the author or publisher, and the date it was published.[III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-026) Many capture tools are even able to identify and save this information automatically. Also, it’s often helpful to capture chapter titles, headings, and bullet-point lists, since they add structure to your notes and represent distillation already performed by the author on your behalf.  
除了捕捉你个人的共鸣之外, 还有一些其他类型的细节通常对保存在你的笔记中很有用。捕获有关笔记来源的关键信息是个好主意, 例如原始网页地址、文章标题、作者或出版商以及出版日期。 [III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-026) 很多抓包工具甚至可以自动识别并保存这些信息。此外, 捕获章节标题、标题和要点列表通常很有帮助, 因为它们可以为您的笔记添加结构并代表作者已经代表您进行的提炼。

### Beyond Your Notetaking App: Choosing Capture Tools  
Beyond Your Notetaking App: 选择捕捉工具

Now that you know what kinds of material to save in your Second Brain, it’s time to get into the nitty-gritty: How does capturing work exactly?  
现在您已经知道要在您的第二大脑中保存哪些类型的材料, 是时候深入了解细节了: 捕捉究竟是如何工作的?

Let’s say while reading that in-depth marketing article, you decide a specific piece of advice is highly relevant to your own plans. Most notetaking apps (introduced in Chapter 2 and covered in detail in the Second Brain Resource Guide at [Buildingasecondbrain.com/resources](http://www.buildingasecondbrain.com/resources)) have built-in features that allow you to capture excerpts from outside sources, and you can always simply cut and paste text directly into a new note. There is also an array of more specialized “capture tools” that are designed to make capturing content in digital form easy and even fun.  
假设在阅读那篇深度营销文章时, 您认为某条特定建议与您自己的计划高度相关。大多数笔记应用程序 (在第 2 章中介绍并在 Buildingasecondbrain.com/resources 的第二大脑资源指南中详细介绍) 都具有内置功能, 允许您从外部来源捕捉摘录, 并且您始终可以直接剪切和粘贴文本进入一个新的笔记。还有一系列更专业的“捕获工具”, 旨在使捕获数字形式的内容变得简单甚至有趣。

The most common options include: 最常见的选项包括:

*   Ebook apps, which often allow you to export your highlights or annotations all at once.  
    电子书应用程序, 通常允许您一次导出所有亮点或注释。
*   Read later apps that allow you to bookmark content you find online for later reading (or in the case of podcasts or videos, listening or watching).  
    Read later 应用程序允许您为在线找到的内容添加书签以供以后阅读 (或者在播客或视频的情况下, 收听或观看)。
*   Basic notes apps that often come preinstalled on mobile devices and are designed for easily capturing short snippets of text.  
    基本笔记应用程序通常预装在移动设备上, 旨在轻松捕获简短的文本片段。
*   Social media apps, which usually allow you to “favorite” content and export it to a notes app.  
    社交媒体应用程序, 通常允许您 “收藏” 内容并将其导出到笔记应用程序。
*   Web clippers, which allow you to save parts of web pages (often included as a built-in feature of notes apps).  
    Web 剪辑器, 允许您保存部分网页 (通常作为笔记应用程序的内置功能包含在内)。
*   Audio/voice transcription apps that create text transcripts from spoken words.  
    从口语创建文本转录的音频 / 语音转录应用程序。
*   Other third-party services, integrations, and plug-ins that automate the process of exporting content from one app to another.  
    自动将内容从一个应用程序导出到另一个应用程序的其他第三方服务、集成和插件。

Some of these tools are free, and others charge a small fee. Some are completely automatic, working silently in the background (for example, to automatically sync your ebook highlights with a notes app), while others require a bit of manual effort (such as taking photos of paper notebooks to save them digitally).[IV](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-025) But in any case, the act of capture takes only moments—to hit share, export, or save—and voilà, you’ve preserved the best parts of whatever you’re consuming in your Second Brain.  
其中一些工具是免费的, 而另一些则收取少量费用。有些是完全自动的, 在后台静默工作 (例如, 自动将电子书亮点与笔记应用程序同步), 而另一些则需要一些手动操作 (例如拍摄纸质笔记本的照片以数字方式保存)。 [IV](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-025) 但无论如何, 捕捉的动作只需要片刻——点击分享、导出或保存——瞧, 你已经在你的第二大脑中保留了你正在消费的任何东西的最好部分。

Make no mistake: you will continue to use many kinds of software to manage information—such as computer folders, cloud storage drives, and various platforms for sharing and collaborating on documents. Think of your capture tools as your extended nervous system, reaching out into the world to allow you to sense your surroundings. No matter how many different kinds of software you use, don’t leave all the knowledge they contain scattered across dozens of places you’ll never think to look.
