Make sure your best findings get routed back to your notes app where you can put them all together and act on them.  
毫无疑问: 您将继续使用多种软件来管理信息, 例如计算机文件夹、云存储驱动器以及用于共享和协作处理文档的各种平台。将您的捕获工具想象成您的扩展神经系统, 伸入世界以让您感知周围的环境。 无论你使用多少种不同的软件, 都不要把它们包含的所有知识分散在几十个你永远不会想去看看的地方。确保将您的最佳发现路由回您的笔记应用程序, 您可以将它们放在一起并根据它们采取行动。

Here are some of the most popular ways of using capture tools to save content you come across:  
以下是使用捕获工具保存您遇到的内容的一些最流行的方法:

*   **Capturing passages from ebooks:** Most ebook apps make it very easy to highlight passages as you read. On Amazon Kindle, you can simply drag your finger across a sentence or paragraph you like to add a highlight. Then use the share menu to export all your highlights from the entire book all at once straight to your digital notes. You can also add comments right alongside the text as you read, which will help you remember what you found interesting about a passage.  
    从电子书中捕捉段落: 大多数电子书应用程序都可以在您阅读时轻松突出显示段落。在 Amazon Kindle 上, 您只需将手指拖过您喜欢的句子或段落即可添加高亮显示。然后使用共享菜单将整本书中的所有亮点一次性全部直接导出到您的数字笔记中。您还可以在阅读时在文本旁边添加评论, 这将帮助您记住您对段落感兴趣的地方。
*   **Capturing excerpts from online articles or web pages:** When you come across an online article or blog post you want to read, save it to a “read later” app, which is like a digital magazine rack of everything you want to read (or watch or listen to) at some point. Whenever you have some free time (such as on breaks or in the evening after work), scroll through the articles you’ve saved and pick one to read. You can make highlights, just as with ebooks, and they can also be automatically exported to your notes app using a third-party platform.  
    从在线文章或网页中摘录: 当您遇到想要阅读的在线文章或博客文章时, 将其保存到 “稍后阅读” 应用程序中, 它就像一个数字杂志架, 包含您想要阅读 (或观看) 的所有内容或听)在某些时候。每当您有空闲时间 (例如休息时间或下班后的晚上) 时, 滚动浏览您保存的文章并选择一篇阅读。您可以制作高亮显示, 就像电子书一样, 它们也可以使用第三方平台自动导出到您的笔记应用程序。
*   **Capturing quotes from podcasts:** Many podcast player apps allow you to bookmark or “clip” segments of episodes as you’re listening to them. Some of them will even transcribe the audio into text, so you can export and search it within your notes.  
    从播客中捕捉引述: 许多播客播放器应用程序允许您在收听时为剧集片段添加书签或 “剪辑”。其中一些甚至会将音频转录成文本, 因此您可以导出并在笔记中搜索。
*   **Capturing voice memos:** Use a voice memo app that allows you to press a button, speak directly into your smartphone, and have every word transcribed into text and exported to your notes.  
    捕获语音备忘录: 使用语音备忘录应用程序, 您只需按下一个按钮, 直接对着您的智能手机说话, 然后将每个单词转录成文本并导出到您的笔记中。
*   **Capturing parts of YouTube videos:** This is a little-known feature, but almost every YouTube video is accompanied by an automatically generated transcript. Just click the “Open transcript” button and a window will open. From there, you can copy and paste excerpts to your notes.  
    Capturing parts of YouTube videos: 这是一个鲜为人知的功能, 但几乎每个 YouTube 视频都附有自动生成的文字记录。只需单击 “打开成绩单” 按钮, 就会打开一个窗口。从那里, 您可以将摘录复制并粘贴到您的笔记中。
*   **Capturing excerpts from emails:** Most popular notes apps include a feature that allows you to forward any email to a special address, and the full text of that email (including any attachments) will be added to your notes.  
    从电子邮件中捕获摘录: 大多数流行的笔记应用程序都包含一项功能, 允许您将任何电子邮件转发到一个特殊地址, 并且该电子邮件的全文 (包括任何附件) 将添加到您的笔记中。
*   **Capturing content from other apps:** You might edit photos in a photos app, or make sketches in a drawing app, or like posts in a social media app. As long as that app has a “share” button or allows for copy-and-paste, you can save whatever you’ve created directly to your notes for safekeeping.  
    从其他应用程序捕获内容: 您可以在照片应用程序中编辑照片, 或在绘图应用程序中绘制草图, 或在社交媒体应用程序中点赞帖子。只要该应用程序具有 “共享” 按钮或允许复制和粘贴, 您就可以将您创建的任何内容直接保存到您的笔记中以便妥善保管。

### The Surprising Benefits of Externalizing Our Thoughts  
将我们的思想外化的惊人好处

Often ideas occur to us at the most random times—during our commute, while watching TV, when we’re playing with our kids, or in the shower.  
我们经常会在最随机的时间产生想法——在我们上下班途中, 在看电视时, 在我们和孩子玩耍时, 或者在淋浴时。

Your Second Brain gives you a place to corral the jumble of thoughts tumbling through your head and park them in a waiting area for safekeeping. Not only does this allow you to preserve them for the long term; there are multiple other profound benefits that come from the simple act of writing something down.  
您的第二大脑为您提供了一个地方, 可以将头脑中翻滚的杂乱思想集中起来, 并将它们存放在等候区以备妥善保管。这不仅可以让您长期保存它们; 写下一些东西这一简单的行为还有其他许多深远的好处。

First, you are much more likely to remember information you’ve written down in your own words. Known as the “Generation Effect,”[10](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn10) researchers have found that when people actively generate a series of words, such as by speaking or writing, more parts of their brain are activated when compared to simply reading the same words. Writing things down is a way of “rehearsing” those ideas, like practicing a dance routine or shooting hoops, which makes them far more likely to stick.  
首先, 你更有可能记住你用自己的话写下的信息。被称为 “生成效应” 的 [10](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn10) 研究人员发现, 当人们主动生成一系列单词时, 例如通过说或写, 与简单地阅读相同的单词相比, 他们大脑的更多部分会被激活。写下 things 是一种 “排练” 这些想法的方式, 例如练习舞蹈套路或投篮, 这使得它们更有可能坚持下去。

Enhancing our memory is just the beginning. When you express an idea in writing, it’s not just a matter of transferring the exact contents of your mind into paper or digital form. Writing creates new knowledge that wasn’t there before. Each word you write triggers mental cascades and internal associations, leading to further ideas, all of which can come tumbling out onto the page or screen.[V](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-024)  
增强我们的记忆力只是一个开始。当您以书面形式表达一个想法时, 这不仅仅是将您的想法的确切内容转化为纸质或数字形式的问题。写作创造了以前不存在的新知识。你写的每一个词都会触发精神连锁反应和内部联想, 从而产生更多的想法, 所有这些想法都会涌现到页面或屏幕上。 [V](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-024)

Thinking doesn’t just produce writing; writing also enriches thinking.  
思考不仅仅产生写作; 写作也丰富了思维。

There is even significant evidence that expressing our thoughts in writing can lead to benefits for our health and well-being.[11](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn11) One of the most cited psychology papers of the 1990s found that “translating emotional events into words leads to profound social, psychological, and neural changes.”  
甚至有重要证据表明, 以书面形式表达我们的想法可以为我们的健康和福祉带来好处。 [11](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch04fn11) 1990 年代被引用最多的心理学论文之一发现,“将情绪事件转化为文字会导致深刻的社会、心理和神经变化。”

In a wide range of controlled studies, writing about one’s inner experiences led to a drop in visits to the doctor, improved immune systems, and reductions in distress. Students who wrote about emotional topics showed improvements in their grades, professionals who had been laid off found new jobs more quickly, and staff members were absent from work at lower rates. The most amazing thing about these findings is that they didn’t rely on input from others. No one had to read or respond to what these people wrote down—the benefits came just from the act of writing.  
在广泛的对照研究中, 写下一个人的内心体验可以减少看医生的次数, 改善免疫系统, 减少痛苦。写情感话题的学生成绩有所提高, 被解雇的专业人士更快找到新工作, 员工缺勤率更低。这些发现最令人惊奇的是它们不依赖于其他人的输入。没有人必须阅读或回应这些人写下的内容——好处来自写作这一行为。

Perhaps the most immediate benefit of capturing content outside our heads is that we escape what I call the “reactivity loop”—the hamster wheel of urgency, outrage, and sensationalism that characterizes so much of the Internet. The moment you first encounter an idea is the worst time to decide what it means. You need to set it aside and gain some objectivity.  
捕捉我们头脑之外的内容的最直接好处可能是我们摆脱了我所说的 “反应循环”—— hamster 的紧迫感、愤怒和耸人听闻的轮子, 这是互联网上如此多的特征。你第一次遇到一个想法的那一刻是决定它意味着什么的最糟糕的时间。你需要把它放在一边并获得一些客观性。

With a Second Brain as a shield against the media storm, we no longer have to react to each idea immediately, or risk losing it forever. We can set things aside and get to them later when we are calmer and more grounded. We can take our time slowly absorbing new information and integrating it into our thinking, free of the pressing demands of the moment. I’m always amazed that when I revisit the items I’ve previously saved to read later, many of them that seemed so important at the time are clearly trivial and unneeded.  
有了第二大脑作为抵御媒体风暴的盾牌, 我们不再需要立即对每个想法做出反应, 否则就有可能永远失去它。我们可以把事情搁置一旁, 等我们更冷静、更踏实时再去处理。我们可以花时间慢慢吸收新信息并将其融入我们的思维, 而不受当下紧迫需求的影响。我总是很惊讶, 当我重温我以前保存下来供以后阅读的项目时, 其中许多在当时看起来非常重要的项目显然是微不足道且不需要的。

Notetaking is the easiest and simplest way of externalizing our thinking. It requires no special skill, is private by default, and can be performed anytime and anywhere. Once our thoughts are outside our head, we can examine them, play with them, and make them better. It’s like a shortcut to realizing the full potential of the thoughts flowing through our minds.  
做笔记是将我们的思维外化的最简单、最简单的方法。它不需要特殊技能, 默认是私有的, 可以随时随地进行。一旦我们的想法脱离了头脑, 我们就可以检查它们, 玩弄它们, 并使它们变得更好。这就像一条捷径, 可以充分发挥流过我们脑海的思想的潜力。

### Your Turn: What Would This Look Like If It Was Easy?  
轮到你了: 如果这很容易, 它会是什么样子?

I’ve introduced a lot of ideas in this chapter, and I know it’s a lot to absorb. There are so many ways to capture knowledge, but when you’re just getting started all those options can just feel overwhelming.  
我在本章中介绍了很多想法, 我知道需要吸收很多东西。获取知识的方法有很多种, 但当您刚刚开始时, 所有这些选择都会让人不知所措。

I want to give you an open question that will help guide you as you embark on this journey: What would capturing ideas look like if it was easy?  
我想给你一个开放性的问题, 它将在你踏上这段旅程时帮助指导你: 如果容易的话, 捕捉想法会是什么样子?

Think about what you would want to capture more of (or less of). How would that feel? What kinds of content are already familiar enough that it would be easy to begin saving them now? What would capturing look like today or this week? On average I capture just two notes per day—what are two ideas, insights, observations, perspectives, or lessons you’ve encountered today that you could write down right now?  
考虑一下您希望捕获更多 (或更少) 的内容。那感觉如何? 什么样的内容已经足够熟悉以至于现在很容易开始保存它们? capturing 今天或本周会是什么样子? 平均而言, 我每天只记录两个笔记——你今天遇到的两个想法、见解、观察、观点或教训是什么, 你可以马上写下来?

It’s important to keep capturing relatively effortless because it is only the first step. You need to do it enough that it becomes second nature, while conserving your time and energy for the later steps when the value of the ideas you’ve found can be fully unleashed.  
保持相对轻松的捕捉很重要, 因为这只是第一步。你需要做得足够多, 让它成为第二天性, 同时为后面的步骤节省时间和精力, 当你发现的想法的价值可以完全释放时。

Capture isn’t about doing more. It’s about taking notes on the experiences you’re already having. It’s about squeezing more juice out of the fruit of life, savoring every moment to the fullest by paying closer attention to the details.  
捕获不是要做得更多。这是关于记录你已经拥有的经历。这是关于从生活的果实中榨出更多汁液, 通过更加关注细节来充分享受每一刻。

Don’t worry about whether you’re capturing “correctly.” There’s no right way to do this, and therefore, no wrong way. The only way to know whether you’re getting the good stuff is to try putting it to use in real life. We’ll get to that soon, but in the meantime, try out a couple of digital notes apps and capture tools to see which ones fit your style. Don’t forget the resource guide I’ve put together to help you make your choice.  
不必担心您是否 “正确” 捕获。没有正确的方法可以做到这一点, 因此也没有错误的方法。了解您是否得到好东西的唯一方法是尝试将其用于现实生活中。我们很快就会谈到这一点, 但与此同时, 尝试几个数字笔记应用程序和捕捉工具, 看看哪些适合你的风格。不要忘记我整理的资源指南, 以帮助您做出选择。

If at any point you feel stuck or overwhelmed, step back and remember that nothing is permanent in the digital world. Digital content is endlessly malleable, so you don’t have to commit to any decision forever. While every step of CODE complements the others, you can also use them one at a time. Start with the parts that resonate with you and expand from there as your confidence grows.  
如果在任何时候您感到受困或不知所措, 请退后一步并记住数字世界中没有什么是永恒的。数字内容具有无限的可塑性, 因此您不必永远做出任何决定。虽然 CODE 的每一步都是对其他步骤的补充, 但您也可以一次使用它们。从与您产生共鸣的部分开始, 并随着您信心的增强从那里扩展。

In the next chapter, I’ll tell you what to do with the knowledge assets you’ve gathered in your Second Brain.  
在下一章中, 我将告诉你如何处理你在第二大脑中收集的知识资产。

1.  [I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-028-backlink). MIT economist César Hidalgo in his book _Why Information Grows_ describes how physical products, which he calls “crystals of imagination,” allow us to turn what we know into concrete objects that other people can access: “Crystallizing our thoughts into tangible and digital objects is what allows us to share our thoughts with others.” And elsewhere: “Our ability to crystallize imagination... gives us access to the practical uses of the knowledge and knowhow residing in the nervous systems of other people.”  
    我 。麻省理工学院经济学家 César Hidalgo 在他的《为什么信息会增长》一书中描述了他称之为 “想象力的结晶” 的物理产品如何让我们将我们所知道的变成其他人可以访问的具体对象:“将我们的思想结晶成有形的和数字的对象是是什么让我们能够与他人分享我们的想法。”还有其他地方:“我们使想象力具体化的能力...... 使我们能够实际使用其他人神经系统中的知识和专有技术。”
2.  [II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-027-backlink). If you’re looking for a more precise answer of how much content to capture in your notes, I recommend no more than 10 percent of the original source, at most. Any more than that, and it will be too difficult to wade through all the material in the future. Conveniently, 10 percent also happens to be the limit that most ebooks allow you to export as highlights.  
    二。如果你正在寻找关于在你的笔记中捕捉多少内容的更精确的答案, 我建议最多不要超过原始来源的 10%。不止于此, 以后要翻遍所有资料就太难了。方便的是, 10% 也恰好是大多数电子书允许您导出为亮点的限制。
3.  [III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-026-backlink). Even if the original web page disappears, you can often use this information to locate an archived version using the Wayback Machine, a project of the Internet Archive that preserves a record of websites: [https://archive.org/web/](https://archive.org/web/).  
    三、即使原始网页消失了, 您也可以经常使用此信息找到使用 Wayback Machine 的存档版本, Wayback Machine 是 Internet Archive 的一个项目, 用于保存网站记录: [https://archive.org/web/](https://archive.org/web/) 。
4.  [IV](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-025-backlink). The software landscape is constantly changing, so I’ve created a resource guide with my continually updated recommendations of the best capture tools, both free and paid, and for a variety of devices and operating systems. You can find it at [buildingasecondbrain.com/resources](http://www.buildingasecondbrain.com/resources).  
    四、软件领域在不断变化, 因此我创建了一个资源指南, 其中包含我不断更新的最佳捕获工具推荐, 包括免费和付费的, 适用于各种设备和操作系统。您可以在 buildingasecondbrain.com/resources 找到它。
5.  [V](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-024-backlink). This is called “detachment gain,” as explained in _The Detachment Gain: The Advantage of Thinking Out Loud_ by Daniel Reisberg, and refers to the “functional advantage to putting thoughts into externalized forms” such as speaking or writing, leading to the “possibility of new discoveries that might not have been obtained in any other fashion.” If you’ve ever had to write out a word to remember how it’s spelled, you’ve experienced this.  
    诉。这被称为 “超脱增益”, 正如丹尼尔 · 赖斯伯格在《超脱增益: 大声思考的优势》中所解释的那样, 指的是“将思想转化为外化形式的功能优势”, 例如口语或写作, 从而导致“可能性以任何其他方式可能无法获得的新发现。” 如果您曾经不得不写下一个单词来记住它的拼写方式, 那么您就经历过这种情况。

Chapter 5 Organize—Save for Actionability  
Chapter 5 Organize——为可操作性而保存
------------------------------------------------------------------------

> Be regular and orderly in your life so that you may be violent and original in your work.  
> 在你的生活中保持规律和有序, 这样你就可以在你的工作中表现出暴力和独创性。
> 
> —Gustave Flaubert, French novelist  
> ——古斯塔夫 · 福楼拜, 法国小说家

Twyla Tharp is one of the most celebrated, inventive dance choreographers in modern times. Her body of work is made up of more than 160 pieces, including 129 dances, twelve television specials, six major Hollywood movies, four full-length ballets, four Broadway shows, and two figure-skating routines.  
Twyla Tharp 是现代最著名、最具创造力的舞蹈编导之一。她的作品由 160 多部作品组成, 包括 129 部舞蹈、12 部电视特别节目、六部好莱坞大片、四部芭蕾舞剧、四部百老汇演出和两部花样滑冰套路。

Dance might seem like the creative medium that could least benefit from “organizing.” It is performed live each time, using primarily the dancers’ own bodies, and often seems improvisational and spontaneous. Yet in her book _The Creative Habit_,[1](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch05fn1) Tharp revealed that a simple organizing technique lies at the heart of a creative process that has propelled her through an incredibly prolific six-decade career.  
舞蹈似乎是最不能从 “组织” 中获益的创意媒介。每次都是现场表演, 主要使用舞者自己的身体, 而且往往看起来是即兴的和自发的。然而, [1](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch05fn1) Tharp 在她的《创意习惯》一书中透露, 一种简单的组织技巧是创意过程的核心, 推动她度过了一个多产的六十年职业生涯。

Tharp calls her approach “the box.” Every time she begins a new project, she takes out a foldable file box and labels it with the name of the project, usually the name of the dance she is choreographing. This initial act gives her a sense of purpose as she begins: “The box makes me feel organized, that I have my act together even when I don’t know where I’m going yet. It also represents a commitment. The simple act of writing a project name on the box means I’ve started work.”  
Tharp 称她的方法为 “盒子”。每次她开始一个新项目时, 她都会拿出一个可折叠的文件盒, 并在上面贴上项目名称的标签, 通常是她正在编舞的舞蹈名称。这个 initial 行为让她在开始时有一种目标感:“这个盒子让我感觉井井有条, 即使我还不知道我要去哪里, 我也会一起行动。这也代表着一种承诺。在盒子上写下项目名称的简单动作意味着我已经开始工作了。”

Into the box she puts anything and everything related to the project, like a swirling cauldron of creative energy. Any time she finds a new piece of material—such as “notebooks, news clippings, CDs, videotapes of me working alone in my studio, videos of the dancers rehearsing, books and photographs and pieces of art that may have inspired me”—she always knows where to put it. It all goes into the box. Which means that any time she works on that project, she knows exactly where to look—in the box.  
她把与项目相关的任何东西都放进盒子里, 就像一个旋转的创意能量大锅。每当她发现一件新材料时——比如 “笔记本、新闻剪报、CD、我在工作室里独自工作的录像带、舞者排练的视频、书籍、照片和可能激发我灵感的艺术品”——她总是知道把它放在哪里。这一切都进入了盒子。这意味着无论何时她在那个项目上工作, 她都清楚地知道要看哪里——在盒子里。

In her book, Tharp tells the story of a specific project where the box proved invaluable: a collaboration with the pop rock icon Billy Joel to turn a collection of his songs into a full-length dance performance. It was a bold idea, somewhere between a concert and a musical, but quite distinct from either one. It wasn’t clear how the characters in different songs, who weren’t written as part of the same story, could be combined into the same narrative.  
在她的书中, 撒普讲述了一个特定项目的故事, 在这个项目中, 盒子被证明是无价的: 与流行摇滚偶像比利乔尔合作, 将他的歌曲集变成了完整的舞蹈表演。这是一个大胆的想法, 介于音乐会和音乐剧之间, 但与两者截然不同。目前还不清楚不同歌曲中的角色, 不是作为同一个故事的一部分, 如何组合成同一个故事。

Even a project as open-ended as this one started the same way as all the others, with her goals: “I believe in starting each project with a stated goal. Sometimes the goal is nothing more than a personal mantra such as ‘keep it simple’ or ‘something perfect’ or ‘economy’ to remind me of what I was thinking at the beginning if and when I lose my way. I write it down on a slip of paper and it’s the first thing that goes into the box.”  
即使是像这个项目这样开放式的项目, 其开始的方式也与其他所有项目相同, 她的目标是:“我相信每个项目都应以既定目标开始。有时, 目标只不过是一句个人口头禅, 例如 “保持简单” 或“完美”或“经济”, 以提醒我在迷路时一开始的想法。我把它写在一张纸条上, 它是第一个进入盒子的东西。”

For the collaboration with Joel, Tharp had two goals: The first was to understand and master the role of narrative in dance, a long-standing creative challenge that had captured her curiosity. The second goal was much more practical, but no less motivating: to pay her dancers well. She said, “So I wrote my goals for the project, ‘tell a story’ and ‘make dance pay,’ on two blue index cards and watched them float to the bottom of the Joel box... they sit there as I write this, covered by months of research, like an anchor keeping me connected to my original impulse.”  
对于与乔尔的合作, 撒普有两个目标: 第一个是理解和掌握叙事在舞蹈中的作用, 这是一个长期存在的创造性挑战, 激发了她的好奇心。第二个目标更实际, 但同样具有激励性: 给她的舞者高薪。她说,“所以我在两张蓝色索引卡上写下了我的项目目标,‘讲 一个故事’和‘让舞蹈付出代价’, 然后看着它们漂浮在乔尔盒子的底部...... 我坐在那里写这篇文章, 经过数月的研究, 就像一个锚, 让我与最初的冲动保持联系。”

After that, every bit of research and every idea potentially relevant to the project went into Tharp’s box. Recordings of Billy Joel’s music videos, live performances, lectures, photographs, news clippings, song lists, and notes about those songs. She gathered news footage and movies about the Vietnam War, important books from the era, and even material from other boxes, including research from an abandoned project that never made it onto the stage.  
在那之后, 与该项目潜在相关的每一项研究和每一个想法都进入了 Tharp 的盒子。比利 · 乔尔 (Billy Joel) 的音乐视频、现场表演、讲座、照片、新闻剪报、歌曲列表以及关于这些歌曲的注释的录音。她收集了有关越南战争的新闻片段和电影、那个时代的重要书籍, 甚至还有其他箱子里的材料, 包括来自一个从未登上舞台的废弃项目的研究。

The artifacts that Tharp collected weren’t just for her own use. They became sparks of inspiration for her team: a pair of earrings and a macramé vest shared with the costume designer; books about psychedelic light events to inspire the lighting designer; photographs from other shows and Joel’s childhood home in Long Island to discuss with the production designer.  
撒普收集的文物不仅仅供她自己使用。它们成为她团队的灵感火花: 与服装设计师共用一对耳环和一件花边背心; 有关激发照明设计师灵感的迷幻灯光事件的书籍; 其他节目的照片和乔尔在长岛的童年故居, 以与制作设计师讨论。

All this creative raw material eventually filled twelve boxes, but all the collecting and gathering from the outside world doesn’t mean that Tharp didn’t add her own creativity. For example, she found an elaborate set of notes from an early song of Joel’s called “She’s Got a Way,” which was full of innocence and sweetness. She decided to change its meaning: “In my notes you can see the song morphing into something harsher, eventually becoming two simultaneous sleazy bar scenes, one in Vietnam, the other back home. I felt obliged to run this by Billy, warning him, ‘This is going to destroy the song.’ He wasn’t worried. ‘Go for it,’ he said.”  
所有这些创意原料最终装满了十二个盒子, 但所有来自外界的收集和收集并不意味着撒普没有加入自己的创意。例如, 她从乔尔的一首名为 “她有办法” 的早期歌曲中找到了一组精心制作的音符, 这首歌充满了天真和甜蜜。她决定改变它的意思:“在我的笔记中, 你可以看到这首歌变成了更刺耳的东西, 最终变成了两个同时出现的低俗酒吧场景, 一个在越南, 另一个在家乡。我觉得有必要让 Billy 运行这个, 警告他,“这会毁了这首歌。”他并不担心。 “去吧,”他说。

Twyla Tharp’s box gave her several powerful advantages as she set out on her creative journey.  
Twyla Tharp 的盒子在她开始创作之旅时给了她几个强大的优势。

The box gave her the security to venture out and take risks: “a box is like soil to me. It’s basic, earthy, elemental. It’s home. It’s what I can always go back to when I need to regroup and keep my bearings. Knowing that the box is always there gives me the freedom to venture out, be bold, dare to fall flat on my face.”  
盒子给了她冒险和冒险的安全感:“盒子对我来说就像土壤。它是基本的, 朴实的, 元素的。这是家。当我需要重新组合并保持方向时, 这就是我 总是可以回去的地方。知道盒子总是在那里, 让我有自由去冒险、大胆、敢于摔倒。”

The box gave Tharp a way to put projects on hold and revisit them later: “The box makes me feel connected to a project... I feel this even when I’ve back-burnered a project: I may have put the box away on a shelf, but I know it’s there. The project name on the box in bold black lettering is a constant reminder that I had an idea once and may come back to it very soon.”  
这个盒子给了萨普一种搁置项目并在以后重新审视它们的方法:“这个盒子让我觉得自己与一个项目有联系...... 即使我已经搁置了一个项目, 我也会有这种感觉: 我可能已经把盒子放在了一个地方架子, 但我知道它在那里。盒子上的项目名称以黑色粗体字体不断提醒我, 我曾经有过一个想法, 可能很快就会回来。”

Finally, it gave her a way to look back on her past victories: “There’s one final benefit to the box: It gives you a chance to look back. A lot of people don’t appreciate this. When they’re done with a project, they’re relieved. They’re ready for a break and then they want to move forward to the next idea. The box gives you the opportunity to reflect on your performance. Dig down through the boxes archaeologically and you’ll see a project’s beginnings. This can be instructive. How did you do? Did you get to your goal? Did you improve on it? Did it change along the way? Could you have done it all more efficiently?”  
最后, 它给了她一种回顾过去胜利的方式:“这个盒子还有一个最后的好处: 它让你有机会回顾过去。很多人不欣赏这一点。当他们完成一个项目时, 他们会松一口气。他们准备好休息一下, 然后他们想继续下一个想法。该框让您有机会反思自己的表现。以考古学的方式深入挖掘这些盒子, 你会看到一个项目的起源。这可能很有启发性。你怎么做的? 你达到目标了吗? 你改进了吗? 一路上有变化吗? 你能更有效地完成这一切吗?

Twyla Tharp’s box reveals the true value of a simple container: it is easy to use, easy to understand, easy to create, and easy to maintain. It can be moved from place to place without losing its contents. A container requires no effort to identify, to share with others, and to put in storage when it’s no longer needed. We don’t need complex, sophisticated systems to be able to produce complex, sophisticated works.  
Twyla Tharp 的盒子揭示了一个简单容器的真正价值: 它易于使用、易于理解、易于创建且易于维护。它可以从一个地方移动到另一个地方而不会丢失其内容。容器无需努力即可识别、与他人共享以及在不再需要时存放。我们不需要复杂、精密的系统来制作复杂、精密的作品。

### The Cathedral Effect: Designing a Space for Your Ideas  
大教堂效应: 为你的想法设计一个空间

Consider how much time we spend designing and arranging our physical environment.  
想想我们花了多少时间来设计和布置我们的物理环境。

We buy nice furniture, deliberate for weeks over the color of our walls, and fiddle with the placement of plants and books. We know that the details of lighting, temperature, and the layout of a space dramatically affect how we feel and think.  
我们买了漂亮的家具, 考虑了几周的墙壁颜色, 摆弄着植物和书籍的摆放位置。我们知道, 照明、温度和空间布局的细节会极大地影响我们的感受和思考方式。

There’s a name for this phenomenon: the Cathedral Effect.[2](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch05fn2) Studies have shown that the environment we find ourselves in powerfully shapes our thinking. When we are in a space with high ceilings, for example—think of the lofty architecture of classic churches invoking the grandeur of heaven—we tend to think in more abstract ways. When we’re in a room with low ceilings, such as a small workshop, we’re more likely to think concretely.  
这种现象有一个名字: 大教堂效应。 [2](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch05fn2) 研究表明, 我们所处的环境极大地塑造了我们的思维。例如, 当我们在一个天花板很高的空间时——想想古典教堂的崇高建筑唤起天堂的宏伟——我们倾向于以更抽象的方式思考。当我们在一个天花板较低的房间里时, 比如一个小作坊, 我们更有可能具体地思考。

No one questions the importance of having physical spaces that make us feel calm and centered, but when it comes to your digital workspace, it’s likely you’ve spent little time, if any, arranging that space to enhance your productivity or creativity. As knowledge workers we spend many hours every day within digital environments—our computers, smartphones, and the web. Unless you take control of those virtual spaces and shape them to support the kinds of thinking you want to do, every minute spent there will feel taxing and distracting.  
没有人会质疑拥有让我们感到平静和专注的物理空间的重要性, 但是当涉及到您的数字工作空间时, 您可能很少花时间 (如果有的话) 安排该空间来提高您的工作效率或创造力。作为知识工作者, 我们每天要花很多时间在数字环境中——我们的电脑、智能手机和网络。除非你控制这些虚拟空间并塑造它们以支持你想做的各种思考, 否则在那里度过的每一分钟都会让人感到疲惫和分心。

Your Second Brain isn’t just a tool—it’s an environment. It is a garden of knowledge full of familiar, winding pathways, but also secret and secluded corners. Every pathway is a jumping-off point to new ideas and perspectives. Gardens are natural, but they don’t happen by accident. They require a caretaker to seed the plants, trim the weeds, and shape the paths winding through them. It’s time for us to put more intention into the digital environments where we now spend so many of our waking hours.  
你的第二大脑不仅仅是一个工具——它还是一个环境。这是一座知识的花园, 有熟悉的曲径通幽, 也有隐秘幽静的角落。每条途径都是新想法和新观点的起点。花园是自然的, 但它们不是偶然发生的。他们需要看守来播种植物、修剪杂草并塑造蜿蜒穿过它们的路径。现在是我们将更多注意力投入到我们现在花费大量清醒时间的数字环境中的时候了。

Once you’ve created this environment, you’ll know where to go when it’s time to execute or create. You won’t have to sit down and spend half an hour painstakingly gathering together all the materials you need to get started. Your Second Brain is like a mind cathedral that you can step into any time you want to shut out the world and imagine a world of your own.  
一旦你创建了这个环境, 你就会知道在执行或创建时去哪里。你不必坐下来花半个小时煞费苦心地收集所有材料 你需要开始。你的第二大脑就像一座心灵大教堂, 你可以随时进入, 想将世界拒之门外, 想象一个属于你自己的世界。

The next step in building your Second Brain is to take the morsels of insight you’ve begun to capture and organize them in a space where you can do your best thinking.  
构建 “第二大脑” 的下一步是将你已经开始捕捉的洞察力碎片整理到一个你可以发挥最佳思维的空间中。

### Organizing for Action: Where 99 Percent of Notetakers Get Stuck (And How to Solve It)  
组织行动: 99% 的记录者陷入困境 (以及如何解决)

As you begin to capture your ideas in a consistent way, you’re likely to experience a new sense of excitement about the information flowing all around you.  
当您开始以一致的方式捕捉您的想法时, 您可能会对周围流动的信息产生一种新的兴奋感。

You’ll start to pay closer attention to the books you read, the conversations you have, and the interviews you listen to, knowing that any interesting idea you encounter can be reliably saved and utilized. You no longer have to hope that you’ll remember your best ideas—you can ensure that you will.  
你会开始更密切地关注你读过的书、你进行的对话和你听过的采访, 知道你遇到的任何有趣的想法都可以可靠地保存和利用。你不再需要希望你会记住你最好的想法——你可以确保你会记住。

Soon, however, you’ll run head on into a new problem: what to do with all this valuable material you’ve gathered. The more diligently you collect it, the bigger this problem will be! Capturing notes without an effective way to organize and retrieve them only leads to more overwhelm.  
然而, 很快, 你就会遇到一个新问题: 如何处理你收集到的所有这些有价值的材料。收集得越用心, 这个问题就越大! 在没有有效的方式组织和检索笔记的情况下捕捉笔记只会导致更加不知所措。

I spent years trying different ways of solving the problem of how to organize my digital life. I tried techniques borrowed from organizing physical spaces, every kind of specially formatted notebook, and even the Dewey decimal system used in libraries. I tried organizing my files by date, by subject, by kind, and countless other elaborate schemes, but every method I tried soon failed.  
我花了数年时间尝试不同的方法来解决如何组织我的数字生活的问题。我尝试了从组织物理空间、各种特殊格式的笔记本、甚至图书馆中使用的杜威十进制系统中借鉴的技术。我尝试过按日期、主题、种类和无数其他精心设计的方案来组织我的文件, 但我尝试过的每一种方法很快都失败了。

The problem was that none of these systems was integrated into my daily life. They always required me to follow a series of elaborate rules that took time away from my other priorities, which meant they would quickly become outdated and obsolete. Every time I fell off the organizing wagon, I reverted to dropping all my notes and files into a folder for whichever project I was currently focused on. This ensured that at least I had exactly what I needed for my current work immediately on hand—no tagging, filing, or keywords needed.  
问题是这些系统都没有融入我的日常生活。他们总是要求我遵循一系列精心设计的 规则, 这些规则占用了我其他优先事项的时间, 这意味着它们很快就会过时和过时。每次我从组织的马车上掉下来时, 我都会恢复到将所有笔记和文件放入我当前关注的项目的文件夹中。这确保至少我手头上有我当前工作所需的东西——不需要标记、归档或关键字。

Then one day I had a realization: Why didn’t I just organize my files that way all the time? If organizing by project was the most natural way to manage information with minimal effort, why not make it the default?  
然后有一天我意识到: 为什么我不一直这样组织我的文件? 如果按项目组织是管理信息最自然的方式, 而且工作量最小, 为什么不将其设为默认方式呢?

That is what I did, and to my surprise, it worked. Over time, I refined, simplified, and tested this action-based approach with thousands of students and followers. I eventually named this organizing system PARA,[I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-023) which stands for the four main categories of information in our lives: Projects, Areas, Resources, and Archives. These four categories are universal, encompassing _any_ kind of information, from _any_ source, in _any_ format, for _any_ purpose.[II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-022)  
这就是我所做的, 令我惊讶的是, 它奏效了。随着时间的推移, 我改进、简化并与成千上万的学生和追随者一起测试了这种基于行动的方法。我最终将这个组织系统命名为 PARA, [I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-023) 代表我们生活中的四大类信息: 项目、区域、资源和档案。这四个类别是通用的, 涵盖任何类型的信息, 来自任何来源, 以任何格式, 用于任何目的。 [II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-022)

PARA can handle it all, regardless of your profession or field, for one reason: it organizes information based on _how actionable it is_, not _what kind of information it is_. The project becomes the main unit of organization for your digital files. Instead of having to sort your notes according to a complex hierarchy of topics and subtopics, you have to answer only one simple question: “In which project will this be most useful?” It assumes only that you are currently working on a certain set of projects, and that your information should be organized to support them.  
PARA 可以处理这一切, 无论您的职业或领域如何, 原因之一是: 它根据信息的可操作性而不是信息的类型来组织信息。该项目成为您数字文件的主要组织单位。不必根据复杂的主题和子主题层次结构对笔记进行分类, 您只需回答一个简单的问题:“这在哪个项目中最有用?” 它仅假定您目前正在从事一组特定的项目, 并且您的信息应该被组织起来以支持它们。

For example, say you come across a useful article on how to become more resilient and capture it in your notes. You’re sure that this information will come in handy one day, but how do you know where to put those notes in the meantime? How will you remember where to look the next time you need them? This can quickly become an anxiety-provoking decision because of the risk of making the wrong choice.  
例如, 假设您看到一篇关于如何变得更有弹性的有用文章并将其记录在笔记中。您确信这些信息总有一天会派上用场, 但是您怎么知道同时将这些笔记放在哪里呢? 下次需要它们时, 您将如何记住去哪里找? 由于做出错误选择的风险, 这很快就会成为一个令人焦虑的决定。

Most people would save this note by subject in a folder called “Psychology.” That seems like a perfectly logical choice. Here’s the problem: the subject of “Psychology” is far too broad to be useful. Imagine your future self a few weeks or months from now. In the middle of your workday, how much time will you have to search through all your notes on such a broad subject? There might be notes from many dozens of articles, books, and other resources in there, much of which won’t be actionable at all. It would take hours just to figure out what you have.  
大多数人会按主题将此笔记保存在名为 “心理学” 的文件夹中。这似乎是一个完全合乎逻辑的选择。这就是问题所在:“心理学”的主题太宽泛以至于没有用处。想象一下几周或几个月后你未来的自己。在一天的工作中, 您将有多少时间来搜索关于如此广泛主题的所有笔记? 那里可能有许多文章、书籍和其他资源的注释, 其中大部分根本无法操作。仅仅弄清楚你拥有什么就需要几个小时。

There’s another way. I will show you how to take the notes you’ve captured and save them according to a practical use case. By taking that small extra step of putting a note into a folder (or tagging it[III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-021)) for a specific project, such as a psychology paper you’re writing or a presentation you’re preparing, you’ll encounter that idea right at the moment it’s most relevant. Not a moment before, and not a moment after.  
还有另一种方法。我将根据实际用例向您展示如何记下您捕获的笔记并保存它们。通过为特定项目 (例如您正在撰写的心理学论文或正在准备的演示文稿) 将笔记放入文件夹(或将其标记为 [III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-021) ) 这一额外的小步骤, 您会正确地遇到这个想法目前它是最相关的。片刻之前没有, 片刻之后也没有。

If there isn’t a current project that your note would be useful for, we have a couple of other options of where to put it, including dedicated places for each of the main “areas” of your life that you are responsible for, and “resources,” which is like a personal library of references, facts, and inspiration. Over time, as you complete your projects, master new skills, and progress toward your goals, you’ll discover that some notes and resources are no longer actionable. I’ll show you how to move them to your “archives” to keep them out of sight but within easy reach.  
如果当前没有您的笔记对您有用的项目, 我们还有其他几个选择可以将其放置在何处, 包括您负责的每个主要生活 “领域” 的专用位置, 以及 “资源”, 就像一个包含参考资料、事实和灵感的个人图书馆。随着时间的推移, 当您完成项目、掌握新技能并朝着目标前进时, 您会 discover 一些笔记和资源不再可操作。我将向您展示如何将它们移动到您的“档案” 中, 使它们远离视线但又触手可及。

These four categories—Projects, Areas, Resources, and Archives—make up the four categories of PARA. We’ll explore each of them soon.  
这四个类别——项目、区域、资源和档案——构成了 PARA 的四个类别。我们将很快探索它们中的每一个。

Instead of requiring tons of time meticulously organizing your digital world, PARA guides you in quickly sorting your ideas according to what really matters: your goals.  
PARA 无需花费大量时间精心组织您的数字世界, 而是指导您根据真正重要的事情: 您的目标, 快速整理您的想法。

One of the biggest temptations with organizing is to get too perfectionistic, treating the process of organizing as an end in itself. There is something inherently satisfying about order, and it’s easy to stop there instead of going on to develop and share our knowledge. We need to always be wary of accumulating so much information that we spend all our time managing it, instead of putting it to use in the outside world.  
组织的最大诱惑之一就是过于完美主义, 将组织过程本身视为目的。秩序本身就让人感到满足, 很容易止步于此, 而不是继续发展和分享我们的知识。我们需要始终保持警惕, 以免积累如此多的信息, 以至于我们将所有时间都花在了管理这些信息上, 而不是将其用于外部世界。

Instead of inventing a completely different organizational scheme for every place you store information, which creates a tremendous amount of friction navigating the inconsistencies between them, PARA can be used everywhere, across any software program, platform, or notetaking tool. You can use the same system with the same categories and the same principles across your digital life.  
与其为存储信息的每个地方发明一个完全不同的组织方案, 这会在处理它们之间的不一致时产生巨大的摩擦, PARA 可以在任何地方使用, 跨越任何软件程序、平台或笔记工具。您可以在整个数字生活中使用具有相同类别和相同原则的相同系统。

You’ll always need to use multiple platforms to move your projects forward. No single platform can do everything. The intention here is not to use a single software program, but to use a single _organizing system_, one that provides consistency even as you switch between apps many times per day. A project will be the same project whether it’s found in your notes app, your computer file system, or your cloud storage drive, allowing you to move seamlessly between them without losing your train of thought.  
您始终需要使用多个平台来推进您的项目。没有一个平台可以做所有事情。这里的目的不是使用一个单一的软件程序, 而是使用一个单一的组织系统, 即使你每天在应用程序之间切换多次, 它也能提供一致性。一个项目将是同一个项目, 无论它是在你的笔记应用程序、你的计算机文件系统还是你的云存储驱动器中, 允许你在它们之间无缝移动而不会失去你的思路。

By structuring your notes and files around the completion of your active projects, your knowledge can go to work for you, instead of collecting dust like an “idea graveyard.” The promise of PARA is that it changes “getting organized” from a herculean, never-ending endeavor into a straightforward task to get over with so you can move on to more important work.  
通过围绕你的活动项目的完成来组织你的笔记和文件, 你的知识可以为你工作, 而不是 of 像 “创意墓地” 一样收集灰尘。 PARA 的承诺是, 它将 “组织起来” 从一项艰巨的、永无止境的努力变成一项简单的任务来克服, 这样你就可以继续从事更重要的工作。

### How PARA Works: Priming Your Mind (and Notes) for Action  
PARA 的工作原理: 启动您的思想 (和笔记) 以采取行动

With the PARA system, every piece of information you want to save can be placed into one of just four categories:  
使用 PARA 系统, 您想要保存的每条信息都可以归入以下四个类别之一:

1.  **Projects:** Short-term efforts in your work or life that you’re working on now.  
    项目: 您现在正在从事的工作或生活中的短期努力。
2.  **Areas:** Long-term responsibilities you want to manage over time.  
    领域: 您希望随着时间的推移管理的长期责任。
3.  **Resources:** Topics or interests that may be useful in the future.  
    资源: 将来可能有用的主题或兴趣。
4.  **Archives:** Inactive items from the other three categories.  
    档案: 来自其他三个类别的非活动项目。

#### Projects: What I’m Working on Right Now  
项目: 我现在正在做的事情

Projects include the short-term outcomes you’re actively working toward right now.  
项目包括您现在正在积极努力的短期成果。

Projects have a couple of features that make them an ideal way to organize modern work. First, they have a beginning and an end; they take place during a specific period of time and then they finish. Second, they have a specific, clear outcome that needs to happen in order for them to be checked off as complete, such as “finalize,” “green-light,” “launch,” or “publish.”  
Projects 有几个特点, 使它们成为组织现代工作的理想方式。首先, 它们有开始和结束; 它们发生在特定的时间段内, 然后结束。其次, 他们有一个特定的、明确的结果需要发生才能被核对为完成, 例如 “完成”、“绿灯”、“启动” 或“发布”。

A project-centric way of working comes naturally in the creative and performing arts. Artists have paintings, dancers have dances, musicians have songs, and poets have poems. These are clearly identifiable, discrete chunks of work. This project-centric approach is increasingly finding its way into all knowledge work, a trend named the “Hollywood model” after the way films are made.  
以项目为中心的工作方式自然而然地出现在创意和表演艺术中。艺术家有绘画, 舞者有舞蹈, 音乐家有歌曲, 诗人有诗歌。这些是清晰可辨的、离散的工作块。这种以项目为中心的方法越来越多地渗透到所有知识工作中, 这种趋势以电影的制作方式命名为 “好莱坞模式”。

As an article in the _New York Times_[3](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch05fn3) explains, “A project is identified; a team is assembled; it works together for precisely as long as is needed to complete the task; then the team disbands... The Hollywood model is now used to build bridges, design apps or start restaurants.” It is becoming more and more common for all of us to work across teams, departments, and even different companies to execute collaborative projects, and then once it’s over, each go our own way.  
正如纽约时报 [3](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch05fn3) 的一篇文章所解释的那样,“一个项目被确定; 组建团队; 只要需要完成任务, 它就会一起工作; 然后团队解散...... 好莱坞模式现在被用来建造桥梁、设计应用程序或开办餐馆。” 我们所有人跨团队、跨部门, 甚至不同公司来执行协作项目变得越来越普遍, 一旦项目结束, 各走各的路。

Examples of projects could include:  
项目示例可能包括:

*   **Projects at work:** Complete web-page design; Create slide deck for conference; Develop project schedule; Plan recruitment drive.  
    工作项目: 完成网页设计; 为会议创建幻灯片; 制定项目进度表; 计划招聘活动。
*   **Personal projects:** Finish Spanish language course; Plan vacation; Buy new living room furniture; Find local volunteer opportunity.  
    个人项目: 完成西班牙语课程; 计划假期; 购买新的客厅家具; 寻找当地的志愿者机会。
*   **Side projects:** Publish blog post; Launch crowdfunding campaign; Research best podcast microphone; Complete online course.  
    业余项目: 发布博客文章; 发起众筹活动; 研究最好的播客麦克风; 完成在线课程。

If you are not already framing your work in terms of specific, concrete projects, making this shift will give you a powerful jump start to your productivity. Whether you’re self-employed, at a large corporation, or somewhere in between, we are all moving toward a world of project-based work. Knowing which projects you’re currently committed to is crucial to being able to prioritize your week, plan your progress, and say no to things that aren’t important.  
如果您还没有根据特定的、具体的项目来规划您的工作, 那么进行这种转变将为您的 productivity 提供一个强大的起点。无论您是个体经营者、大公司还是介于两者之间, 我们都在朝着基于项目的工作世界迈进。了解您当前致力于哪些项目对于确定您的一周的优先级、计划您的进度以及对不重要的事情说不至关重要。

#### Areas: What I’m Committed to Over Time  
领域: 随着时间的推移, 我致力于什么

As important as projects are, not everything is a project.  
尽管项目很重要, 但并非一切都是项目。

For example, the area of our lives called “Finances” doesn’t have a definite end date. It’s something that we will have to think about and manage, in one way or another, for as long as we live. It doesn’t have a final objective. Even if you win the lottery, you’ll still have finances to manage (and it will probably require a lot more attention!).  
例如, 我们生活中称为 “财务” 的领域没有明确的结束日期。这是我们一生都必须以某种方式思考和管理的事情。它没有最终目标。即使您中了彩票, 您仍然需要管理财务(并且可能需要更多的关注!)。

In our work lives, we have various ongoing areas we’re responsible for, such as “product development,” “quality control,” or “human resources.” These are the job responsibilities that we were hired to take on. Sometimes there are others that we officially or unofficially have taken ownership of over time.  
在我们的工作生活中, 我们负责不同的持续领域, 例如 “产品开发”、“质量控制” 或“人力资源”。这些是我们受雇承担的工作职责。有时, 随着时间的推移, 我们正式或非正式地拥有其他人的所有权。

Each of these is an example of an area of responsibility, and together they make up the second main category of PARA. All these areas, both personal and professional, require certain information to be handled effectively, but they’re not the same as projects.  
其中每一个都是一个责任领域的例子, 它们一起构成了 PARA 的第二个主要类别。所有这些个人和专业领域都需要有效处理某些信息, 但它们与项目不同。

In the case of finances, you may have notes from calls with your financial advisor, receipts or invoices for business purchases, and your monthly household budget, among many other kinds of information. You might also have more speculative information to manage, like financial projections, research on personal finance software, and data on investment trends you’re keeping an eye on.  
在财务方面, 您可能有与财务顾问通话的记录、商业采购的收据或发票、每月的家庭预算, 以及许多其他类型的信息。您可能还需要管理更多投机信息, 例如财务预测、个人理财软件研究以及您关注的投资趋势数据。

For a work-related area like “product development,” you might need to save product specifications, R&D findings, notes from customer research interviews, and customer satisfaction ratings. You could also have photos of products you admire to use as design inspiration, manufacturing blueprints, or color palettes. It all depends on your relationship to that area of your life, and how you want to manage it or move it forward.  
对于 “产品开发” 等与工作相关的领域, 您可能需要保存产品规格、研发结果、客户研究访谈记录和客户满意度评级。您还可以将您欣赏的产品照片用作设计灵感、制造蓝图或调色板。这完全取决于您与生活中那个领域的关系, 以及您想如何管理它或推动它前进。

Examples of areas from your personal life could include:  
您个人生活领域的示例可能包括:

*   **Activities or places you are responsible for:** Home/apartment; Cooking; Travel; Car.  
    您负责的活动或地点: 家 / 公寓; 烹饪; 旅行; 车。
*   **People you are responsible for or accountable to:** Friends; Kids; Spouse; Pets.  
    您负责或向其负责的人: 朋友; 孩子们; 配偶; 宠物。
*   **Standards of performance you are responsible for:** Health; Personal growth; Friendships; Finances.  
    您负责的绩效标准: 健康; 个人成长; 友谊; 财政。

In your job or business: 在您的工作或业务中:

*   **Departments or functions you are responsible for:** Account management; Marketing; Operations; Product development.  
    您负责的部门或职能: 账户管理; 营销; 操作; 产品开发。
*   **People or teams you are responsible for or accountable to:** Direct reports; Manager; Board of directors; Suppliers.  
    您负责或向其负责的人员或团队: 直接下属; 经理; 董事会; 供应商。
*   **Standards of performance you are responsible for:** Professional development; Sales and marketing; Relationships and networking; Recruiting and hiring.  
    您负责的绩效标准: 专业发展; 销售和营销; 关系和网络; 招聘和雇用。

Even though areas have no final outcome, it is still important to manage them. In fact, if you look at the list above, these areas are critical to your health, happiness, security, and life satisfaction.  
即使区域没有最终结果, 管理它们仍然很重要。事实上, 如果您查看上面的列表, 这些领域对您的健康、幸福、安全和生活满意度至关重要。

While there is no goal to reach, there is a _standard_ that you want to uphold in each of these areas. For finances, that standard may be that you always pay your bills on time and provide for your family’s basic needs. For health, it may be that you exercise a certain number of times per week and keep your cholesterol below a certain number. For family, it may be that you spend quality time with them every evening and on the weekend.  
虽然没有要达到的目标, 但您希望在每个领域都坚持一个标准。对于财务, 该标准可能是您始终按时支付账单并满足家庭的基本需求。为了健康, 可能是你每周锻炼一定次数, 让你的胆固醇保持在一定数量以下。对于家人, 您可能每天晚上和周末都与他们共度美好时光。

Only you can decide what those standards are. For our purposes, it helps tremendously to have a place dedicated to each of them. That way you always have somewhere to put any thought, reflection, idea, or useful tidbit of information relevant to each important aspect of your life.  
只有您可以决定这些标准是什么。就我们的目的而言, 为他们每个人设立一个专门的地方非常有帮助。这样一来, 您总有地方可以放置与您生活的各个重要方面相关的任何想法、反思、想法或有用的信息。

#### Resources: Things I Want to Reference in the Future  
资源: 以后想参考的东西

The third category of information that we want to keep is resources. This is basically a catchall for anything that doesn’t belong to a project or an area and could include any topic you’re interested in gathering information about.  
我们要保留的第三类信息是资源。这基本上是对不属于项目或区域的任何事物的统称, 并且可以包括您有兴趣收集相关信息的任何主题。

For example: 例如:

*   **What topics are you interested in?** Architecture; Interior design; English literature; Beer brewing.  
    您对哪些主题感兴趣? 建筑学; 室内设计; 英国文学; 啤酒酿造。
*   **What subjects are you researching?** Habit formation; Notetaking; Project management; Nutrition.  
    你在研究什么课题? 习惯养成; 做笔记; 项目管理; 营养。
*   **What useful information do you want to be able to reference?** Vacation itineraries; Life goals; Stock photos; Product testimonials.  
    您希望能够参考哪些有用的信息? 假期行程; 生活目标; 库存照片; 产品评价。
*   **Which hobbies or passions do you have?** Coffee; Classic movies; Hip-hop music; Japanese anime.  
    你有哪些爱好或爱好? 咖啡; 经典电影; 嘻哈音乐; 日本动漫。

Any one of these subjects could become its own resource folder. You can also think of them as “research” or “reference materials.” They are trends you are keeping track of, ideas related to your job or industry, hobbies and side interests, and things you’re merely curious about. These folders are like the class notebooks you probably kept in school: one for biology, another for history, another for math. Any note or file that isn’t relevant or actionable for a current project or area can be placed into resources for future reference.  
这些主题中的任何一个都可以成为其自己的资源文件夹。您也可以将它们视为 “研究” 或“参考资料”。它们是您正在跟踪的趋势、与您的工作或行业相关的想法、爱好和副业兴趣, 以及您只是好奇的事情。这些文件夹就像您可能在学校保留的课堂笔记本: 一个用于生物学, 另一个用于历史, 另一个用于数学。任何与当前项目或区域不相关或不可操作的注释或文件都可以放入资源中以供将来参考。

#### Archives: Things I’ve Completed or Put on Hold  
档案: 我已经完成或搁置的事情

Finally, we have our archives. This includes any item from the previous three categories that is no longer active. For example:  
最后, 我们有我们的档案。这包括前三个类别中不再活跃的任何项目。例如:

*   Projects that are completed or canceled  
    已完成或取消的项目
*   Areas of responsibility that you are no longer committed to maintaining (such as when a relationship ends or after moving out of your apartment)  
    您不再致力于维护的责任领域 (例如关系结束或搬出公寓后)
*   Resources that are no longer relevant (hobbies you lose interest in or subjects you no longer care about)  
    不再相关的资源 (您失去兴趣的爱好或您不再关心的主题)

The archives are an important part of PARA because they allow you to place a folder in “cold storage” so that it doesn’t clutter your workspace, while safekeeping it forever just in case you need it. Unlike with your house or garage, there is no penalty for keeping digital stuff forever, as long as it doesn’t distract from your day-to-day focus. If you need access to that information in the future—for example, if you take on a project similar to one you previously completed—you can call it up within seconds.  
档案是 PARA 的重要组成部分, 因为它们允许您将文件夹放在 “冷库” 中, 这样​​它就不会弄乱您的工作空间, 同时永远妥善保管它以备不时之需。与您的房子或车库不同, 永远保留数字内容不会受到任何惩罚, 只要它不会分散您的日常注意力即可。如果您将来需要访问该信息——例如, 如果您从事与之前完成的项目类似的项目——您可以在几秒钟内调用它。

### What PARA Looks Like: A Behind-the-Scenes Snapshot  
PARA 的样子: 幕后花絮

PARA is a universal system of organization designed to work across your digital world. It doesn’t work in only one place, requiring you to use completely different organizing schemes in each of the dozens of places you keep things. It can and should be used everywhere, such as the documents folder on your computer, your cloud storage drives, and of course, your digital notes app.  
PARA 是一个通用的组织系统, 旨在在您的数字世界中运作。它不仅在一个地方有效, 还要求你在你存放东西的几十个地方中的每一个地方都使用完全不同的组织方案。它可以而且应该随处使用, 例如计算机上的文档文件夹、云存储驱动器, 当然还有数字笔记应用程序。

Let me show you what it looks like.  
让我告诉你它的样子。

Here’s an example of what the folders in my notes app look like with PARA:  
这是我的笔记应用程序中的文件夹在 PARA 中的样子的示例:

Inside each of these top-level folders, I have individual folders for the specific projects, areas, resources, and archives that make up my life. For example, here are the folders for each one of my active projects:  
在每个顶级文件夹中, 我都有用于构成我生活的特定项目、区域、资源和档案的单独文件夹。例如, 这里是我的每个活动项目的文件夹:

Inside these folders live the actual notes that contain my ideas. The number of active projects usually ranges from five to fifteen for the average person. Notice that the number of notes inside each one (indicated by the number in parentheses after the title) varies greatly, from just two to over two hundred for the book you’re reading right now.  
在这些文件夹中存放着包含我的想法的实际笔记。对于普通人来说, 活动项目的数量通常在 5 到 15 之间。请注意, 每本书中的注释数量 (由标题后括号中的数字表示) 差异很大, 对于您正在阅读的这本书, 从只有两个到超过 200 个不等。

Here are the notes found within a typical project folder for a midsize project, a remodel of our garage into a home studio (which we’ll dive deeper into in subsequent chapters):  
以下是在中型项目的典型项目文件夹中找到的注释, 将我们的车库改造成家庭工作室 (我们将在后续章节中深入探讨):

The left half of the window displays a list of the twenty-seven notes within this folder. Clicking a note, such as the one shown above containing a collection of photos we used to inspire our own remodel, reveals its contents in the right half of the window.  
窗口的左半部分显示了该文件夹中的二十七个音符的列表。单击一个注释, 例如上面显示的包含我们用来激发我们自己改造的照片集的注释, 会在窗口的右半部分显示其内容。

That’s it—just three levels of hierarchy to encompass the thousands of notes I’ve accumulated over the years: the top-level PARA categories, the project folder, and the notes themselves.  
就是这样——只有三个层次结构来包含我多年来积累的数千条笔记: 顶级 PARA 类别、项目文件夹和笔记本身。

Here is what some of my areas look like:  
这是我的一些领域的样子:

Each of these folders contains the notes relevant to each of those ongoing areas of my life. Areas related to my business begin with “FL” for Forte Labs, so they appear together in alphabetical order. Here are some of the notes in the “Health” area:  
这些文件夹中的每一个都包含与我生活中每个正在进行的领域相关的笔记。与我的业务相关的领域以 Forte Labs 的 “FL” 开头, 因此它们按字母顺序一起出现。以下是 “健康” 区域的一些注意事项:

Under resources I have folders for each of the topics I’m interested in. This information isn’t currently actionable, so I don’t want it cluttering up my projects, but it will be ready and waiting if I ever need it.  
在资源下, 我有我感兴趣的每个主题的文件夹。此信息当前不可操作, 所以我不希望 it 弄乱我的项目, 但它会准备好并在我需要时等待它。

The archives contain any folder from the previous three categories that is no longer active. I want them completely out of sight and off my mind, but in case I ever need to access research, learnings, or material from the past, it will always be preserved.  
档案包含前三个类别中不再活动的任何文件夹。我希望它们完全消失在我的视线之外, 也从我的脑海中消失, 但如果我需要访问过去的研究、学习或材料, 它们将始终被保存下来。

PARA can be used across all the different places where you store information, meaning you can use the same categories and the same rules of thumb no matter where you keep content. For example, here is the documents folder on my computer:  
PARA 可用于您存储信息的所有不同位置, 这意味着无论您在何处保存内容, 都可以使用相同的类别和相同的经验法则。例如, 这是我电脑上的文档文件夹:

And the folders for each one of my active projects:  
以及我的每个活动项目的文件夹:

Inside these folders live the files that I use to execute each project. Here is the project folder dedicated to the book you’re reading right now:  
在这些文件夹中存放着我用来执行每个项目的文件。这是专用于您正在阅读的书的项目文件夹:

### Where Do I Put This?—How to Decide Where to Save Individual Notes  
我应该把它放在哪里?——如何决定在哪里保存个人笔记

Setting up folders is relatively easy. The harder question that strikes fear into the heart of every organizer is “Where do I put this?”  
设置文件夹相对容易。让每个组织者心生恐惧的更难的问题是 “我把它放在哪里?”

Apps have made it extremely easy to capture content—it’s just a click or a tap away. However, we are given no guidance for what to do next. Where does a note go once it’s been created? What is the correct location for an incoming file? The more material piles up, the more urgent and stressful this problem becomes.  
应用程序使捕获内容变得极其容易——只需单击一下或轻点一下即可。但是, 我们没有得到下一步该做什么的指导。笔记创建后会去哪里? 传入文件的正确位置是什么? 材料堆积得越多, 这个问题就越紧迫、压力越大。

The temptation when initially capturing notes is to also try to decide where they should go and what they mean. Here’s the problem: the moment you first capture an idea is the worst time to try to decide what it relates to. First, because you’ve just encountered it and haven’t had any time to ponder its ultimate purpose, but more importantly, because forcing yourself to make decisions every time you capture something adds a lot of friction to the process. This makes the experience mentally taxing and thus less likely to happen in the first place.  
最初记录笔记时的诱惑是还试图决定它们应该去哪里以及它们的意思。问题是: 你第一次捕捉到一个想法的那一刻是试图决定它与什么相关的最糟糕的时间。首先, 因为你刚刚遇到它, 还没有时间去思考它的最终目的, 但更重要的是, 因为每次你捕捉到一些东西时都强迫自己做出决定, 这会给这个过程增加很多摩擦。这使得这种体验在精神上很费力, 因此一开始就不太可能发生。

This is why it’s so important to separate capture and organize into two distinct steps: “keeping what resonates” in the moment is a separate decision from deciding to save something for the long term. Most notes apps have an “inbox” or “daily notes” section where new notes you’ve captured are saved until you can revisit them and decide where they belong. Think of it as a waiting area where new ideas live until you are ready to digest them into your Second Brain. Separating the capturing and organizing of ideas helps you stay present, notice what resonates, and leave the decision of what to do with them to a separate time (such as a “weekly review,” which I will cover in Chapter 9).  
这就是为什么将捕捉和组织分为两个不同的步骤如此重要: 当下 “保持共鸣” 是一个独立的决定, 而不是决定长期保存一些东西。大多数笔记应用程序都有一个 “收件箱” 或“每日笔记”部分, 您捕获的新笔记会保存在其中, 直到您可以重新访问它们并决定它们属于何处。把它想象成一个等候区, 在你准备好将它们消化到你的第二大脑之前, 新的想法就在那里。将想法的捕捉和组织分开可以帮助你保持当下, 注意哪些是共鸣的, 并将如何处理它们的决定留给单独的时间(比如“每周回顾”, 我将在第 9 章中介绍)。

Once you’ve captured a batch of notes and it’s time to organize them, PARA comes into play. The four main categories are ordered by actionability to make the decision of where to put notes as easy as possible:  
一旦您捕获了一批笔记并且是时候组织它们了, PARA 就会发挥作用。四个主要类别按可操作性排序, 以便尽可能轻松地决定将注释放在哪里:

*   Projects are most actionable because you’re working on them right now and with a concrete deadline in mind.  
    项目是最可操作的, 因为你现在正在处理它们, 并且心中有一个具体的截止日期。
*   Areas have a longer time horizon and are less immediately actionable.  
    区域的时间跨度较长, 不太可立即采取行动。
*   Resources may become actionable depending on the situation.  
    根据情况, 资源可能变得可操作。
*   Archives remain inactive unless they are needed.  
    除非需要, 否则档案保持不活动状态。

This order gives us a convenient checklist for deciding where to put a note, starting at the top of the list and moving down:  
此顺序为我们提供了一个方便的清单, 用于决定在何处放置注释, 从列表顶部开始向下移动:

1.  In which project will this be most useful?  
    这在哪个项目中最有用?
2.  _If none_: In which area will this be most useful?  
    如果没有: 这在哪个领域最有用?
3.  _If none_: Which resource does this belong to?  
    如果没有: 这属于哪个资源?
4.  _If none_: Place in archives. 如果没有: 放入档案。

In other words, you are always trying to place a note or file not only where it will be useful, but where it will be useful the _soonest_. By placing a note in a project folder, you ensure you’ll see it next time you work on that project. By placing it in an area folder, you’ll come across it next time you’re thinking about that area of your work or life. By placing it in a resource folder, you’ll notice it only if and when you decide to dive into that topic and do some reading or research. By placing it in archives, you never need to see it again unless you want to.  
换句话说, 您总是试图不仅将笔记或文件放在有用的地方, 而且放在最快有用的地方。通过将注释放在项目文件夹中, 您可以确保下次处理该项目时能看到它。将它放在区域文件夹中, 下次您在考虑工作或生活的那个区域时就会遇到它。通过将它放在资源文件夹中, 只有当您决定深入研究该主题并进行一些阅读或研究时, 您才会注意到它。通过将它放入档案中, 除非您愿意, 否则您永远不需要再次看到它。

It can be easy to let our projects and goals fall by the wayside when life gets busy. Personal projects and long-term goals feel especially flexible, like you can always get around to them later. Notes, bookmarks, highlights, and research that we worked hard to find sink deeper and deeper into our file systems, until eventually we forget they even exist.  
当生活变得忙碌时, 我们的项目和目标很容易半途而废。个人项目和长期目标感觉特别灵活, 就像你以后总是可以抽空处理它们一样。我们努力寻找的笔记、书签、亮点和研究越来越深入我们的文件系统, 直到最终我们忘记它们的存在。

Organizing by actionability counteracts our tendency to constantly procrastinate and postpone our aspirations to some far-off future. PARA pulls these distant dreams into the here and now, by helping us see that we already have a lot of the information we need to get started. The goal of organizing our knowledge is to move our goals forward, not get a PhD in notetaking. Knowledge is best applied through execution, which means whatever doesn’t help you make progress on your projects is probably detracting from them.  
按可操作性进行组织可以抵消我们不断拖延并将我们的愿望推迟到某个遥远的未来的倾向。 PARA 将这些遥远的梦想带到了此时此地, 帮助我们了解我们已经拥有了开始所需的大量信息。组织我们的知识的目标是推动我们的目标向前发展, 而不是获得笔记方面的博士学位。知识最好通过执行来应用, 这意味着任何不能帮助您在项目上取得进展的事情都可能会减损它们。

### Organizing Information Like a Kitchen—What Am I Making?  
像厨房一样组织信息——我在做什么?

There is a parallel between PARA and how kitchens are organized.  
PARA 与厨房的组织方式有相似之处。

Everything in a kitchen is designed and organized to support an outcome—preparing a meal as efficiently as possible. The archives are like the freezer—items are in cold storage until they are needed, which could be far into the future. Resources are like the pantry—available for use in any meal you make, but neatly tucked away out of sight in the meantime. Areas are like the fridge—items that you plan on using relatively soon, and that you want to check on more frequently. Projects are like the pots and pans cooking on the stove—the items you are actively preparing right now. Each kind of food is organized according to how accessible it needs to be for you to make the meals you want to eat.  
厨房里的一切都经过精心设计和组织, 以支持一种结果——尽可能高效地准备一顿饭。档案馆就像冰箱一样——物品在需要时一直处于冷藏状态, 这可能是遥远的未来。资源就像食品储藏室——可供您做的任何一顿饭使用, 但同时又被整齐地藏在看不见的地方。区域就像冰箱——您计划相对较快使用的物品, 并且您希望更频繁地检查它们。项目就像在炉子上烹饪的锅碗瓢盆——你现在正在积极准备的东西。每种食物都是根据您制作自己想吃的饭菜所需的方便程度来组织的。

Imagine how absurd it would be to organize a kitchen instead by _kind of food_: fresh fruit, dried fruit, fruit juice, and frozen fruit would all be stored in the same place, just because they all happen to be made of fruit. Yet this is exactly the way most people organize their files and notes—keeping all their book notes together just because they happen to come from books, or all their saved quotes together just because they happen to be quotes.  
想象一下, 如果用食物来组织厨房是多么荒谬: 新鲜水果、干果、果汁和冷冻水果都存放在同一个地方, 只因为它们恰好都是用水果制成的。然而, 这正是大多数人组织文件和笔记的方式——将所有的书本笔记放在一起只是因为它们恰好来自书本, 或者将所有保存的引语放在一起只是因为它们恰好是引语。

Instead of organizing ideas according to _where they come from_, I recommend organizing them according to _where they are going—_specifically, the outcomes that they can help you realize. The true test of whether a piece of knowledge is valuable is not whether it is perfectly organized and neatly labeled, but whether it can have an impact on someone or something that matters to you.  
我建议不要根据想法的来源来组织想法, 而是根据它们的去向来组织它们——特别是它们可以帮助你实现的结果。真正检验一项知识是否有价值的标准, 不是它是否组织得当、标签是否整齐, 而是它能否对你所关心的人或事产生影响。

PARA isn’t a filing system; it’s a production system. It’s no use trying to find the “perfect place” where a note or file belongs. There isn’t one. The whole system is constantly shifting and changing in sync with your constantly changing life.  
PARA 不是归档系统; 这是一个生产系统。试图找到笔记或文件所属的 “完美位置” 是没有用的。没有一个。整个系统随着你不断变化的生活而不断变化。

This is a challenging idea for a lot of people to wrap their head around. We are used to organizational systems that are static and fixed. We expect to find a strict set of rules that tells us exactly where each item goes, like the precise call numbers for books in a library.  
对于很多人来说, 这是一个具有挑战性的想法。我们习惯于静态和固定的组织系统。我们期望找到一套严格的规则来告诉我们每件物品的确切去向, 例如图书馆中书籍的准确索书号。

When it comes to our personal knowledge, there is no such assigned spot. We are organizing for actionability, and “what’s actionable” is always changing. Sometimes we can receive one text message or email and the entire landscape of our day changes. Because our priorities can change at a moment’s notice, we have to minimize the time we spend filing, labeling, tagging, and maintaining our digital notes. We can’t run the risk of all that effort going to waste.  
当涉及到我们的个人知识时, 没有这样指定的位置。我们正在组织可操作性, 而 “可操作的” 是 always changing。有时我们可以收到一条短信或电子邮件, 我们一天的整个景观都会改变。因为我们的优先事项可以随时改变, 所以我们必须尽量减少花在归档、标记、标记和维护数字笔记上的时间。我们不能冒所有努力都白费的风险。

Any piece of information (whether a text document, an image, a note, or an entire folder) can and should flow between categories. You might save a note on coaching techniques to a project folder called “Coaching class,” for a class you’re taking. Later, when you become a manager at work and need to coach your direct reports, you might move that note to an area folder called “Direct reports.” At some point you might leave that company, but still remain interested in coaching, and move the note to resources. One day you might lose interest in the subject altogether and move it to the archives. In the future, that note could find its way all the way back to projects when you decide to start a side gig as a business coach, making that knowledge actionable once again.  
任何信息 (无论是文本文档、图像、笔记还是整个文件夹) 都可以而且应该在类别之间流动。您可以将有关教练技术的笔记保存到名为 “教练课程” 的项目文件夹中, 以用于您正在上的课程。稍后, 当您成为工作经理并需要指导您的直接下属时, 您可以将该注释移动到名为 “直接下属” 的区域文件夹中。在某个时候你可能会离开那家公司, 但仍然对教练感兴趣, 并将笔记移至资源。有一天你可能会完全失去对这个主题的兴趣并将它移到档案中。将来, 当您决定开始兼职作为商业教练时, 该笔记可能会一直回到项目中, 从而使该知识再次具有可操作性。

The purpose of a single note or group of notes can and does change over time as your needs and goals change. Every life moves through seasons, and your digital notes should move along with them, churning and surfacing new tidbits of insight from the deep waters of your experience.  
随着您的需求和目标的变化, 单个笔记或一组笔记的目的可以而且确实会随着时间的推移而改变。每个生命都在季节中移动, 您的数字笔记应该随之移动, 从您的经历的深水中搅动和浮现新的洞察力。

### Completed Projects Are the Oxygen of Your Second Brain  
已完成的项目是您第二大脑的氧气

Your efforts to capture content for future use will be tremendously easier and more effective if you know what that content is for. Using PARA is not just about creating a bunch of folders to put things in. It is about identifying the structure of your work and life—what you are committed to, what you want to change, and where you want to go.  
如果您知道该内容的用途, 那么您为将来使用而捕获内容的工作将变得更加容易和有效。使用 PARA 不仅仅是创建一堆文件夹来放入东西。它是关于确定你的工作和生活的结构——你致力于什么, 你想要改变什么, 以及你想去哪里。

I had to learn this lesson the hard way. Back in college, I worked part-time at an Apple Store in San Diego while I finished my studies. At the time it was one of the five busiest Apple Stores in the world, with thousands of people walking through our doors every day. It was there that I got my first taste of teaching people how to use computers more effectively.  
我不得不以艰难的方式学习这一课。回到大学时, 我一边完成学业, 一边在圣地亚哥的一家 Apple Store 做兼职。当时它是世界上最繁忙的五家 Apple Store 之一, 每天都有成千上万的人走进我们的大门。在那里, 我第一次尝到了教人们如何更有效地使用计算机的滋味。

I taught morning classes to small groups of people who had just bought their first Mac, and also did one-on-one consultation sessions. This was the golden age of Apple’s iLife suite of creative software: every single Mac computer came preinstalled with user-friendly apps for creating websites, recording music, printing photo books, and making videos. It was like having a complete multimedia studio at your fingertips at no additional cost.  
我给刚买了第一台 Mac 的一小群人上了早课, 还进行了一对一的咨询会议。这是 Apple iLife 创意软件套件的黄金时代: 每台 Mac 电脑都预装了用户友好的应用程序, 用于创建网站、录制音乐、打印相册和制作视频。这就像拥有一个触手可及的完整多媒体工作室, 无需额外费用。

I would sit down with customers and answer any questions they had about the new computer they had just bought. In most cases they had just migrated all their files over from Windows, and years of accumulated documents lay scattered across their desktop and documents folders.  
我会与客户坐下来回答他们对刚购买的新电脑的任何问题。在大多数情况下, 他们刚刚将所有文件从 Windows 迁移过来, 多年积累的文档散落在他们的桌面和文档文件夹中。

At first I tried guiding them through organizing each document one at a time. It quickly became clear that this didn’t work at all. The one-on-one sessions were only one hour, not nearly enough time to make even a dent in the hundreds or even thousands of files they had. It wasn’t time well spent anyway, because these were often old documents that weren’t relevant to their current goals or interests.  
起初, 我试着引导他们一次整理一份文件。很快就发现这根本行不通。一对一的会议只有一个小时, 时间不足以对他们拥有的数百甚至数千份文件产生影响。无论如何, 这些时间都花得不值, 因为这些通常是与他们当前的目标或兴趣无关的旧文件。

I knew I needed a new approach. I started asking questions and listening, and eventually realized that these people didn’t need or want an organized computer. They had spent all this money and time moving to a Mac because there was something they wanted to create or achieve.  
我知道我需要一种新方法。我开始提问和倾听, 最终意识到这些人不需要或不想要一台井井有条的电脑。他们花费所有这些金钱和时间转向 Mac, 因为他们想要创造或实现一些东西。

They wanted to make a video for their parents’ anniversary party, a website for their cupcake shop, or a record showcasing their band’s songs. They wanted to research their family genealogy, graduate from college, or land a better job. Everything else was just an obstacle to get past on the way to their goal.  
他们想为他们父母的周年纪念派对制作视频, 为他们的纸杯蛋糕店制作网站, 或者制作一张展示他们乐队 歌曲的唱片。他们想研究他们的家谱, 从大学毕业, 或者找到一份更好的工作。其他一切都只是他们实现目标的障碍。

I decided to take a different approach: I took all the files they’d migrated over and moved them all to a new folder titled “Archive” plus the date (for example, “Archive 5-2-21”). There was always a moment of fear and hesitation at first. They didn’t want anything to get lost, but very quickly, as they saw that they would always be able to access anything from the past, I watched them come alive with a renewed sense of hope and possibility.  
我决定采取不同的方法: 我把他们迁移过来的所有文件都移到了一个名为 “Archive” 的新文件夹中加上日期(例如,“Archive 5-2-21”)。一开始总会有片刻的恐惧和犹豫。他们不想丢失任何东西, 但很快, 当他们看到他们总是能够访问过去的任何东西时, 我看着他们带着新的希望和可能性重新焕发活力。

They had repeatedly postponed their creative ambitions to some far-off, mythical time when somehow everything would be perfectly in order. Once we set that aside and just focused on what they actually wanted to do right now, they suddenly gained a tremendous sense of clarity and motivation.  
他们一再将他们的创作抱负推迟到某个遥远的、神话般的时代, 那时一切都井然有序。一旦我们将其搁置一旁, 只关注他们现在真正想做的事情, 他们就会突然获得一种巨大的清晰感和动力。

For a while I was sure this would come back to haunt me. Eventually they’d want to go back and organize all those old files, right? I would often see the same people coming back to our store again and again. I waited expectantly in fear for someone to return and accuse me of losing all their old files.  
有一段时间我确信这会再次困扰我。最终他们会想回去整理所有那些旧文件, 对吧? 我经常会看到同一个人一次又一次地回到我们的商店。我满怀期待地等待着有人回来并指责我丢失了他们所有的旧文件。

Let me tell you: no one ever did.  
让我告诉你: 从来没有人这样做过。

Not once did someone come back and say, “You know, I’d really like to go back and organize all those files from my old computer.” What they did tell me were the stories of the impact their creative projects had: on their families, on their business, on their grades, on their career. One person organized a fundraising drive for a friend who had recently been diagnosed with leukemia. Another put together a successful application for a small business loan to start a dance studio. One student told me that the ability to tame the chaos of her digital world was the only reason she had finished college as the first graduate from her family. The details of how they organized their computer or took notes were trivial, but the impact their creativity had on their own lives and the lives of others—that was anything but.  
从来没有人回来说,“你知道, 我真的很想回去整理我旧电脑上的所有文件。” 他们告诉我的是他们的创意项目所产生影响的故事: 对他们的家庭、对他们的事业、对他们的成绩、对他们的职业。一个人为一位最近被诊断出患有白血病的朋友组织了一场筹款活动。另一个人成功申请了一笔小企业贷款, 开办了一家舞蹈工作室。一位学生告诉我, 她能够驯服数字世界的混乱, 这是她作为家里第一个毕业生完成大学学业的唯一原因。他们如何组织他们的计算机 或做笔记的细节是微不足道的, 但他们的创造力对他们自己的生活和他人的生活产生的影响——这绝不是。

There are a few lessons I took away from this experience.  
我从这次经历中吸取了一些教训。

The first is that people need clear workspaces to be able to create. We cannot do our best thinking and our best work when all the “stuff” from the past is crowding and cluttering our space. That’s why that archiving step is so crucial: you’re not losing anything, and it can all be found via search, but you need to move it all out of sight and out of mind.  
首先是人们需要清晰的工作空间才能进行创作。当过去的所有 “东西” 挤满我们的空间时, 我们就无法进行最好的思考和最好的工作。这就是归档步骤如此重要的原因: 您不会丢失任何东西, 它们都可以通过搜索找到, 但您需要将它们全部移出视线和脑海。

Second, I learned that creating new things is what really matters. I’d see a fire light up in people’s eyes when they reached the finish line and published that slideshow or exported that video or printed that résumé. The newfound confidence they had in themselves was unmistakable as they walked out of the store knowing they had everything they needed to move forward.  
其次, 我了解到创造新事物才是真正重要的。当人们到达终点线并发布该幻灯片或导出该视频或打印该简历时, 我会看到他们眼中的火光。当他们走出商店时, 他们对自己新获得的信心是显而易见的, 因为他们知道自己拥有前进所需的一切。

I’ve learned that completed creative projects are the blood flow of your Second Brain. They keep the whole system nourished, fresh, and primed for action. It doesn’t matter how organized, aesthetically pleasing, or impressive your notetaking system is. It is only the steady completion of tangible wins that can infuse you with a sense of determination, momentum, and accomplishment. It doesn’t matter how small the victories. Even the tiniest breakthrough can become a stepping-stone to more creative, more interesting futures than you can imagine.  
我了解到, 完成的创意项目是您第二大脑的血液流动。它们使整个系统保持滋养、新鲜, 并为行动做好准备。无论您的笔记系统多么有条理、多么美观或令人印象深刻, 都无关紧要。只有稳步完成实实在在的胜利, 才能让你充满决心、动力和成就感。不管胜利有多小。即使是最微小的突破也可以成为通往比你想象的更有创意、更有趣的未来的垫脚石。

### Your Turn: Move Quickly, Touch Lightly  
轮到你了: 快速移动, 轻轻触摸

A mentor of mine once gave me a piece of advice that has served me ever since: move quickly and touch lightly.  
我的一位导师曾经给我一条忠告, 从那以后一直为我服务: 动作要快, 触摸要轻。

She saw that my standard approach to my work was brute force: to stay late at the office, fill every single minute with productivity, and power through mountains of work as if my life depended on it. That wasn’t a path to success; it was a path to burnout. Not only did I exhaust my mental and physical reserves time and again; my frontal assaults weren’t even very effective. I didn’t know how to set my intentions, craft a strategy, and look for sources of leverage that would allow me to accomplish things with minimal effort.  
她看到我的标准工作方法是蛮力: 在办公室待到很晚, 让每一分钟都充满生产力, 并在堆积如山的工作中充满力量, 就好像我的生命取决于它一样。那不是通往成功的道路; 这是一条通向倦怠的道路。我不仅一次次耗尽我的精神和体力储备; 我的正面攻击甚至不是很有效。我不知道如何设定我的意图、制定策略以及寻找能让我以最小的努力完成事情的杠杆来源。

My mentor advised me to “move quickly and touch lightly” instead. To look for the path of least resistance and make progress in short steps. I want to give the same advice to you: don’t make organizing your Second Brain into yet another heavy obligation. Ask yourself: “What is the smallest, easiest step I can take that moves me in the right direction?”  
我的导师建议我 “快走轻触”。寻找阻力最小的路径, 步步为营。我想给你同样的建议: 不要让组织你的第二大脑成为另一项沉重的义务。问问自己:“让我朝着正确的方向前进的最小、最简单的步骤是什么?”

When it comes to PARA, that step is generally to create folders for each of your active projects in your notes app and begin to fill them with the content related to those projects. Once you have a home for something, you tend to find more of it. Start by asking yourself, “What projects am I currently committed to moving forward?” and then create a new project folder for each one. Here are some questions to ask yourself to help you think of the projects that might be on your plate:  
对于 PARA, 该步骤通常是为笔记应用程序中的每个活动项目创建文件夹, 并开始用与这些项目相关的内容填充它们。一旦你有了一个家, 你往往会找到更多。首先问自己,“我目前致力于推进哪些项目?” 然后为每个创建一个新的项目文件夹。这里有一些问题可以问自己, 以帮助您思考可能在您盘子里的项目:

*   **Notice what’s on your mind:** What’s worrying you that you haven’t taken the time to identify as a project? What needs to happen that you’re not making consistent progress on?  
    注意你的想法: 什么让你担心你没有花时间确定为一个项目? 您没有取得一致进展的情况需要发生什么?
*   **Look at your calendar:** What do you need to follow up on from the past? What needs planning and preparation for the future?  
    看看你的日历: 你需要跟进过去的什么? 未来需要规划和准备什么?
*   **Look at your to-do list:** What actions are you already taking that are actually part of a bigger project you’ve not yet identified? What communication or follow-up actions you’ve scheduled with people are actually part of a bigger project?  
    查看您的待办事项清单: 您已经采取的哪些行动实际上是您尚未确定的更大项目的一部分? 您与人们安排的哪些沟通或后续行动实际上是更大项目的一部分?
*   **Look at your computer desktop, downloads folder, documents folder, bookmarks, emails, or open browser tabs:** What are you keeping around because it is part of a larger project?  
    看看你的电脑桌面、下载文件夹、文档文件夹、书签、电子邮件或打开的浏览器标签: 你保留了什么, 因为它是一个更大项目的一部分?

Here’s some examples of projects my students have come up with:  
以下是我的学生提出的一些项目示例:

*   Find new doctor who accepts my insurance  
    寻找接受我的保险的新医生
*   Plan goals and agenda for annual team retreat  
    计划年度团队务虚会的目标和议程
*   Collect list of common food supplies and set up recurring deliveries  
    收集常见食品供应清单并设置定期送货
*   Develop content strategy for next quarter  
    制定下一季度的内容策略
*   Review draft of new reimbursement policy and provide feedback  
    审查新的报销政策草案并提供反馈
*   Share collaboration ideas with research partner  
    与研究伙伴分享合作想法
*   Research and draft article on health equity  
    关于健康公平的研究和文章草案
*   Complete online course on creative writing  
    完成创意写作在线课程

You could also create folders for your areas and resources, but I recommend starting only with projects to avoid creating lots of empty containers. You can always add others later when you have something to put inside them. Although you can and should use PARA across all the platforms where you store information—the three most common ones besides a notetaking app are the documents folder on your computer, cloud storage drives like Dropbox, and online collaboration suites like Google Docs—I recommend starting with just your notes app for now.  
您还可以为您的区域和资源创建文件夹, 但我建议只从项目开始, 以避免创建大量空容器。当您有东西要放入其中时, 您可以随时添加其他人。尽管您可以而且应该在存储信息的所有平台上使用 PARA——除了笔记应用程序之外最常见的三个平台是计算机上的文档文件夹、Dropbox 等云存储驱动器和 Google Docs 等在线协作套件——我建议开始现在只有你的笔记应用程序。

Practice capturing new notes, organizing them into folders, and moving them from one folder to another. Each time you finish a project, move its folder wholesale to the archives, and each time you start a new project, look through your archives to see if any past project might have assets you can reuse.  
练习捕捉新笔记, 将它们组织到文件夹中, 并将它们从一个文件夹移动到另一个文件夹。每次你完成一个项目, 将它的文件夹全部移动到档案中, 每次你开始一个新项目时, 查看你的档案, 看看是否有任何过去的项目可能有你可以重复使用的资产。

As you create these folders and move notes into them, don’t worry about reorganizing or “cleaning up” any existing notes. You can’t afford to spend a lot of time on old content that you’re not sure you’re ever going to need. Start with a clean slate by putting your existing notes in the archives for safekeeping. If you ever need them, they’ll show up in searches and remain just as you left them.  
当您创建这些文件夹并将笔记移入其中时, 不必担心重新组织或 “清理” 任何现有笔记。您无法在不确定自己是否需要的旧内容上花费大量时间。将您现有的笔记放入档案中以进行妥善保管, 从而从头开始。如果您需要它们, 它们会出现在搜索结果中并保持原样。

Your goal is to clear your virtual workspace and gather all the items related to each active project in one place. Once you do, you’ll gain the confidence and clarity to take action on those ideas, rather than letting them pile up with no end in sight.  
您的目标是清理您的虚拟工作区并将与每个活动项目相关的所有项目集中在一个地方。一旦你这样做了, 你就会有信心和清晰地对这些想法采取行动, 而不是让它们堆积如山, 看不到尽头。

The key thing to keep in mind is that these categories are anything but final. PARA is a dynamic, constantly changing system, not a static one. Your Second Brain evolves as constantly as your projects and goals change, which means you never have to worry about getting it perfect, or having it finished.  
要记住的关键是这些类别绝不是最终类别。 PARA 是一个动态的、不断变化的系统, 而不是静态的。你的第二大脑会随着你的项目和目标的变化而不断发展, 这意味着你永远不必担心让它变得完美或完成。

In the next chapter, we’ll look at how to distill the knowledge we’ve gathered to be able to put it to use effectively.  
在下一章中, 我们将研究如何提炼我们收集到的知识以便能够有效地使用它。

1.  [I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-023-backlink). _Para_ is a Greek word that means “side by side,” as in “parallel”; this convenient definition reminds us that our Second Brain works “side by side” with our biological brain.  
    I.Para 是一个希腊词, 意思是 “并排”, 如“平行”; 这个方便的定义提醒我们, 我们的第二大脑与我们的生物大脑“并肩” 工作。
2.  [II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-022-backlink). As you can probably tell, I’m a big fan of four-letter frameworks. Researchers have called it the “Magic Number 4” because it is the highest number that we can count at a glance and hold in our minds without extra effort.  
    二。您可能会说, 我是四字母框架的忠实粉丝。研究人员将其称为 “神奇数字 4”, 因为它是我们无需额外努力即可一目了然并牢记在心的最高数字。
3.  [III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-021-backlink). I will use the term “folder” to refer to the main unit of organization used by most notes apps; some software instead uses tags, which work just as well.  
    三、我将使用术语 “文件夹” 来指代大多数笔记应用程序使用的主要组织单位; 有些软件改为使用标签, 效果也一样。

Chapter 6 Distill—Find the Essence  
第 6 章 Distill——找到本质
--------------------------------------------------------

> To attain knowledge, add things every day. To attain wisdom, remove things every day.  
> 要获得知识, 每天都要添加东西。要获得智慧, 每天都要去除东西。
> 
> —Lao Tzu, ancient Chinese philosopher  
> ——中国古代哲学家老子

In 1969, studio executives at Paramount Pictures were desperate to find a film director for a new movie they had purchased the rights to, a crime drama based on the New York Mafia.  
1969 年, 派拉蒙影业公司的高管迫切希望为一部他们购买了版权的新电影——一部以纽约黑手党为背景的犯罪剧——寻找电影导演。

One after another, all the top directors of the era turned the project down. They all found it too sensationalistic for their tastes. Gangster movies were known as cliché and gimmicky, and there had been several recent duds in the genre.  
一个又一个时代的顶级导演纷纷否决了这个项目。他们都觉得这对他们的口味来说太耸人听闻了。黑帮电影以陈词滥调和噱头着称, 而且最近有几部这种类型的电影失败了。

After exhausting all their top choices, studio executives approached a young film director who had done a few small indie films. The director was a relative novice, with no major commercially successful films to his name. He was an outsider, working out of San Francisco instead of Hollywood, the industry capital. And he was known as an artist who wanted to experiment with new ideas, not a director of big budget movies.  
在用尽所有最佳选择后, 制片厂高管找到了一位拍过几部小型独立电影的年轻电影导演。导演是一个相对的新手, 他的名字没有商业上成功的主要电影。他是一个局外人, 在旧金山而不是工业之都好莱坞工作。他被认为是一位想要尝试新想法的艺术家, 而不是大预算电影的导演。

That director’s name was Francis Ford Coppola, and the movie he was asked to make was, of course, _The Godfather_.  
那位导演的名字叫弗朗西斯 · 福特 · 科波拉, 他要拍的电影当然是《教父》。

Coppola initially turned down the project. As recounted in the _Hollywood Reporter_,[1](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn1) he said, “It was more commercial and salacious than my own taste.” However, his partner and protégé George Lucas (of future _Star Wars_ fame) noted that they were broke. Without a major infusion of cash, they’d soon be evicted.  
科波拉最初拒绝了这个项目。正如 Hollywood Reporter 所述, [1](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn1) 他说,“这比我自己的口味更商业化和淫秽。”然而, 他的搭档兼门生乔治 · 卢卡斯 (后来因星球大战而声名鹊起) 指出他们已经破产了。如果没有大量现金注入, 他们很快就会被驱逐。

Mounting financial pressure, plus a second reading of the novel, changed Coppola’s mind, as he realized that it could be framed as “a story that was a metaphor for American capitalism in the tale of a great king with three sons.”
