越来越大的经济压力, 再加上对小说的重读, 改变了科波拉的想法, 因为他意识到这可以被描述为 “一个在一个伟大的国王和三个儿子的故事中隐喻美国资本主义的故事”。

_The Godfather_ would go on to become one of the greatest critical and commercial successes in filmmaking history. In 2007 the American Film Institute named it the third-best American movie of all time.[2](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn2) It ultimately grossed $245 million, won three Oscars, and spawned a series of sequels and spinoffs eaten up by a rabid fan base obsessed with the story of the fictional Corleone family.  
教父将继续成为电影制作史上最伟大的评论和商业成功之一。 2007 年, 美国电影学院将其评为有史以来第三好的美国电影。 [2](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn2) 它最终获得了 2.45 亿美元的票房收入, 赢得了三项奥斯卡奖, 并催生了一系列的续集和衍生剧, 这些续集和衍生剧被痴迷于虚构的柯里昂家族故事的狂热粉丝群所吞噬。

Coppola’s strategy for making the complex, multifaceted film rested on a technique he learned studying theater at Hofstra College, known as a “prompt book.” He started by reading _The Godfather_ novel and capturing the parts that resonated with him in a notebook—his own version of Twyla Tharp’s box. But his prompt book went beyond storage: it was the starting point for a process of revisiting and refining his sources to turn them into something new.  
科波拉制作这部复杂、多方面的电影的策略基于他在霍夫斯特拉学院学习戏剧时学到的一种技术, 被称为 “提示书”。他从阅读《教父》小说开始, 并将与他产生共鸣的部分记在笔记本上——他自己版本的 Twyla Tharp 盒子。但他的提示书不仅仅是存储: 它是重新访问和提炼他的资源以将它们变成新事物的过程的起点。

The book was made from a three-ring binder, into which he would cut and paste pages from the novel on which the film was based. It was designed to last, with reinforced grommets to ensure the pages wouldn’t tear even after many turnings. There he could add the notes and directions that would later be used to plan the screenplay and production design of the film.  
这本书是用一个三环活页夹做成的, 他会把电影所依据的小说中的几页剪切并粘贴到里面。它的设计经久耐用, 带有加固的索环以确保页面即使在多次转动后也不会撕裂。他可以在其中添加注释和说明, 这些注释和说明稍后将用于规划电影的剧本和制作设计。

In a short documentary titled _Francis Coppola’s Notebook_[3](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn3) released in 2001, Coppola explained his process. He started with an initial read of the entire novel, noting down anything that stuck out to him: “I think it’s important to put your impressions down on the first reading because those are the initial instincts about what you thought was good or what you didn’t understand or what you thought was bad.”  
在 2001 年发行的名为 Francis Coppola 的笔记本 [3](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn3) 的短片中, Coppola 解释了他的过程。他从初读整本小说开始, 记下任何让他印象深刻的地方:“我认为在第一次阅读时留下你的印象很重要, 因为这些是你最初的直觉, 你认为什么是好的, 什么不是你认为好的不明白或者你认为不好的地方。

Coppola then began to add his own interpretations, distilling and reconstituting his own version of the story. He broke down each scene according to five key criteria: a synopsis (or summary) of the scene; the historical context; the imagery and tone for the “look and feel” of a scene; the core intention; and any potential pitfalls to avoid. In his own words, “I endeavored to distill the essence of each scene into a sentence, expressing in a few words what the point of the scene was.”  
科波拉随后开始添加自己的解释, 提炼和重构他自己的故事版本。他根据五个关键标准分解每个场景: 场景的概要 (或摘要); 历史背景; 场景“外观和感觉” 的意象和色调; 核心意图; 以及要避免的任何潜在陷阱。用他自己的话说,“我努力把每个场景的精髓提炼成一句话, 用几句话表达场景的重点。”

Coppola described his binder as “a kind of multi-layered road map for me to direct the film... so I was able to review not only Mario Puzo’s original text but all my first notation as to what... was important to me or what I felt was really going on in the book.” His comments in the margins included “Hitchcock” to remind him of how the famed director of thrillers would have framed a shot, or “Frozen time” to remind him to slow down a sequence. He used different kinds of annotations to emphasize to his future self which parts of a scene were most important: “As I was reading the book and making these notes and then putting them on the margins obviously the more pens I was using and the more rulers, and the more squiggly lines, sort of implied the excitement of the book was higher and higher, so that the sheer amount of ink on the page would tell me later on this is one of the most important scenes.”  
科波拉将他的活页夹描述为 “我导演这部电影的一种多层次路线图...... 所以我不仅可以回顾马里奥 · 普佐的原文, 还可以回顾我所有关于什么...... 对我很重要或我的感受的第一个符号书中真的发生了。” 他在页边空白处的评论包括“希区柯克”, 以提醒他这位著名的惊悚片导演是如何构图的, 或者“冰冻时间”, 以提醒他放慢一个序列。他使用不同类型的注释向未来的自己强调场景的哪些部分最重要:“当我阅读这本书并做这些笔记然后将它们放在页边空白处时, 显然我使用的笔越多, 尺子越多, 还有更多的波浪线, 某种程度上暗示着这本书的精彩程度越来越高, 以至于页面上的大量墨水稍后会告诉我这是最重要的场景之一。”

The Godfather Notebook is a perfect example of the behind-the-scenes process used by successful creative professionals. Coppola considered the prompt book that emerged from this process the most important asset in the production of his now classic film: “the script was really an unnecessary document; I didn’t need a script because I could have made the movie just from this notebook.”  
教父笔记本是成功的创意专业人士使用的幕后流程的完美示例。科波拉认为从这个过程中产生的提示书是他现在的经典电影制作中最重要的资产:“脚本 确实是一个不必要的文件; 我不需要剧本, 因为我可以用这本笔记本拍电影。”

We might imagine a movie as emerging straight out of the mind of a screenplay writer or director, when in fact it depends on collecting and refining source material. Coppola’s story demonstrates that we can systematically gather building blocks from our reading and research that ultimately make the final product richer, more interesting, and more impactful.  
我们可能会想象一部电影是直接从编剧或导演的脑海中浮现出来的, 而实际上它取决于收集和提炼原始资料。科波拉的故事表明, 我们可以系统地从阅读和研究中收集构建模块, 最终使最终产品更丰富、更有趣、更有影响力。

If Francis Ford Coppola relied so much on a step-by-step process for notetaking, then so can we. We can also use our notes to drill down to the essence of the stories, research, examples, and metaphors that make up our own source material. This is the third step of CODE, to Distill. This is the moment we begin turning the ideas we’ve captured and organized into our own message. It all begins and ends with notes.  
如果弗朗西斯 · 福特 · 科波拉如此依赖循序渐进的记笔记过程, 那么我们也可以。我们还可以使用我们的笔记深入了解构成我们自己的原始资料的故事、研究、示例和隐喻的本质。这是 CODE 的第三步, 蒸馏。这是我们开始将我们捕获和组织的想法转化为我们自己的信息的时刻。这一切都以笔记开始和结束。

### Quantum Notetaking: How to Create Notes for an Unknown Future  
量子笔记: 如何为未知的未来做笔记

I’ve shown you how to capture notes with interesting ideas from the outside world or your own thoughts. You may have started organizing those notes according to their actionability and relevance to your current projects.  
我已经向您展示了如何用来自外部世界或您自己的想法的有趣想法来记录笔记。您可能已经开始根据它们的可操作性和与您当前项目的相关性来组织这些笔记。

Now what? 怎么办?

This is where even the most dedicated notetakers usually stop. They aren’t sure what to do next. They’ve gathered some interesting knowledge, but it hasn’t led anywhere. Our notes are things to use, not just things to collect.  
这是即使是最专注的笔记者通常也会停下来的地方。他们不确定下一步该做什么。他们收集了一些有趣的知识, 但并没有取得任何进展。我们的笔记是要使用的东西, 而不仅仅是要收集的东西。

When you initially capture a note, you may have only seconds to get it into your Second Brain before the next meeting, urgent task, or crying child comes calling. Not nearly enough time to fully understand what it means or how it might be used. When you first capture them, your notes are like unfinished pieces of raw material. They require a bit more refinement to turn them into truly valuable knowledge assets, like a chemist distilling only the purest compound. This is why we separate capturing and organizing from the subsequent steps: you need to be able to store something quickly and save any future refinement for later.  
当您最初记下一个笔记时, 您可能只有几秒钟的时间将其输入您的第二大脑, 然后才能召开下一次会议、紧急任务或哭闹的孩子。没有足够的时间来完全理解它的含义或如何使用它。当您第一次捕捉它们时, 您的笔记就像是未完成的原材料。他们需要更多的改进才能将它们变成真正有价值的知识资产, 就像化学家只提炼出最纯净的化合物一样。这就是我们将捕获和组织与后续步骤分开的原因: 您需要能够快速存储某些内容并保存任何未来的改进以备后用。

In this sense, notetaking is like time travel—you are sending packets of knowledge through time to your future self.  
从这个意义上说, 记笔记就像时间旅行——你正在通过时间将知识包发送给未来的自己。

You probably consume a lot of books, articles, videos, and social media posts full of interesting insights, but what are the chances that you’ll be ready to put any given piece of advice into action right at that instant? How likely is it that life will intervene, in the form of a crisis at work, an urgent meeting at your kid’s school, or an unexpected cold? In my experience, life is constantly pushing and pulling us away from our priorities. The more determined we are to focus and get something done, the more aggressively life tends to throw emergencies and delays in our face.  
您可能阅读了大量充满有趣见解的书籍、文章、视频和社交媒体帖子, 但您准备好立即将任何给定建议付诸行动的可能性有多大? 生活以工作危机、孩子学校的紧急会议或意外感冒的形式介入的可能性有多大? 根据我的经验, 生活不断地推动和拉动我们远离我们的优先事项。我们越坚定地专注并完成某件事, 生活就越倾向于将紧急情况和延误抛在我们面前。

You’re watching a YouTube video on home renovation now, but that knowledge can be put to use only in a few months, when you move into your new place. You’re reading an article about time management techniques now, but they end up being most useful at the end of the year, when your new child is born and you suddenly have much greater demands on your time. You’re talking to a sales prospect about their goals and challenges now, but when you could really use that information is next year, when they start taking bids for a huge new contract.  
您现在正在观看有关家庭装修的 YouTube 视频, 但这些知识只能在您搬入新住所后的几个月内派上用场。你现在正在阅读一篇关于时间管理技巧的文章, 但它们最终在年底最有用, 那时你的新孩子出生了, 你突然对你的时间有了更多的要求。您现在正在与销售潜在客户谈论他们的目标和挑战, 但您真正可以使用这些信息的时间是明年, 那时他们将开始为一份巨大的新合同进行投标。

This holds true for so much of the ideas and inspiration around us. There is a key idea that catches our attention in the moment. We feel enraptured and obsessed with it. It’s difficult to imagine ever forgetting the new idea. It’s changed our lives forever! But after a few hours or days or weeks, it starts to fade from our memory. Soon our recollection of that exciting new idea is nothing but a pale shadow of something we once knew, that once intrigued us. Your job as a notetaker is to preserve the notes you’re taking on the things you discover in such a way that they can survive the journey into the future. That way your excitement and enthusiasm for your knowledge builds over time instead of fading away.  
这适用于我们周围的许多想法和灵感。有一个关键的想法立即引起了我们的注意。我们对此感到欣喜若狂和着迷。很难想象会忘记这个新想法。它永远改变了我们的生活! 但几个小时、几天或几周后, 它开始从我们的记忆中消失。很快, 我们对那个令人兴奋的新想法的回忆只不过是 something 我们曾经知道的、曾经让我们感兴趣的东西的苍白影子。作为记笔记者, 你的工作是保存你正在做的关于你发现的事情的笔记, 这样它们就可以在未来的旅程中幸存下来。这样, 你对知识的兴奋和热情会随着时间的推移而增强, 而不是消退。

### Discoverability—The Missing Link in Making Notes Useful  
可发现性——使笔记有用的缺失环节

The most important factor in whether your notes can survive that journey into the future is their _discoverability_—how easy it is to discover what they contain and access the specific points that are most immediately useful.  
决定你的笔记能否在未来的旅程中幸存下来的最重要因素是它们的可发现性——发现它们包含的内容并访问最直接有用的特定点是多么容易。

Discoverability is an idea from information science that refers to “the degree to which a piece of content or information can be found in a search of a file, database, or other information system.”[I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-020) Librarians think about discoverability when deciding how to lay out books on the shelves. Web designers think about it when they create menus for the websites you visit every day. Social media platforms work hard to make the best content on their platforms as discoverable as possible.  
可发现性是信息科学的一个概念, 指的是 “在文件、数据库或其他信息系统的搜索中可以找到一段内容或信息的程度”。 [I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-020) 图书馆员在决定如何在书架上布置书籍时会考虑可发现性。网页设计师在为您每天访问的网站创建菜单时会考虑到这一点。社交媒体平台努力使平台上最好的内容尽可能地被发现。

Discoverability is the element most often missing from people’s notes. It’s easy to save tons and tons of content, but turning it into a form that will be accessible in the future is another matter. To enhance the discoverability of your notes, we can turn to a simple habit you probably remember from school: highlighting the most important points. Highlighting is an activity that everyone understands, takes hardly any additional effort, and works in any app you might use.  
可发现性是人们笔记中最常缺少的元素。保存成吨的内容很容易, 但是将其变成将来可以访问的形式是另一回事。为了提高笔记的可发现性, 我们可以养成您可能在学校记得的一个简单习惯: 突出显示最重要的点。突出显示是一项每个人都能理解的活动, 几乎不需要任何额外的努力, 并且适用于您可能使用的任何应用程序。

Imagine your future self as a demanding customer. They will surely be impatient and very busy. They won’t have time to pore through page after page of details just to find the hidden gems. It’s your job to “sell” them on the value of the notes you are taking now. Your future self might have mere minutes before a meeting starts to quickly search their notes for a reference they need. In that sense, each note is like a product you are creating for the benefit of that future customer. If they don’t buy it—they don’t think it’s worth the effort of revisiting past notes—then all the value of the work you’re doing now will be lost.  
把你未来的自己想象成一个苛刻的客户。他们肯定会很不耐烦而且很忙。他们将没有时间仔细阅读 page 一页又一页的细节, 只是为了找到隐藏的宝石。你的工作是根据你现在所记笔记的价值 “出售” 它们。你未来的自己可能在会议开始前只有几分钟的时间快速搜索他们的笔记以寻找他们需要的参考。从这个意义上说, 每张纸条都像是您为未来客户的利益而创造的产品。如果他们不买账——他们认为重温过去的笔记不值得——那么你现在所做工作的所有价值都将丢失。

This points to a paradox that a lot of people experience as they take notes: the more notes they gather, the more the volume of information grows, the more time and effort it takes to review it all, and the less time they have to do so. Paradoxically, the more notes they collect, the less discoverable they become! This realization tends to either discourage them from taking any notes in the first place, or alternatively, to keep switching from one notetaking tool to another every time the volume gets overwhelming. Thus, they miss out on most of the benefits of their knowledge compounding over time.  
这指向了很多人在做笔记时都会经历的一个悖论: 他们收集的笔记越多, 信息量就越大, 复习所有内容所需的时间和精力就越多, 而他们要做的时间就越少所以。矛盾的是, 他们收集的笔记越多, 他们就越不容易被发现! 这种认识往往会阻止他们一开始就做任何笔记, 或者每次当音量变得不堪重负时, 他们就会不断地从一种笔记工具切换到另一种笔记工具。因此, 随着时间的推移, 他们错过了知识复合的大部分好处。

What do you do when communicating with a very busy, very impatient, very important person? You distill your message down to the key points and action steps. When you send an email to your boss, you don’t bury your request somewhere near the bottom of a massive wall of text. You identify the most urgent questions that you need them to respond to right at the top of the email. When you’re giving a presentation to the leadership of your organization, you don’t drone on for hours. You leave out unnecessary details and get right to the point.  
当你与一个非常忙碌、非常不耐烦、非常重要的人交流时, 你会怎么做? 您将信息提炼为关键点和行动步骤。当您向老板发送电子邮件时, 您不会将您的请求埋在大量文本墙底部附近的某个地方。您可以在电子邮件的顶部确定您需要他们回复的最紧迫的问题。当您向组织的领导层做演示时, 您不会一连几个小时都在喋喋不休。你省去了不必要的细节, 开门见山。

Distillation is at the very heart of all effective communication. The more important it is that your audience hear and take action on your message, the more distilled that message needs to be. The details and subtleties can come later once you have your audience’s attention.  
提炼是所有有效沟通的核心。您的听众听到您的信息并对其采取行动越重要, 该信息就越需要提炼。一旦你引起了观众的注意, 细节和微妙之处就会出现。

What if your future self was just as important as these VIPs? How could you communicate with them through time in the most efficient, concise way?  
如果你未来的自己和这些贵宾一样重要怎么办? 您如何以最有效、最简洁的方式与他们进行时空沟通?

### Highlighting 2.0: The Progressive Summarization Technique  
Highlighting 2.0: 渐进式摘要技术

Progressive Summarization is the technique I teach to distill notes down to their most important points. It is a simple process of taking the raw notes you’ve captured and organized and distilling them into usable material that can directly inform a current project.  
渐进式总结是我教授的一种技巧, 可以将笔记提炼成最重要的要点。这是一个简单的过程, 将您捕获和组织的原始笔记提炼成可以直接为当前项目提供信息的可用材料。

Progressive Summarization takes advantage of a tool and a habit that we are all intimately familiar with—highlighting—while leveraging the unique capabilities of technology to make those highlights far more useful than anything you did in school.  
Progressive Summarization 利用我们都非常熟悉的工具和习惯——突出显示——同时利用技术的独特功能使这些突出显示比你在学校所做的任何事情都更有用。

The technique is simple: you highlight the main points of a note, and then highlight the main points of _those_ highlights, and so on, distilling the essence of a note in several “layers.” Each of these layers uses a different kind of formatting so you can easily tell them apart.  
技巧很简单: 突出笔记的要点, 然后突出这些亮点的要点, 依此类推, 将笔记的本质提炼成几个 “层”。这些层中的每一层都使用不同类型的格式, 因此您可以轻松区分它们。

Here is a snapshot of the four layers of Progressive Summarization:[II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-019)  
这是四层渐进式总结的快照: [II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-019)

Here’s an example of a note I captured from an article in _Psychology Today_.[4](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn4) I came across the link when it was shared on social media and saved it with two clicks to my read later app, where I collect bookmarks of everything I want to read, watch, or listen to. A few evenings later, when I wanted to do some casual reading to wind down for the day, I read the article and highlighted the passages I found most interesting. I have my read later app synced to my digital notes app, so any passage I highlight there automatically gets saved in my notes, including a link to the source.  
这是我从《今日心理学》的一篇文章中截取的笔记示例。 [4](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn4) 当链接在社交媒体上共享时, 我偶然发现了该链接, 并通过单击两次将其保存到我的稍后阅读应用程序中, 我在其中收集了我想阅读、观看或收听的所有内容的书签。几个晚上后, 当我想做一些休闲阅读以放松一天时, 我阅读了这篇文章并突出显示了我觉得最有趣的段落。我将我的稍后阅读应用程序同步到我的数字笔记应用程序, 因此我在此处突出显示的任何段落都会自动保存在我的笔记中, 包括指向源的链接。

This is what I call “layer one”—the chunks of text initially captured in my notes. Notice that I didn’t save the entire article—only a few key excerpts.[III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-018) By limiting what I keep to only the best, most important, most relevant parts, I’m making all the subsequent steps of organizing, distilling, and expressing much easier. If I ever need to know the full details, I have the link to the original article right there at the bottom.  
这就是我所说的 “第一层”——最初在我的笔记中捕获的文本块。请注意, 我没有保存整篇文章——只保存了 几个关键摘录。 [III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-018) 通过将我保留的内容限制为最好、最重要、最相关的部分, 我使所有后续的组织、提炼和表达步骤变得更加容易。如果我需要了解全部细节, 我可以在底部找到原始文章的链接。

As interesting as this content is, it’s not nearly succinct enough. Once again, in the midst of a chaotic workday, I would be hard-pressed to find the time to casually look through multiple paragraphs of text to find the relevant points. Unless I highlight those points in a way that my future self can instantly grasp, I’ll likely never see them again.  
尽管此内容很有趣, 但还不够简洁。又一次, 在忙碌的工作日里, 我很难抽出时间随意翻阅多段文字, 找到相关的要点。除非我以未来的自己可以立即掌握的方式强调这些要点, 否则我可能再也见不到它们了。

To enhance the discoverability of this note, I need to add a second layer of distillation. I usually do this when I have free time during breaks or on evenings or weekends, when I come across the note while working on other projects, or when I don’t have the energy for more focused work. All I have to do is bold the main points within the note. This could include keywords that provide hints of what this text is about, phrases that capture what the original author was trying to say, or sentences that especially resonated with me even if I can’t explain why. Looking over the bolded parts of the same note below, can you see how much easier it is to quickly grasp the gist of this note by looking only at those parts?  
为了提高这篇笔记的可发现性, 我需要添加第二层蒸馏。我通常会在休息时间、晚上或周末有空闲时间时、在处理其他项目时偶然发现笔记时, 或者当我没有精力专注于更专注的工作时这样做。我所要做的就是将笔记中的要点加粗。这可能包括提供本文内容提示的关键字、捕捉原作者试图表达的内容的短语, 或者即使我无法解释原因也能引起我特别共鸣的句子。查看下面同一注释的粗体部分, 您能看出仅通过查看这些部分更容易快速掌握该注释的要点吗?

At layer two, this note is already dramatically more discoverable. Imagine the difference between reading the original article, which might take five to ten minutes of focused attention, versus glancing over these bolded points, which would take less than a minute.  
在第二层, 这个笔记已经非常容易被发现。想象一下阅读原文和浏览这些粗体点之间的区别, 前者可能需要集中注意力五到十分钟, 后者只需不到一分钟。

We’re not done yet! For those notes that are especially long, interesting, or valuable, it is sometimes worth adding a third layer of highlighting. I advise using the “highlighting” feature offered by most notes apps, which paints passages in bright yellow just like the fluorescent highlighters we used in school (which appear in light gray below). If your notes app doesn’t have a highlighting feature, you can use underlining or another kind of formatting instead. Look only at the bolded passages you identified in layer two and highlight only the most interesting and surprising of those points. This will often amount to just one or two sentences that encapsulate the message of the original source.  
我们还没有完成! 对于那些特别长、有趣或有价值的笔记, 有时值得添加第三层突出显示。我建议使用大多数笔记应用程序提供的 “突出显示” 功能, 该功能将段落涂成亮黄色, 就像我们在学校使用的荧光笔 (在下面显示为浅灰色) 一样。如果您的笔记应用程序没有突出显示功能, 您可以使用下划线或其他格式来代替。仅查看您在第二层中识别的粗体段落, 并仅突出显示这些要点中最有趣和最令人惊讶的部分。这通常只是一两句话来概括原始来源的信息。

Looking at the note above, can you see how those few highlighted sentences jump out and catch your eye? They convey the main message of this article in a highly distilled form that takes just seconds to grasp. When I come across this note in the future—while doing a search or browsing the notes within a folder—I’ll be able to decide in the blink of an eye whether this source is relevant to my needs. If it is, I’ll have all the additional details and context I need to remember it right in front of me, as well as the link to the original article to check the source.  
看看上面的注释, 你能看出那几个突出显示的句子是如何跳出来并抓住你的眼球的吗? 它们以高度提炼的形式传达了本文的主要信息, 只需几秒钟即可掌握。将来当我在搜索或浏览文件夹中的笔记时遇到这个笔记时, 我将能够在眨眼间决定这个来源是否与我的需要相关。如果是, 我将在我面前提供我需要记住的所有其他详细信息和上下文, 以及原始文章的链接以检查来源。

There is one more layer we can add, though it is quite rarely needed. For only the very few sources that are truly unique and valuable, I’ll add an “executive summary” at the top of the note with a few bullet points summarizing the article in my own words. The best sign that a fourth layer is needed is when I find myself visiting a note again and again, clearly indicating that it is one of the cornerstones of my thinking. Looking only at the points I’ve previously bolded and highlighted in layers two and three makes it far easier to write this summary than if I was trying to summarize the entire article all at once.  
我们可以再添加一层, 尽管很少需要它。对于真正独特且有价值的极少数资源, 我将在注释顶部添加一个 “执行摘要”, 其中包含一些要点, 用我自己的话对文章进行总结。需要第四层的最好迹象是当我发现自己一次又一次地访问一个笔记时, 清楚地表明它是我思考的基石之一。 只看我之前在第二层和第三层中加粗和突出显示的点, 这比我试图一次总结整篇文章更容易写这个总结。

I recommend using bullet points to encourage yourself to make this executive summary succinct. Use your own words, define any unusual terms you’re using, and think about how your future self, who may not remember anything about this source, might interpret what you’re writing.  
我建议使用要点来鼓励自己使这份执行摘要简洁明了。用你自己的话, 定义你正在使用的任何不寻常的术语, 并思考你未来的自己, 可能不记得这个来源的任何事情, 可能会如何解释你正在写的东西。

By reviewing this executive summary, I can rapidly recall the main takeaways from this article in a fraction of the time it would take to reread the original. Since the takeaways are already in my own words, they are easy to incorporate into whatever I’m working on. Speed is everything when it comes to recall: you have only a limited amount of time and energy, and the faster you can move through your notes, the more diverse and interesting ideas you can connect together.  
通过回顾这份执行摘要, 我可以快速回忆起这篇文章的主要内容, 而所花的时间只是重新阅读原文所花时间的一小部分。由于要点已经用我自己的话表达出来, 因此很容易将它们融入到我正在做的任何事情中。谈到回忆, 速度就是一切: 你只有有限的时间和精力, 你翻阅笔记的速度越快, 你就能将越多、越有趣的想法联系在一起。

### Zooming In and Out of Your Map of Knowledge  
放大和缩小你的知识地图

The layers of Progressive Summarization give you multiple ways of interacting with your notes depending on the needs of the moment. The first time you read about a new idea, you might want to dive into the details and explore every nuance. The next time you revisit that idea, you probably don’t want to repeat all that effort and read the same piece from beginning to end again. You want to pick up where you left off, looking only at the highlights left over from the last time you visited that note. You can review all the details at layer one, or if you’re pressed for time (and when are we ever not pressed for time?), just focus on layers two, three, or four. You can _customize_ how much attention you spend on a note based on your energy level and time available.  
渐进式总结的层次为您提供了多种方式来根据当时的需要与您的笔记进行交互。第一次阅读新想法时, 您可能想要深入了解细节并探索每一个细微差别。下次你重新审视这个想法时, 你可能不想重复所有的努力并再次从头到尾阅读同一篇文章。您想从上次中断的地方继续, 只查看上次访问该笔记时留下的亮点。您可以查看第一层的所有细节, 或者如果您时间紧迫 (我们什么时候没有时间紧迫?), 只需关注第二层、第三层或第四层。您可以根据您的能量水平和可用时间自定义您在笔记上花费的注意力。

It’s like having a digital map of your notes that can be zoomed in or out depending on how many details you want to see, like a maps app on your smartphone. Navigating to a new destination, you might want to zoom in and see exactly which driveway to turn into. On the other hand, if you’re planning a cross-country road trip, you might want to zoom out and see your entire itinerary in one glance. The same is true for your landscape of knowledge—sometimes you want to zoom in and examine one specific research finding, while other times you want to zoom out and see the broad sweep of an argument all at once.  
这就像你的笔记的数字地图, 可以根据你想看到多少细节来放大或缩小, 就像你智能手机上的地图应用程序一样。导航到新目的地时, 您可能想要放大并准确查看要转入的车道。另一方面, 如果您计划进行一次越野公路旅行, 您可能希望缩小并一眼看清您的整个行程。对于你的知识图景也是如此——有时你想放大并检查一个特定的研究发现, 而 其他时候你想缩小并同时看到一个论点的广泛范围。

With Progressive Summarization, you are building up a map of the best ideas found in your Second Brain. Your highlights are like signposts and waypoints that help you navigate through the network of ideas you’re exploring. You are building this map without moving anything or deleting anything. Every sentence gets left right where you found it, giving you the freedom to leave things out without worrying that you’ll lose them. With this map in hand, you can actually see what you’ve captured, helping you find what you’re looking for but also what you don’t even know you’re looking for.  
借助渐进式总结, 您正在构建第二大脑中发现的最佳想法的地图。你的亮点就像路标和路标, 可以帮助你在你正在探索的想法网络中导航。您正在构建此地图而无需移动或删除任何内容。每句话都在您找到它的地方, 让您可以自由地省略一些东西, 而不必担心会丢失它们。有了这张地图, 您实际上可以看到您捕获的内容, 帮助您找到您正在寻找的内容以及您甚至不知道自己在寻找的内容。

Highlighting can sometimes feel risky. You may wonder, “Am I making the right decision about which points are most important, or what this source means?” The multiple layers of Progressive Summarization are like a safety net; if you go in the wrong direction, or make a mistake, you can always just go back to the original version and try again. Nothing gets forgotten or deleted.  
突出显示有时会让人觉得有风险。您可能会想,“关于哪些点最重要, 或者这个来源意味着什么, 我做出的决定是否正确?” Progressive Summarization 的多层就像一个安全网; 如果你走错了方向, 或者犯了错误, 你总是可以回到原来的版本再试一次。什么都不会被遗忘或删除。

Progressive Summarization helps you focus on the _content_ and the _presentation_ of your notes,[IV](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-017) instead of spending too much time on labeling, tagging, linking, or other advanced features offered by many information management tools. It gives you a practical, easy thing to do that adds value even when you don’t have the energy for more challenging tasks. Most importantly, it keeps your attention on the substance of what you’re reading or learning, which is what matters in the long term.  
渐进式摘要帮助您专注于笔记的内容和呈现方式, [IV](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-017) 而不是花费太多时间在标签、标记、链接或许多信息管理工具提供的其他高级功能上。它为您提供了一件实用、简单的事情, 即使您没有精力完成更具挑战性的任务, 也能增加价值。最重要的是, 它让你的注意力集中在你正在阅读或学习的内容上, 从长远来看, 这才是最重要的。

### Four Examples of Progressive Summarization  
渐进式总结的四个例子

Progressive Summarization can be used across a wide variety of different kinds of content. As long as a source can be turned into text,[V](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-016) you can add layers of highlighting in any information management tool you use.  
渐进式摘要可用于各种不同类型的内容。只要源代码可以转换为文本, [V](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-016) 你就可以在你使用的任何信息管理工具中添加高亮层。

Let’s look at more examples of progressively summarized notes:  
让我们看看更多渐进式总结笔记的例子:

#### Wikipedia Articles 维基百科文章

Have you ever found yourself visiting the same Wikipedia article again and again or trying to remember something from that one article you read weeks ago?  
您是否发现自己一次又一次地访问同一篇维基百科文章, 或者试图记住您几周前阅读的那篇文章中的内容?

By saving the best excerpts from Wikipedia articles you read, you can create your own private encyclopedia with only the parts that are most relevant to you. In the note below, I captured a few key sentences from the article explaining “Baumol’s Cost Disease,” a somewhat esoteric term from economics that I’d seen referenced a few times.  
通过保存您阅读过的维基百科文章的最佳摘录, 您可以创建自己的私人百科全书, 只包含与您最相关的部分。在下面的注释中, 我摘录了解释 “鲍莫尔成本病” 的文章中的几个关键句子, 这是一个经济学中有点深奥的术语, 我曾多次看到它被引用过。

When I first captured the note, I didn’t have time to add tags, highlights, or an executive summary of my own. I saved it to a resource folder for “Economics” to revisit later. A few months later, when it came up in a search for “wages,” I took a few moments to bold a couple of key sentences and highlight the most important one, so I could get the gist of it with a glance.  
当我第一次记录笔记时, 我没有时间添加标签、亮点或我自己的执行摘要。我将它保存到 “经济学” 的资源文件夹中, 以便稍后重新访问。几个月后, 当它出现在搜索 “工资” 时, 我花了一些时间将 几个关键句子加粗并突出显示最重要的句子, 这样我一眼就能明白它的要点.

I was once on a panel when one of the speakers mentioned this term. Within the ten seconds before it was my turn to respond, I was able to run a search, look up this note on my tablet (where all my notes are synchronized), and speak confidently on the subject as if I had known it all along.  
当一位发言人提到这个词时, 我曾经在一个小组中。在轮到我回答之前的十秒内, 我能够进行搜索, 在我的平板电脑上查找这条笔记 (我所有的笔记都同步了), 然后自信地谈论这个话题, 就好像我一直都知道一样.

#### Online Articles 在线文章

Much of the time we consume information without a specific purpose in mind. We might peruse the newspaper over breakfast, listen to a podcast while working out, or check out a newsletter to casually learn about a topic. We consume information to stay up to date, pass the time, entertain ourselves, and keep our minds engaged.  
很多时候, 我们在没有特定目的的情况下消费信息。我们可能会在早餐时仔细阅读报纸, 在锻炼时收听播客, 或者查看时事通讯以随意了解某个主题。我们消费信息是为了了解最新信息、打发时间、娱乐自己并保持专注。

These moments are some of the most valuable opportunities to capture tidbits of insight that you probably might not find otherwise. Because this casual reading and listening tends to range over a wide number of topics and interests, you are exposed to more diverse ideas than usual.  
这些时刻是捕捉您可能无法通过其他方式获得的洞察力的一些最宝贵的机会。因为这种随意的阅读和聆听往往涉及广泛的主题和兴趣, 所以你会比平时接触到更多不同的想法。

One evening I was reading an online article that I saw shared on social media. The article explained how Google used “structured interviews” as part of their hiring process to reduce bias, ensure consistency, and learn from past hires. I was a solo freelancer at the time and had no immediate use for knowledge about hiring practices. I knew that someday I might, so I decided to save the paragraph you see below to my Second Brain.  
一天晚上, 我正在阅读一篇在社交媒体上分享的在线文章。这篇文章解释了谷歌如何在招聘过程中使用 “结构化 interviews” 来减少偏见、确保一致性并从过去的招聘中学习。当时我是一名独立的自由职业者, 并没有立即使用有关招聘实践的知识。我知道有一天我会的, 所以我决定把你在下面看到的这段话保存到我的第二大脑中。

Almost two years later, I was finally ready to hire my first employee. I remember the feeling of anxiety as I prepared to take on this major financial commitment, not to mention the responsibility of managing a direct report. Luckily, I had a handful of highly actionable notes saved in a resource folder called “Hiring.” To get started, I moved the entire folder from resources to projects. Then I spent about thirty minutes to review the notes it contained and highlight the most relevant takeaways. Those highlights were the starting point for the hiring process I ended up using for my own business, inspired by one of the most innovative and desirable employers in the world.  
差不多两年后, 我终于准备好雇用我的第一位员工了。我记得当我准备承担这项重大财务承诺时的焦虑感, 更不用说管理直接下属的责任了。幸运的是, 我在名为 “招聘” 的资源文件夹中保存了一些高度可操作的笔记。首先, 我将整个文件夹从资源移动到项目。然后我花了大约三十分钟来回顾它包含的注释并突出显示最相关的要点。这些亮点是我最终用于自己企业的招聘流程的起点, 灵感来自世界上最具创新性和最理想的雇主之一。

#### Podcasts and Audio 播客和音频

Notes can come in handy even when you’re not able to write them down in real time. I was driving with my wife one weekend to a small Airbnb cabin in the Sierra Nevada mountains of California, and we decided to listen to a podcast. It was a casual conversation between the host and a course instructor named Meghan Telpner, who ran an online school called the Academy of Culinary Nutrition.[5](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn5)  
即使您无法实时记下笔记, 笔记也能派上用场。一个周末, 我和我的妻子开车去加利福尼亚州内华达山脉的 小型 Airbnb 小屋, 我们决定收听播客。这是主持人与一位名叫梅根 · 特尔普纳 (Meghan Telpner) 的课程讲师之间的随意对话, 他经营着一家名为烹饪营养学院的在线学校。 [5](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn5)

I had never heard of her and put on the episode without any particular goal in mind. Over the next hour, as we ascended the steep mountainside roads, we were captivated by the story of the education business she had managed to build. She had faced so many of the same challenges we had. It was a relief to hear that we weren’t alone in our struggles. I was driving and unable to write anything down, but as soon as we arrived I sat in the car for a few minutes and captured the ideas I remembered. This is actually a great way to filter down the volume of notes you’re taking—the best stuff always sticks in your mind for an hour or two.  
我从来没有听说过她, 并且在没有任何特定目标的情况下穿上了这一集。在接下来的一个小时里, 当我们登上陡峭的山腰道路时, 我们被她成功建立的教育事业的故事所吸引。她曾面临过许多与我们相同的挑战。听到我们并不孤单, 这让我松了一口气。我当时在开车, 无法写下任何东西, 但我们一到, 我就在车里坐了几分钟, 记下了我记得的想法。这实际上是减少笔记数量的好方法——最好的东西总是会在你的脑海中停留一两个小时。

A few months later, we were preparing a launch campaign for a new version of our online course. I had only a couple of weeks to prepare for it—definitely not enough time to do more research. I had to make use of the ideas I’d already collected. As part of my preparation, I went through this note (which I found within an area folder for “Online education”) and bolded the parts that most resonated with me. Then just before our launch kickoff I highlighted the parts I wanted to apply to our own situation. The highlighted passages you see here were the sparks that eventually led to us hiring alumni of the course to coach new students. This freed up my time to implement another idea from Telpner’s interview: adding a new “executive” coaching tier. You truly never know where inspiration will come from and the extraordinary impact it can have.  
几个月后, 我们正在为新版在线课程准备启动活动。我只有几周的准备时间——绝对没有足够的时间做更多的研究。我必须利用我已经收集的想法。作为我准备工作的一部分, 我仔细阅读了这篇笔记 (我在“在线教育” 的区域文件夹中找到了它), 并将最能引起我共鸣的部分加粗。然后就在我们的发布开始之前, 我突出显示了我想应用到我们自己的情况的部分。您在此处看到的突出显示的段落是最终导致我们聘请该课程的校友来指导新学生的火花。这让我腾出了时间来实施 Telpner 采访中的另一个想法: 增加一个新的 “执行” 教练层级。你真的永远不知道灵感会从何而来, 也不知道它会产生非凡的影响。

Like many people, I spend a fair percentage of my time on phone calls and in meetings. I want to make the best use of that time, so I take notes during most meetings of new ideas, suggestions, feedback, and action steps that come up.  
和许多人一样, 我将相当一部分时间花在打电话和开会上。我想充分利用这段时间, 所以我会在大多数会议上记录新想法、建议、反馈和出现的行动步骤。

Taking notes during meetings is a common practice, but it’s often not clear what we should do with those notes. They are often messy, with the action items buried among random comments. I often use Progressive Summarization to summarize my notes after phone calls to make sure I’m extracting every bit of value from them.  
在会议期间做笔记是一种常见的做法, 但通常不清楚我们应该如何处理这些笔记。它们通常是杂乱无章的, 行动项目隐藏在随意的评论中。我经常在通话后使用渐进式总结来总结我的笔记, 以确保我从中提取了每一点价值。

I captured this note during a conversation with a friend of mine who has experience designing recording studios. We were remodeling our garage into a home studio and I wanted to get his advice. He was kind enough to come over and walk me through his recommendations, and I wrote down the main points in a notes app on my smartphone as he spoke.  
我在与一位有录音室设计经验的朋友的谈话中记下了这个笔记。我们正在将我们的车库改造成一个家庭工作室, 我想听听他的建议。他很友好地走过来向我介绍他的建议, 我在他说话的同时在智能手机上的笔记应用程序中记下了要点。

Sometime later, I happened to be driving by the local hardware store on my way home. I realized that I could pop in and get some of the supplies my friend had recommended. I took out my smartphone, did a search for “home studio,” and found this note. I took a few minutes while sitting in the car and bolded the items I would need to purchase at some point, which were buried among other suggestions he had made.  
一段时间后, 我在回家的路上碰巧路过当地的五金店。我意识到我可以进去买一些我朋友推荐的用品。我拿出智能手机, 搜索 “家庭工作室”, 找到了这张便条。我坐在车里花了几分钟时间, 将我需要在某个时候购买的物品加粗, 这些物品被他提出的其他建议所掩盖。

Here’s what it looked like: 这是它的样子:

I then copied and pasted only the bolded items I was ready to purchase into a separate list below my original notes, and suddenly I had a convenient shopping list I could easily reference while browsing the store.  
然后我只复制并粘贴我准备购买的粗体项目到我原始笔记下方的单独列表中, 突然我有了一个方便的购物清单, 我可以在浏览商店时轻松参考。

This example illustrates how even Progressively Summarizing notes from our own conversations can be immensely useful. Often your own thoughts need some distillation before you can take action on them.  
这个例子说明了从我们自己的对话中逐步总结笔记是如何非常有用的。在您对它们采取行动之前, 您自己的想法通常需要一些提炼。

### Picasso’s Secret: Prune the Good to Surface the Great  
毕加索的秘密: 剔除美好, 浮现伟大

We can look at masters of creativity throughout history to see how distilling their ideas shaped their work.  
我们可以看看历史上的创造力大师, 看看他们的想法如何塑造他们的作品。

One of Pablo Picasso’s most famous drawings, created in 1945 and known as _Picasso’s Bull_, offers a master class in how distillation works. It is a sequence of images that he drew to study a bull’s essential form. The process of distillation happens in every art form, but this example is unusual in that Picasso preserved each step of his process.  
巴勃罗 · 毕加索 (Pablo Picasso) 最著名的画作之一, 创作于 1945 年, 被称为毕加索的公牛 (Picasso's Bull), 提供有关蒸馏工作原理的大师班。 这是他为研究公牛的基本形态而画的一系列图像。蒸馏的过程发生在每一种艺术形式中, 但这个例子是不寻常的, 因为毕加索保留了他的过程的每一步。

Pablo Picasso, _Le Taureau_ (series of 11 lithographs), 1945–46 (© 2021 Estate of Pablo Picasso / Artists Rights Society (ARS), New York).  
巴勃罗毕加索, Le Taureau(系列 11 幅石版画),1945–46(© 2021 Estate of Pablo Picasso / Artists Rights Society (ARS), 纽约)。

Starting with the top left image and moving across and down, Picasso deconstructed the shapes of the bull one step at a time. In the first couple of drawings, he adds more detail. The horns are fuller, the tail becomes three-dimensional, and the hide has more depth and texture. Picasso is starting by building up detail so that he has more options to choose from when it comes time to take some away.  
从左上角的图像开始, 毕加索一步一步地解构公牛的形状。在前几幅图中, 他添加了更多细节。牛角更饱满, 尾巴变得立体, 皮革更有深度和 纹理。毕加索从建立细节开始, 这样当他需要拿走一些东西时, 他就有更多的选择。

The process of distillation begins with the fourth image. He outlines the main muscles of the animal using sharp white lines. Soft curves become more angular, and the animal as a whole starts to take on a more geometric look. In the fifth and sixth images, the drawing starts to become radically simplified as Picasso drops most of the detail in the bull’s head and further simplifies its horns, tail, and legs. A thick white line representing the bull’s center of gravity is added, cutting across the animal from front to back.  
蒸馏过程从第四张图片开始。他用清晰的白线勾勒出动物的主要肌肉。柔和的曲线变得更有棱角, 整个动物开始呈现出更几何的外观。在第五张和第六张图片中, 由于毕加索放弃了公牛头部的大部分细节, 并进一步简化了它的角、尾巴和腿, 这幅画开始变得彻底简化。添加了一条代表公牛重心的粗白线, 从前到后横切该动物。

By the last few images, the bull has become nothing more than an interconnected series of simple, black-and-white shapes. The legs have become single lines. Solid blocks of color define the front and back of the animal. In the final drawings, even those details are abstracted away. We end with a drawing that is nothing but a single, continuous stroke, which somehow still manages to capture the very essence of the bull.[6](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn6)  
通过最后几张图片, 公牛只不过是一系列相互关联的简单黑白形状。腿变成了单线。实心色块定义了动物的正面和背面。在最终的图纸中, 甚至那些细节都被抽象掉了。我们以一幅画作结尾, 它只不过是一个单一的、连续的笔画, 不知怎的, 它仍然设法抓住了公牛的本质。 [6](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch06fn6)

Picasso’s act of distillation involves stripping away the unnecessary so that only the essential remains. Crucially, Picasso couldn’t have started with the single line drawing. He needed to go through each layer of the bull’s form step-by-step to absorb the proportions and shapes into his muscle memory. The result points to a mysterious aspect of the creative process: it can end up with a result that looks so simple, it seems like anyone could have made it. That simplicity masks the effort that was needed to get there.  
毕加索的提炼行为包括去除不必要的东西, 只留下最重要的东西。至关重要的是, 毕加索不可能从单线画开始。他需要一步步经历公牛形态的每一层, 将比例和形状吸收到他的肌肉记忆中。结果指向了创作过程的一个神秘方面: 它最终可能会得到一个看起来如此简单的结果, 似乎任何人都可以做到。这种简单掩盖了到达那里所需的努力。

Another example comes from documentary filmmaking. Ken Burns, the renowned creator of award-winning films like _The Civil War_, _Baseball_, and _Jazz_, has said that only a tiny percentage of the raw footage he captures eventually makes it into the final cut. This ratio can be as high as 40- or 50-to-1, which means that for every forty to fifty hours of footage he captures, only one hour makes it into the final film. Along the way, Burns and his team are performing a radical act of distillation—finding the most interesting, surprising, moving moments hidden amidst hundreds of hours of recordings.[VI](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-015)  
另一个例子来自纪录片制作。肯 · 伯恩斯 (Ken Burns) 是《内战》、《棒球》和《爵士乐》等获奖影片的著名创作者, 他曾表示, 他拍摄的原始镜头中只有一小部分最终会进入最终剪辑。这个比例可以高达 40 比 1 或 50 比 1, 这意味着对于他拍摄的每 40 到 50 小时的镜头, 只有一个小时进入了​​ final 电影。在此过程中, 伯恩斯和他的团队正在进行一项激进的提炼行动——找到隐藏在数百小时录音中的最有趣、最令人惊讶、最感人的时刻。 [VI](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-015)

Progressive Summarization is not a method for remembering as much as possible—it is a method for forgetting as much as possible. As you distill your ideas, they naturally improve, because when you drop the merely good parts, the great parts can shine more brightly. To be clear, it takes skill and courage to let the details fall away. As Picasso’s bull and Burns’s documentaries illustrate, in making decisions about what to keep, we inevitably have to make decisions about what to throw away. You cannot highlight the main takeaways from an article without leaving some points out. You cannot make a highlight reel of a video without cutting some of the footage. You cannot give an effective presentation without leaving out some slides.  
渐进式总结不是一种尽可能多地记住的方法——它是一种尽可能多地忘记的方法。当你提炼你的想法时, 它们自然会得到改善, 因为当你放下仅仅好的部分时, 伟大的部分会更加闪耀。需要明确的是, 让细节消失需要技巧和勇气。正如毕加索的公牛和伯恩斯的纪录片所说明的那样, 在决定保留什么的同时, 我们不可避免地要决定扔掉什么。你不能在不遗漏一些要点的情况下突出文章的主要内容。如果不剪切某些素材, 就无法制作视频的精彩片段。如果不遗漏一些幻灯片, 就无法进行有效的演示。

### The Three Most Common Mistakes of Novice Notetakers  
记笔记新手最常犯的三个错误

Here are a few guidelines to help you avoid common pitfalls as you embark on highlighting your own notes.  
以下是一些指南, 可帮助您在着手突出显示自己的笔记时避免常见的陷阱。

#### Mistake #1: Over-Highlighting 错误 #1: 过度突出显示

The biggest mistake people make when they start to distill their notes is that they highlight way too much. You may have experienced this pitfall in school, highlighting paragraph after paragraph or entire pages of textbooks in the vain hope that you’d automatically be able to remember everything in yellow for the test.  
人们在开始提炼笔记时犯的最大错误是他们突出了太多。您可能在学校遇到过这种陷阱, 逐段或整个 pages 教科书突出显示, 徒劳地希望您能够自动记住黄色的所有内容以进行测试。

When it comes to notetaking for work, less is more. You can capture entire books, articles with dozens of pages, or social media posts by the hundreds. No one will stop you, but you’ll quickly learn that such volume will only create a lot more work later on when you have to figure out what all that information means. If you’re going to capture everything, you might as well capture nothing.  
说到工作笔记, 少即是多。您可以捕获整本书、数十页的文章或数百个社交媒体帖子。没有人会阻止你, 但你会很快了解到, 当你必须弄清楚所有这些信息的含义时, 这样的数量只会在以后产生更多的工作。如果您要捕获所有内容, 则还不如什么都不捕获。

Remember that notes are not authoritative texts. You don’t need to and shouldn’t include every tiny detail. They are more like bookmarks peeking out from the pages of a book on the shelf, signaling to you, “Hey! There’s something interesting here!” You will always be able to go back and review the full, original source if needed. Your notes only solve the problem of rediscovering those sources when you need them.  
请记住, 注释不是权威文本。你不需要也不应该包括每一个微小的细节。它们更像是书签, 从书架上的书页中探出头来, 向你发出信号,“嘿! 这里有什么有趣的东西!” 如果需要, 您将始终能够返回并查看完整的原始来源。您的笔记只解决了在您需要时重新发现这些资源的问题。

A helpful rule of thumb is that each layer of highlighting should include no more than 10–20 percent of the previous layer. If you save a series of excerpts from a book amounting to five hundred words, the bolded second layer should include no more than one hundred words, and highlighted third layer no more than twenty. This isn’t an exact science, but if you find yourself highlighting everything, this rule should give you pause.  
一个有用的经验法则是, 每一层高亮显示不应超过前一层的 10-20%。如果你保存一本书的一系列节选, 总计五百字, 加粗的第二层不应超过一百字, 突出显示的第三层不应超过二十字。这不是一门精确的科学, 但如果你发现自己要突出显示所有内容, 这条规则应该会让你停下来。

#### Mistake #2: Highlighting Without a Purpose in Mind  
错误 #2: 没有目的的突出显示

The most common question I hear about Progressive Summarization is “When should I be doing this highlighting?” The answer is that you should do it _when you’re getting ready to create something._  
我听到的关于渐进式总结的最常见问题是 “我应该什么时候进行突出显示?” 答案是当你准备好创造一些东西时你应该这样做。

Unlike Capture and Organize, which take mere seconds, it takes time and effort to distill your notes. If you try to do it with every note up front, you’ll quickly be mired in hours of meticulous highlighting with no clear purpose in mind. You can’t afford such a giant investment of time without knowing whether it will pay off.  
与只需几秒钟的捕获和整理不同, 提炼您的笔记需要时间和精力。如果您尝试在前面的每个音符 上都这样做, 您很快就会陷入数小时的细致突出显示而没有明确的目的。在不知道是否会得到回报的情况下, 您无法承担如此巨大的时间投资。

Instead, wait until you know how you’ll put the note to use. For example, when I’m preparing to write a blog post or article, I’ll usually start by highlighting the most interesting points from a group of notes that I think will be relevant to the topic at hand. That way I have a predictable, not-too-difficult task to get me warmed up for writing, the same way an athlete might have a warm-up and stretching routine.  
相反, 等到你知道如何使用笔记。例如, 当我准备写一篇博文或文章时, 我通​​常会从我认为与手头主题相关的一组笔记中突出显示最有趣的点。这样我就有了一个可预测的、不太困难的任务来让我为写作热身, 就像运动员可能有热身和伸展运动一样。

When I’m about to get on a call with my lawyer, I’ll often prepare by highlighting my notes from our last call and drawing out decision points and action items into an agenda. He always thinks I’m well prepared, when in fact I just want to finish the call quickly to minimize the time I’m being billed for!  
当我要与我的律师通话时, 我通常会通过突出显示上次通话中的笔记并将决策点和行动项目绘制到议程中来做好准备。他总是认为我准备充分, 而实际上我只是想尽快结束通话, 以尽量减少我被计费的时间!

You have to always assume that, until proven otherwise, any given note won’t necessarily ever be useful. You have no idea what your future self will need, want, or be working on. This assumption forces you to be conservative in the time you spend summarizing notes, doing so only when it’s virtually guaranteed that it will be worth it.  
您必须始终假设, 除非另有证明, 否则任何给定的注释都不一定有用。你不知道未来的自己需要什么、想要什么或正在从事什么。这种假设迫使你在花时间总结笔记时要保守, 只有在几乎可以保证这样做是值得的时候才这样做。

The rule of thumb to follow is that every time you “touch” a note, you should make it a little more discoverable for your future self[VII](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-014)—by adding a highlight, a heading, some bullets, or commentary. This is the “campsite rule” applied to information—leave it better than you found it. This ensures that the notes you interact with most often will naturally become the most discoverable in a virtuous cycle.  
要遵循的经验法则是, 每次你 “触摸” 一个笔记时, 你应该让你未来的自己 [VII](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-014) 更容易发现它——通过添加高亮、标题、一些项目符号或评论。这是适用于信息的 “营地规则”——留下它比你 找到它更好。这确保了您最常交互的笔记自然会成为良性循环中最容易被发现的笔记。

#### Mistake #3: Making Highlighting Difficult  
错误 #3: 难以突出显示

Don’t worry about analyzing, interpreting, or categorizing each point to decide whether to highlight it. That is way too taxing and will break the flow of your concentration. Instead, rely on your intuition to tell you when a passage is interesting, counterintuitive, or relevant to your favorite problems or a current project.  
不要担心分析、解释或分类每个点来决定是否突出显示它。那太费力了, 会打断你的注意力。相反, 当一段话有趣、违反直觉或与你最喜欢的问题或当前项目相关时, 依靠你的直觉来告诉你。

Just as you listened for a feeling of internal resonance in deciding what content to save in the first place, the same rule applies for the insights _within_ the note. Certain passages will move you, pique your attention, make your heart beat faster, or provoke you. Those are clear signals that you’ve found something important, and it’s time to add a highlight. You can apply the same criteria I introduced earlier in Chapter 4, looking out for individual points that are surprising, useful, inspiring, or personal to decide which ones are worth highlighting.  
正如您在决定首先保存哪些内容时聆听内部共鸣的感觉一样, 同样的规则适用于笔记中的见解。某些段落会打动您、引起您的注意、让您心跳加速或激怒您。这些都是您发现重要内容的明确信号, 是时候添加亮点了。您可以应用我在第 4 章前面介绍的相同标准, 寻找令人惊讶、有用、鼓舞人心或个人的个人要点, 以决定哪些值得强调。

When you learn the art of distillation, you will gain a lifelong skill that will impact every area of your life. Think of a storyteller who captivates you with every word. Their story is well distilled, with unnecessary details stripped away. Think about the last time you were entranced by a drawing or painting. Its ability to grab you immediately is a sign that the concept behind the artwork is compressed into its most compact form, allowing it to travel efficiently from the canvas straight into your brain.  
当您学习蒸馏的艺术时, 您将获得终生受益的技能, 它将影响您生活的方方面面。想想一个讲故事的人, 他的每一个字都让你着迷。他们的故事提炼得很好, 去掉了不必要的细节。回想一下您上一次被一幅图画或绘画所吸引的情景。它能够立即吸引您, 这表明艺术品背后的概念被压缩成最紧凑的形式, 使其能够从画布上高效地直接进入您的大脑。

Even in our daily conversations, the ability to be succinct without missing key details is what leads to exciting conversations that leave both people feeling enlivened. Distillation is at the heart of the communication that is so central to our friendships, our working relationships, and our leadership abilities. Notetaking gives you a way to deliberately practice the skill of distilling every day.  
即使在我们的日常对话中, 能够在不遗漏关键细节的情况下保持简洁, 也会导致令人兴奋的对话, 让双方都感到活跃。提炼是沟通的核心, 这对我们的友谊、我们的工作 关系和我们的领导能力至关重要。做笔记让你有意识地练习每天提炼的技巧。

### Your Turn: Keep Your Future Self in Mind  
轮到你了: 牢记未来的自己

The effort we put into Progressive Summarization is meant for one purpose: to make it easy to find and work with our notes in the future.  
我们在 Progressive Summarization 上的努力是为了一个目的: 让我们的笔记在未来更容易找到和使用。

More is not better when it comes to thinking and creating. Distilling makes our ideas small and compact, so we can load them up into our minds with minimal effort. If you can’t locate a piece of information quickly, in a format that’s convenient and ready to be put to use, then you might as well not have it at all. Our most scarce resource is time, which means we need to prioritize our ability to quickly rediscover the ideas that we already have in our Second Brain.  
在思考和创造方面, 并不是越多越好。提炼使我们的想法变得小而紧凑, 因此我们可以毫不费力地将它们加载到我们的脑海中。如果您不能以一种方便且随时可用的格式快速找到一条信息, 那么您还不如根本没有它。我们最稀缺的资源是时间, 这意味着我们需要优先考虑快速重新发现第二大脑中已有想法的能力。

When the opportunity arrives to do our best work, it’s not the time to start reading books and doing research. You need that research to already be done.[VIII](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-013) You can prepare in advance for the future challenges and opportunities you don’t even yet know you’ll face, by taking advantage of the effort you’re already spending reading books, learning new things, and simply being curious about the world around you.  
当我们有机会做到最好时, 不是开始读书和做研究的时候。您需要已经完成该研究。 [VIII](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-013) 你可以提前为你甚至还不知道你将面临的未来挑战和机遇做好准备, 方法是利用你已经花在阅读书籍上的努力, 学习新事物, 并且只是对你周围的世界。

To put what you’ve just learned into practice immediately, find an interesting piece of content you consumed recently, such as an article, audiobook, or YouTube video. This could be content you’ve captured already and organized in one of your PARA folders. Or it could be a new piece of content floating around your email inbox or in a read later app.  
要立即将刚刚学到的知识付诸实践, 请找到最近消费的有趣内容, 例如文章、有声读物或 YouTube 视频。这可能是您已经捕获并组织在您的某个 PARA 文件夹中的内容。或者它可能是漂浮在您的电子邮件收件箱或稍后阅读应用程序中的新内容。

Start by saving only the best excerpts from that piece of content in a new note, either using copy-paste or a capture tool. This is layer one, the initial excerpts you save in your Second Brain. Next, read through the excerpts, bolding the main points and most important takeaways. Don’t make it an analytical decision—listen for a feeling of resonance and let that be your guide for what to bold. These bolded passages are layer two.  
首先, 使用复制粘贴或捕捉工具, 仅在新笔记中保存该内容的最佳摘录。这是第一层, 您保存在第二大脑中的初始摘录。接下来, 通读摘录, 将要点和最重要的要点加粗。不要让它成为一个分析性的决定——倾听一种共鸣的感觉, 让它成为你大胆尝试的指南。这些加粗的段落是第二层。

Now read through only the bolded passages, and highlight (or, if your notes app doesn’t have a highlighting feature, underline) the best of the best passages. The key here is to be very picky: the entire note may have only a few highlighted sentences, or even just one. Not only is that fine, it represents a highly distilled and discoverable note. These highlights are layer three, which is distilled enough for most use cases.  
现在只通读粗体段落, 并突出显示 (或者, 如果您的笔记应用程序没有突出显示功能, 则在下划线) 最好的段落中的最好的。这里的关键是要非常挑剔: 整个笔记可能只有几个突出显示的句子, 甚至只有一个。不仅如此, 它还代表了一种高度提炼和可发现的音符。这些亮点是第三层, 对于大多数用例来说已经足够了。

The true test of whether a note you’ve created is discoverable is whether you can get the gist of it at a glance. Put it aside for a few days and set a reminder to revisit it once you’ve forgotten most of the details. When you come back to it, give yourself no more than thirty seconds and see if you can rapidly get up to speed on what it’s about using the highlights you previously made. You’ll quickly be able to tell if you’ve added too many highlights or too few.  
对您创建的笔记是否可被发现的真正测试是, 您是否可以一目了然地了解它的要点。将它搁置几天, 并设置一个提醒, 以便在您忘记大部分细节后重新访问它。当你回头看时, 给自己不超过 30 秒的时间, 看看你是否可以使用之前制作的亮点快速了解它的内容。您很快就能判断是添加了太多高光还是太少。

Each time you decide to add a highlight, you are developing your judgment: distinguishing the bits that truly matter from those that don’t. This is a skill you can become better at over time. The more you exercise your judgment, the more efficient and enjoyable your notetaking will become because you know that every minute of attention you invest is creating lasting value. There are few things more satisfying than the feeling of making consistent progress.  
每次你决定添加亮点时, 你都在发展你的判断力: 区分真正重要的部分和不重要的部分。这是一项你可以随着时间的推移变得更好的技能。你越多地运用你的判断力, 你的 notetaking 就会变得越高效和愉快, 因为你知道你投入的每一分钟注意力都在创造持久的价值。没有什么比不断进步的感觉更令人满足的了。

In the next chapter, we will move on to the final step of CODE, drawing on the material you’ve collected and distilled and using it to express your own point of view.  
在下一章中, 我们将继续进行 CODE 的最后一步, 利用您收集和提炼的材料, 并用它来表达您自己的观点。

1.  [II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-019-backlink). I like to think of layer one as the “soil”—an excerpt from a source or my own thinking (whether as words, drawings, images, or audio) I initially capture into my notes. They are like the ground on which my understanding will be built. Layer two is “oil,” as in “I’ve struck oil!,” conveniently represented by black, bolded text. Layer three is “gold,” which is even more valuable, and shines in highlighter yellow in many apps. Layer four is the “gems,” the most rare and illuminating finds that I’ve distilled in my own words as an executive summary.  
    二。我喜欢将第一层视为 “土壤”——我最初捕捉到笔记中的来源或我自己的想法(无论是文字、图画、图像还是音频) 的摘录。它们就像是我理解的基础。第二层是“石油”, 如“我发现石油了!”, 方便地用黑色粗体文本表示。第三层是“黄金”, 它更有价值, 在许多应用程序中闪耀着荧光笔的黄色。第四层是“宝石”, 是我用自己的话提炼出来的最罕见、最有启发性的发现, 作为执行摘要。
2.  [III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-018-backlink). In his book _A New Method of Making Common-Place-Books_, John Locke similarly advised that “We extract only those Things which are Choice and Excellent, either for the Matter itself, or else the Elegancy of the Expression, and not what comes next.”  
    三、约翰 · 洛克在他的《制作普通书本的新方法》一书中同样建议,“我们只提取那些精选和优秀的东西, 无论是为了事情本身, 还是为了表达的优雅, 而不是接下来会发生什么”
3.  [IV](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-017-backlink). As humans we are exquisitely sensitive to the way information is presented. In web design, a slight change of color for a button or a slightly reworded headline can easily have a double-digit impact on the number of visitors who click on it. Imagine if we put as much thought into how the information on our own devices is presented to us as we do into the public web. Even something as simple as an informative heading, a paragraph break, or a highlighted phrase can make it dramatically easier to absorb a piece of text.  
    四、作为人类, 我们对信息的呈现方式极为敏感。在网页设计中, 按钮颜色的轻微变化或标题的轻微改写很容易对点击它的访问者数量产生两位数的影响。想象一下, 如果我们像对待公共网络一样, 尽可能多地考虑自己设备上的信息是如何呈现给我们的。即使像信息性标题、段落分隔符或突出显示的短语这样简单的内容也可以使吸收一段文本变得非常容易。
4.  [V](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-016-backlink). Distillation also applies to other kinds of media such as images, audio, and video, but looks quite different and is beyond the scope of this book.  
    诉。蒸馏也适用于其他类型的媒体, 如图像、音频和视频, 但看起来很不一样, 超出了本书的范围。
5.  [VI](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-015-backlink). In a class on documentary filmmaking on the educational platform MasterClass, Burns offered his suggestions for keeping track of material related to a film project: “Is there an article in the newspaper that relates to your project? Cut it out and file it. Have you written a draft of narration or dialogue? Print it out and file it. Thought of some great questions to ask your first interview subject? Jot them down on a scrap of paper and file that too.”  
    六. 在教育平台 MasterClass 上的纪录片制作课程中, 伯恩斯提出了他对跟踪与电影项目相关的材料的建议:“报纸上有与你的项目相关的文章吗? 把它剪下来归档。你写过旁白或对话的草稿吗? 打印出来并存档。想到一些重要的问题来问你的第一个采访对象? 把它们记在一张纸片上, 然后也归档。”
6.  [VII](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-014-backlink). This principle is called stigmergy—to leave “marks” on the environment that make your future efforts easier. It is a strategy used by ant colonies to find food. If an ant finds a food source, it will bring a piece of it back to the colony, while leaving a special pheromone along the trail. Other ants can follow this trail to find the food for themselves, enabling a multitude of ants to quickly find and collect new sources of food.  
    七。这个原则被称为耻辱——在环境上留下 “标记”, 使你未来的努力更容易。这是蚁群用来寻找食物的策略。如果一只蚂蚁找到了食物来源, 它会把其中的一部分带回蚁群, 同时在沿途留下一种特殊的信息素。其他蚂蚁可以沿着这条轨迹为自己寻找食物, 使众多蚂蚁能够快速找到并收集新的食物来源。
7.  [VIII](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-013-backlink). As Sönke Ahrens observes in his book _How to Take Smart Notes_, this is the fundamental paradox at the heart of writing: you have to do the research before you know what you will write about. In his words: “We have to read with a pen in hand, develop ideas on paper and build up an ever-growing pool of externalised thoughts. We will not be guided by a blindly made-up plan picked from our unreliable brains, but by our interest, curiosity and intuition, which is formed and informed by the actual work of reading, thinking, discussing, writing and developing ideas—and is something that continuously grows and reflects our knowledge and understanding externally.”  
    八。正如 Sönke Ahrens 在他的《如何做聪明的笔记》一书中所观察到的, 这是写作核心的基本悖论: 在知道要写什么之前, 你必须先做研究。用他的话说:“我们必须手持笔阅读, 在纸上发展想法, 并建立不断增长的外化思想库。我们不会被从我们不可靠的大脑中挑选出来的盲目制定的计划所引导, 而是被我们的兴趣、好奇心和直觉所引导, 这些兴趣、好奇心和直觉是由阅读、思考、讨论、写作和发展想法的实际工作形成和启发的——并且是不断增长并从外部反映我们的知识和理解的东西。”

Chapter 7 Express—Show Your Work  
Chapter 7 Express——展示你的作品
------------------------------------------------------------

> Verum ipsum factum (“We only know what we make”)  
> Verum ipsum factum(“我们只知道我们做了什么”)
> 
> —Giambattista Vico, Italian philosopher  
> ——詹巴蒂斯塔 · 维科, 意大利哲学家

In June 1947, a baby girl named Octavia Estelle Butler was born in Pasadena, California.  
1947 年 6 月, 一个名叫 Octavia Estelle Butler 的女婴在加利福尼亚州帕萨迪纳出生。

Known in her early years as simply “Estelle,” she was raised by a single, widowed mother who worked domestic jobs to make ends meet. Painfully shy and introverted from a young age, Estelle became an easy target for bullying at school, which led her to believe she was “ugly and stupid, clumsy, socially hopeless.”[1](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch07fn1) Her shyness combined with mild dyslexia made schoolwork difficult.  
她早年被简称为 “Estelle”, 由一位寡居的单身母亲抚养长大, 母亲从事家政工作以维持生计。埃斯特尔从小就非常害羞内向, 在学校很容易成为欺凌的目标, 这让她认为自己 “又丑又蠢, 笨拙, 在社交上毫无希望”。 [1](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch07fn1) 她的害羞加上轻度阅读障碍使功课变得困难。

In response, Estelle turned inward to her own imagination and outward to the Pasadena Central Library, where she would spend countless hours reading fairy tales and horse stories, and later, the fantasy and science-fiction novels that would eventually inspire her to become a writer.  
作为回应, 埃斯特尔向内转向自己的想象力, 向外转向帕萨迪纳中央图书馆, 在那里她会花无数个小时阅读童话故事和马的故事, 后来, 奇幻小说和科幻小说最终激发了她成为一名作家的灵感.

Despite the odds stacked against her, this young woman would eventually become one of the most successful and influential science-fiction writers of her generation, winning multiple Hugo and Nebula awards (the genre’s highest honors) and in 1995 becoming the first sci-fi writer to receive a MacArthur “Genius” Fellowship.  
尽管困难重重, 这位年轻女性最终成为了她那一代最成功和最有影响力的 科幻作家之一, 多次获得雨果奖和星云奖 (该类型的最高荣誉), 并于 1995 年成为第一位科幻作家 - fi 作家获得麦克阿瑟“天才” 奖学金。

But Estelle wasn’t always so successful. Her teachers at Garfield Elementary School evaluated her earliest writing harshly, with comments like “Hyperbolic” and “You’re not even trying” scribbled in the margins.[2](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch07fn2) An elementary school teacher once asked, “Why include the science fiction touch? I think the story would be more universal if you kept to the human, earthly touch.” The teacher reported to her mother that “She has the understanding, but doesn’t apply it. She needs to learn self-discipline.”  
但埃斯特尔并不总是那么成功。她在加菲尔德小学的老师严厉地评价了她最早的作品, 在空白处潦草地写下了诸如 “双曲线” 和“你甚至没有尝试”之类的评论。 [2](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch07fn2) 一位小学老师曾问:“为什么要加入科幻元素? 我认为, 如果你保持人性化、世俗化, 这个故事会更具有普遍性。” 老师向她的母亲报告说:“她有理解力, 但没有应用它。她需要学会自律。”

When she was twelve years old, Estelle watched the 1954 film _Devil Girl From Mars_, a sensationalist B movie that was so terrible it convinced Estelle that she could write something better. She recalls, “Until I began writing my own stories, I never found quite what I was looking for... In desperation, I made up my own.”  
埃斯特尔 12 岁时看了 1954 年的电影《来自火星的恶魔女孩》, 这是一部耸人听闻的 B 级电影, 非常糟糕, 它让埃斯特尔相信她可以写出更好的东西。她回忆说,“在我开始写自己的故事之前, 我从来没有找到我想要的东西...... 无奈之下, 我编了自己的故事。”

As the possibility of becoming a professional writer slowly dawned on her, Estelle began her transformation into “Octavia,” whom she thought of as her powerful, assertive alter ego. Octavia took on a series of temporary or part-time jobs after graduating from high school: clerical, factory, warehouse, laundry, and food preparation gigs—anything that wasn’t too mentally taxing, and that allowed her to maintain her routine of waking before dawn each morning to write.  
随着成为一名职业作家的可能性慢慢浮现, 埃斯特尔开始转变为 “奥克塔维亚”, 她认为她是她强大、自信的另一个自我。奥克塔维亚高中毕业后从事了一系列临时或兼职工作: 文员、工厂、仓库、洗衣店和准备食物的工作——任何不太费脑力的工作, 这让她能够保持她每天醒来的习惯每天早上黎明前写作。

The emerging Octavia made three rules for herself:  
崭露头角的 Octavia 为自己制定了三项规则:

1.  Don’t leave your home without a notebook, paper scraps, something to write with.  
    不要在没有笔记本、纸屑和可以用来写字的东西的情况下离开家。
2.  Don’t walk into the world without your eyes and ears focused and open.  
    不要在眼睛和耳朵没有专注和开放的情况下走进这个世界。
3.  Don’t make excuses about what you don’t have or what you would do if you did, use that energy to “find a way, make a way.”  
    不要为你没有的东西或如果你有你会做什么找借口, 用那种能量去 “找到方法, 开辟方法”。

Thus began a lifelong relationship with her commonplace books. Butler would scrape together twenty-five cents to buy small Mead memo pads, and in those pages she took notes on every aspect of her life: grocery and clothes shopping lists, last-minute to-dos, wishes and intentions, and calculations of her remaining funds for rent, food, and utilities. She meticulously tracked her daily writing goals and page counts, lists of her failings and desired personal qualities, her wishes and dreams for the future, and contracts she would sign with herself each day for how many words she committed to write.  
就这样开始了她与平凡书籍的终生关系。 Butler 会凑出 25 美分买小的 Mead 记事本, 在这些记事本上她记下了她生活的方方面面: 杂货和衣服购物清单、最后一分钟的待办事项、愿望和意图, 以及她的计算剩余资金用于租金、食品和水电费。她一丝不苟地记录她每天的写作目标和页数, 列出她的失败和想要的个人品质, 她对未来的愿望和梦想, 以及她每天要与自己签订的关于她承诺写多少字的合同。

Of course, Butler also gathered material for her fantastic stories: lyrics to songs she’d heard on the radio, an idea for a character’s name or motivation, a new topic to research, details of news stories—everything she needed to build the worlds her stories would take place in. She studied dozens of topics—anthropology, English, journalism, and speech. She traveled to the Amazon and Incan ruins in Peru to get a firsthand taste of biodiversity and civilizational collapse. Like a journalist, Butler had a love for cold, hard facts to imbue her stories with a sense of authenticity and concreteness: “The greater your ignorance the more verifiably accurate must be your facts,” she once remarked.  
当然, 巴特勒也为她的奇妙故事收集了材料: 她在收音机里听到的歌曲的歌词、人物名字或动机的想法、要研究的新主题、新闻故事的细节——她构建世界所需的一切她的故事将发生在。她研究了几十个主题——人类学、英语、新闻学和演讲。她前往秘鲁的亚马逊和印加遗址, 亲身体验生物多样性和文明崩溃。就像一名记者一样, 巴特勒喜欢冷酷无情的事实, 使她的故事充满真实感和具体感:“你越无知, 你的事实就越准确,” 她曾经说过。

One of Butler’s novels, _The Parable of the Sower_, hit the _New York Times_ bestseller list for the first time in 2020,[3](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch07fn3) fulfilling one of Butler’s life goals fourteen years after her death. The book portrays a postapocalyptic future in the aftermath of runaway climate disasters, in which small communities must band together in order to survive. These eerily prescient forecasts resonated with readers as the COVID-19 pandemic unfolded, as our own time began to seem similarly bleak and uncertain. The radical reimagination of what life could look like in the midst of a crisis was no longer idle speculation—it had become a daily preoccupation for people around the world. Butler has been called a prophet for her ability to forecast the future, but she always said that her work came from simply imagining, “If this goes on... it extrapolates from current technology, current ecological conditions, current social conditions, current practices of any sort. It offers good possibilities—as well as warnings.”  
巴特勒的其中一部小说《播种者的寓言》在 2020 年首次登上纽约时报畅销书排行榜, [3](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch07fn3) 在她去世十四年后实现了巴特勒的人生目标之一。这本书描绘了失控的气候灾难过后的世界末日未来, 小社区必须团结起来才能生存。随着 COVID-19 大流行的展开, 这些极具先见之明的预测引起了读者的共鸣, 因为我们自己的时代开始显得同样黯淡和不确定。对 在危机中的生活可能是什么样子的彻底重新想象不再是无聊的猜测——它已经成为世界各地人们每天都在关注的事情。巴特勒因其预测未来的能力而被称为先知, 但她总是说她的工作来自于简单的想象,“如果这种情况继续下去...... 种类。它提供了很好的可能性——以及警告。”

Butler knew that science fiction was more than entertainment. It was a transformative way of viewing the future. As one of the first Black women to gain recognition in the sci-fi genre, Butler explored ideas and themes that had been previously ignored: the potential consequences of environmental collapse due to climate change, corporate greed and the growing gap between the wealthy and poor, gender fluidity and the “othering” of marginalized groups, and criticism of the hierarchical nature of society, among other themes.  
巴特勒知道科幻小说不仅仅是娱乐。这是一种看待未来的变革方式。作为第一批在科幻小说中获得认可的黑人女性之一, 巴特勒探索了以前被忽视的想法和主题: 气候变化导致环境崩溃的潜在后果、企业的贪婪以及日益扩大的贫富差距, 性别流动性和边缘化群体的 “他者化”, 以及对社会等级性质的批评等主题。

Butler pioneered Afrofuturism, a genre that cast African Americans as protagonists who embrace radical change in order to survive. Her stories allowed her readers to visualize futures in which marginalized people are heroes, not victims. Through her writing, she expanded our vision of the future to include the untold stories of the disenfranchised, the outcast, and the unconventional.  
巴特勒开创了非洲未来主义, 这是一种将非裔美国人塑造成主角的流派, 他们为了生存而接受激进的变革。她的故事让她的读者想象未来, 边缘化的人是英雄, 而不是受害者。通过她的写作, 她扩大了我们对未来的视野, 将被剥夺权利者、被遗弃者和非传统者的不为人知的故事包括在内。

How do we know so much about even the tiniest details of Butler’s life? Because she kept it all—journals, commonplace books, speeches, library call slips, essay and story drafts, school notes, calendars, and datebooks as well as assorted odds and ends like school progress reports, bus passes, yearbooks, and contracts. This collection contained 9,062 items and filled 386 boxes when it was donated to the Huntington Library in San Marino, California, after Butler’s passing.[4](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch07fn4)  
我们怎么知道巴特勒生活中最细微的细节呢? 因为她保留了一切——日记、普通书籍、演讲稿、图书馆借阅单、论文和故事草稿、学校笔记、日历和记事本, 以及学校进度报告、公车通行证、年鉴和合同等各种零碎物品。在巴特勒去世后, 该馆藏被捐赠给加利福尼亚州圣马力诺的亨廷顿图书馆, 共包含 9,062 件物品并装满了 386 个盒子。 [4](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch07fn4)

How could a painfully shy little girl become a world-renowned, award-winning writer? How could an impoverished and overworked young woman emerge as a powerful prophet of the future? In her own words: “My mother was a maid, my father shined shoes, and I wanted to write science fiction, who was I kidding?”  
一个非常害羞的小女孩怎么能成为世界知名的获奖作家呢? 一个穷困潦倒、劳累过度的年轻女性如何成为未来的强大预言家? 用她自己 的话来说:“妈妈是女仆, 爸爸擦鞋, 我想写科幻小说, 我跟谁开玩笑?”

Butler did it by drawing on her life experience: “The painful, horrifying, the unpleasant things that happen, affect my work more strongly than the pleasant ones. They’re more memorable and more likely to goad me into writing interesting stories.”  
巴特勒通过借鉴她的生活经历做到了这一点:“发生的痛苦、可怕和不愉快的事情对我的工作的影响比愉快的事情更强烈。它们更令人难忘, 更有可能促使我写出有趣的故事。”

She used her notes and her writing to confront her demons: “The biggest obstacle I had to overcome was my own fear and self-doubt—fear that maybe my work really wasn’t good enough, maybe I wasn’t smart enough; maybe the people telling me I couldn’t make it were right.”  
她用她的笔记和她的写作来对抗她的恶魔:“我必须克服的最大障碍是我自己的恐惧和自我怀疑——害怕我的工作可能真的不够好, 也许我不够聪明; 也许那些告诉我我做不到的人是对的。”

She used every bit of insight and detail she could muster from both her daily life and the books she immersed herself in: “Use what you have; even if it seems meager, it may be magic in your hands.” Butler found a way to express her voice and her ideas even when her circumstances made it seem impossible.  
她运用了她从日常生活和她沉浸其中的书籍中所能收集到的每一点洞察力和细节:“使用你所拥有的; 哪怕看起来微不足道, 在你手中也可能是魔法。” 巴特勒找到了一种方式来表达她的声音和想法, 即使她的情况似乎不可能。

The myth of the writer sitting down before a completely blank page, or the artist at a completely blank canvas, is just that—a myth. Professional creatives constantly draw on outside sources of inspiration—their own experiences and observations, lessons gleaned from successes and failures alike, and the ideas of others. If there is a secret to creativity, it is that it _emerges_ from everyday efforts to gather and organize our influences.  
作家坐在完全空白的页面前, 或者艺术家坐在完全空白的画布前的神话, 只是一个神话。专业创意人员不断汲取外部灵感来源——他们自己的经验和观察、从成功和失败中吸取的教训, 以及他人的想法。如果说创造力有什么秘诀, 那就是它源于每天收集和组织我们的影响力的努力。

### How to Protect Your Most Precious Resource  
如何保护您最宝贵的资源

As knowledge workers, attention is our most scarce and precious resource.  
作为知识工作者, 注意力是我们最稀缺和最宝贵的资源。

The creative process is fueled by attention at every step. It is the lens that allows us to make sense of what’s happening, to notice what resources we have at our disposal, and to see the contribution we can make. The ability to intentionally and strategically allocate our attention is a competitive advantage in a distracted world. We have to jealously guard it like a valuable treasure.  
创作过程的每一步都受到关注的推动。它是让我们了解正在发生的事情的镜头, 注意到我们拥有哪些资源, 并看到我们可以 make 做出的贡献。在一个分心的世界中, 有意识地和战略性地分配我们注意力的能力是一种竞争优势。我们必须像珍宝一样小心翼翼地保护它。

You have twenty-four hours in a day, but how many of those hours include your highest-quality attention? Some days are so frenetic and fragmented that you might not have any at all. Attention can be cultivated but also destroyed—by distractions, interruptions, and environments that don’t protect it. The challenge we face in building a Second Brain is how to establish a system for personal knowledge that _frees up_ attention, instead of taking more of it.  
你一天有二十四小时, 但其中有多少小时是你最高质量的注意力? 有些日子如此狂热和支离破碎, 你可能根本没有。注意力可以通过分心、干扰和不保护它的环境来培养, 但也可以被破坏。我们在构建第二大脑时面临的挑战是如何建立一个个人知识系统来释放注意力, 而不是占用更多注意力。

We’ve been taught that it’s important to work “with the end in mind.” We are told that it is our responsibility to deliver results, whether that is a finished product on store shelves, a speech delivered at an event, or a published technical document.  
我们被教导说,“以终为始” 工作很重要。我们被告知, 交付结果是我们的责任, 无论是商店货架上的成品、活动中的演讲, 还是已发布的技术文档。

This is generally good advice, but there is a flaw in focusing only on the final results: all the intermediate work—the notes, the drafts, the outlines, the feedback—tends to be underappreciated and undervalued. The precious attention we invested in producing that in-between work gets thrown away, never to be used again. Because we manage most of our “work-in-process” in our head, as soon as we finish the project and step away from our desks, all that valuable knowledge we worked so hard to acquire dissolves from our memory like a sandcastle washed away by the ocean waves.  
这通常是个好建议, 但只关注最终结果存在一个缺陷: 所有中间工作——笔记、草稿、大纲、反馈——往往被低估和低估。我们投入制作中间作品的宝贵注意力被扔掉了, 永远不会再被使用。因为我们在头脑中管理大部分 “半成品”, 所以一旦我们完成项目并离开办公桌, 我们辛辛苦苦获得的所有宝贵知识就会像沙堡一样从我们的记忆中消失由海浪。

If we consider the focused application of our attention to be our greatest asset as knowledge workers, we can no longer afford to let that intermediate work disappear. If we consider how precious little time we have to produce something extraordinary in our careers, it becomes imperative that we recycle that knowledge back into a system where it can become useful again.  
如果我们认为集中注意力是我们作为知识工作者的最大资产, 那么我们就不能再让这种中间工作消失了。如果我们考虑到在我们的职业生涯中创造出非凡的东西所需要的时间是多么宝贵, 那么我们就必须将这些知识回收到一个可以再次发挥作用的系统中。

What are the knowledge assets you’re creating today that will be most reusable in the future? What are the building blocks that will move forward your projects tomorrow? How can you package up what you know in a form that you’ll be able to revisit it again and again no matter what endeavors you take on in the future?  
您今天创建的知识资产有哪些将来最可重用? 明天将推进您的项目的基石是什么? 你怎么能把你所知道的打包成一种形式, 无论你在未来做出什么努力, 你都可以一次又一次地重新访问它?

The final stage of the creative process, Express, is about refusing to wait until you have everything perfectly ready before you share what you know. It is about expressing your ideas earlier, more frequently, and in smaller chunks to test what works and gather feedback from others. That feedback in turn gets drawn in to your Second Brain, where it becomes the starting point for the next iteration of your work.  
创意过程的最后阶段, 表达, 就是拒绝等到一切都准备就绪后再分享你所知道的。它是关于更早、更频繁地以更小的块表达您的想法, 以测试哪些有效并收集他人的反馈。该反馈反过来会被吸引到您的第二大脑中, 成为您下一次工作迭代的起点。

### Intermediate Packets: The Power of Thinking Small  
中间数据包: 从小思考的力量

The idea of dividing our work into smaller units isn’t new. You’ve probably heard this advice a hundred times: if you’re stuck on a task, break it down into smaller steps.  
将我们的工作分成更小的单元的想法并不新鲜。你可能已经听过这个建议一百遍了: 如果你被困在一个任务上, 把它分解成更小的步骤。

Every profession and creative medium has its own version of “intermediate steps” on the way to full-fledged final works. For example:  
在通往成熟最终作品的道路上, 每个专业和创意媒体都有自己版本的 “中间步骤”。例如:

*   “Modules” in software development  
    软件开发中的 “模块”
*   “Betas” tested by start-ups 初创企业测试的 “贝塔”
*   “Sketches” in architecture 建筑中的 “草图”
*   “Pilots” for television series 电视连续剧的 “飞行员”
*   “Prototypes” made by engineers 工程师制作的 “原型”
*   “Concept cars” in auto design 汽车设计中的 “概念车”
*   “Demos” in music recording 音乐录音中的 “小样”

Each of these terms is the equivalent of a “rough draft” you create as part of the process of making something new.  
这些术语中的每一个都相当于您在制作新事物的过程中创建的 “草稿”。

Here’s what most people miss: it’s not enough to simply divide tasks into smaller pieces—you then need a _system_ for managing those pieces. Otherwise, you’re just creating a lot of extra work for yourself trying to keep track of them.  
这是大多数人所忽略的: 简单地将任务分成更小的部分是不够的——你还需要一个系统来管理这些部分。否则, 您只是在为自己跟踪它们而做很多额外的工作。

That system is your Second Brain, and the small pieces of work-in-process it contains I call “Intermediate Packets.” Intermediate Packets are the concrete, individual building blocks that make up your work.[I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-012) For example, a set of notes from a team meeting, a list of relevant research findings, a brainstorm with collaborators, a slide deck analyzing the market, or a list of action items from a conference call. Any note can potentially be used as an Intermediate Packet in some larger project or goal.  
这个系统就是你的第二大脑, 它包含的小块半成品我称之为 “中间数据包”。中间数据包是构成您的工作的具体的、独立的构建块。 [I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-012) 例如, 团队会议的一组笔记、相关研究结果的列表、与合作者的头脑风暴、分析市场的幻灯片或电话会议的行动项目列表。任何笔记都可能用作某个较大项目或目标中的中间包。

Think of a salesperson planning a new campaign for a health-branded energy drink. Sales might seem like the kind of job that is least related to “knowledge management.” Isn’t it all about making calls, having meetings, sending pitches, and closing deals?  
想一想销售人员正在为一种健康品牌的能量饮料策划一项新的活动。销售似乎是与 “知识管理” 关系最少的工作。这不就是打电话、开会、发送推介和完成交易吗?

If we take a closer look, there are many building blocks such a sales job relies on. The company brochure, the sales prospectus, the cold-calling scripts, the list of warm leads, notes from past calls with an important distributor—these are the assets that a salesperson depends on for their performance.  
如果我们仔细观察, 就会发现这样的销售工作依赖于许多构建模块。公司宣传册、销售说明书、推销电话脚本、热情线索清单、过去与重要经销商通话的记录——这些都是销售人员取得业绩所依赖的资产。

Like LEGO blocks, the more pieces you have, the easier it is to build something interesting. Imagine that instead of starting your next project with a blank slate, you started with a set of building blocks—research findings, web clippings, PDF highlights, book notes, back-of-the-envelope sketches—that represent your long-term effort to make sense of your field, your industry, and the world at large.  
就像乐高积木一样, 您拥有的积木越多, 就越容易拼出有趣的东西。想象一下, 您不是从一张白纸开始您的下一个项目, 而是从一组构建块开始——研究发现、网络剪报、PDF 亮点、书本笔记、信封背面草图——它们代表了您的长期努力了解您的领域、您的行业和整个世界。

Our time and attention are scarce, and it’s time we treated the things we invest in—reports, deliverables, plans, pieces of writing, graphics, slides—as knowledge assets that can be reused instead of reproducing them from scratch. Reusing Intermediate Packets of work frees up our attention for higher-order, more creative thinking. Thinking _small_ is the best way to elevate your horizons and expand your ambitions.  
我们的时间和注意力是稀缺的, 是时候将我们投资的东西——报告、可交付成果、计划、文章、图形、幻灯片——视为可以重复使用的知识资产, 而不是从头开始复制它们。重复使用中间的工作包可以释放我们的注意力, 让我们进行更高层次、更具创造性的思考。从小处着想是提升视野和扩大抱负的最佳方式。

There are five kinds of Intermediate Packets you can create and reuse in your work:  
您可以在工作中创建和重用五种中间包:

*   **Distilled notes:** Books or articles you’ve read and distilled so it’s easy to get the gist of what they contain (using the Progressive Summarization technique you learned in the previous chapter, for example).  
    提炼笔记: 您阅读和提炼的书籍或文章, 因此很容易了解它们包含的内容的要点 (例如, 使用您在上一章中学到的渐进式摘要技术)。
*   **Outtakes:** The material or ideas that didn’t make it into a past project but could be used in future ones.  
    Outtakes: 没有在过去的项目中使用但可以在未来的项目中使用的材料或想法。
*   **Work-in-process:** The documents, graphics, agendas, or plans you produced during past projects.  
    半成品: 您在过去的项目中制作的文档、图形、议程或计划。
*   **Final deliverables:** Concrete pieces of work you’ve delivered as part of past projects, which could become components of something new.  
    最终可交付成果: 作为过去项目的一部分, 您交付的具体工作可能会成为新项目的组成部分。
*   **Documents created by others:** Knowledge assets created by people on your team, contractors or consultants, or even clients or customers, that you can reference and incorporate into your work.  
    他人创建的文档: 由您的团队成员、承包商或顾问, 甚至客户创建的知识资产, 您可以参考这些知识资产并将其纳入您的工作中。

If you’re reading how-to articles in your free time, you can save the best tips in your notes and turn them into _distilled notes_ for when it’s time to put them to use. If you’re writing an essay and decide to cut a paragraph, you can save those _outtakes_ in case you ever write a follow-up. If you are in product development and create a detailed set of requirements, you can save that _work-in-process_ as a template for future products. If you’re a management consultant, you can save the slides you presented to an executive team as a _final deliverable_, and reuse them for similar presentations. If you’re a lab scientist and a colleague designs the perfect lab protocol, you can reuse and improve that _document_ for your own use (with their permission, of course).
