*   If you want to write a book, you could dial down the scope and write a series of online articles outlining your main ideas. If you don’t have time for that, you could dial it down even further and start with a social media post explaining the essence of your message.  
    如果你想写一本书, 你可以缩小范围, 写一系列概述你的主要思想的在线文章。如果你没有时间这样做, 你可以进一步降低它并从社交媒体帖子开始解释你的信息的本质。
*   If you want to deliver a workshop for paying clients, you could dial it down to a free workshop at a local meetup, or dial it down even further and start with a group exercise or book club for a handful of colleagues or friends.  
    如果你想为付费客户举办一个研讨会, 你可以在当地聚会上将其调低到一个免费的研讨会, 或者进一步调低它并从为少数同事或朋友进行的小组练习或读书俱乐部开始.
*   If you’d like to make a short film, start with a YouTube video, or if that’s too intimidating, a livestream. If it’s still too much, record a rough cut on your phone and send it to a friend.  
    如果你想制作一部短片, 可以从 YouTube 视频开始, 或者如果这太吓人的话, 可以从直播开始。如果仍然太多, 请在手机上录制粗剪并发送给朋友。
*   If you want to design a brand identity for a company, start with a mock-up of a single web page. Even easier, start with a few hand-drawn sketches with your ideas for a logo.  
    如果您想为公司设计品牌标识, 请从单个网页的模型开始。更简单的是, 从一些手绘草图和您对徽标的想法开始。

How can you know which direction to take your thinking without feedback from customers, colleagues, collaborators, or friends? And how can you collect that feedback without showing them something concrete? This is the chicken-and-egg problem of creativity: you don’t know what you should create, but you can’t discover what people want until you create something. Dialing Down the Scope is a way of short-circuiting that paradox and testing the waters with something small and concrete, while still protecting the fragile and tentative edges of your work.  
如果没有来自客户、同事、合作者或朋友的反馈, 您怎么能知道将您的想法转向哪个方向? 您如何在不向他们展示具体内容的情况下收集反馈? 这就是创造力的先有鸡还是先有蛋的问题: 你不知道自己应该创造什么, 但只有创造出一些东西, 你才能发现人们想要什么。缩小范围是一种让悖论短路并用小而具体的东西测试水域的方法, 同时仍然保护你工作的脆弱和试探性的边缘。

Divergence and convergence are not a linear path, but a loop: once you complete one round of convergence, you can take what you’ve learned right back into a new cycle of divergence. Keep alternating back and forth, making iterations each time until it’s something you can consider “done” or “complete” and share more widely.  
发散和收敛不是一条直线路径, 而是一个循环: 一旦你完成一轮收敛, 你就可以把你学到的东西带回到新的发散循环中。保持来回交替, 每次都进行迭代, 直到你认为它 “完成” 或“完成”并更广泛地分享。

### Divergence and Convergence in the Wild: Behind the Scenes of a Home Project  
野外的分歧与融合: 家庭项目的幕后

Let me share an example of one of my own projects in which I used all three of these techniques: remodeling our garage into a home office.  
让我分享一个我自己的项目的例子, 我在其中使用了所有这三种技术: 将我们的车库改造成家庭办公室。

When we moved into our home, my wife and I soon realized we needed a better workspace. We both work from home, and the tiny extra bedroom wasn’t cutting it, especially once our son was born. We excitedly made plans to turn our garage into a home studio. The moment I created a dedicated project folder, I knew it was on.  
当我们搬进我们的家时, 我和我的妻子很快意识到我们需要一个更好的工作空间。我们都在家工作, 额外的小卧室并没有减少它, 尤其是在我们的儿子出生后。我们兴奋地制定了将我们的车库变成家庭工作室的计划。在我创建专用项目文件夹的那一刻, 我就知道它已经打开了。

I started by creating an Archipelago of Ideas—an outline of the main questions, considerations, desired features, and constraints I thought our project would entail. Here is the outline I came up with after fifteen minutes:  
我首先创建了一个想法群岛——我认为我们的项目将涉及的主要问题、考虑因素、所需特性和限制的概述。这是我在十五分钟后得出的大纲:

I didn’t know up front what the main headings of this document would include, but as I wrote out my thoughts they soon emerged: Intro, Cost, Ideas, Phases, Aesthetics, Zoom Setup, Equipment, and Open Questions. I did a few searches of my Second Brain for terms like “home office” and “home studio” and found several existing notes that could come in handy as well. For example, I found the notes with recommendations from a friend who had experience designing studios, which I mentioned previously; photos of a beautifully designed café in Mexico City that my wife and I loved visiting and wanted to mimic; and a note with best practices for hosting Zoom calls, such as finding the right lighting and a background that isn’t too distracting. I added links to them at the bottom of my outline as well.  
我事先不知道这份文件的主要标题会包括什么, 但当我写下我的想法时, 它们很快就出现了: 介绍、成本、想法、阶段、美学、缩放设置、设备和未决问题。我在我的第二大脑中搜索了一些术语, 如 “家庭办公室” 和“家庭工作室”, 并发现了一些现有的笔记也可以派上用场。例如, 我找到了一个有设计工作室经验的朋友推荐的笔记, 这是我之前提到的; 墨西哥城一家设计精美的咖啡馆的照片, 我和我的妻子很喜欢光顾并想模仿; 以及有关主持 Zoom 通话的最佳实践的说明, 例如找到合适的照明和不会太分散注意力的背景。我还在大纲底部添加了指向它们的链接。

Even with some existing material to work with, there were gaps in our plan. Over the next few weeks, whenever I had a free window of time, I collected and captured tidbits of content to inform our home studio remodel. I saved photos from Pinterest showing examples of home offices that I thought looked neat; notes from a conversation with a musician friend who taught me about soundproofing; and a list of local contractors a neighbor shared with me to reach out to. I even went on a late-night spree watching dozens of videos of YouTubers giving tours of their studios, taking notes on the small details of how they converted empty spaces into functional workspaces.  
即使有一些现有的材料可以使用, 我们的计划也存在差距。在接下来的几周里, 每当我有空闲时间时, 我都会收集并捕捉内容花絮, 为我们的家庭工作室改造提供信息。我保存了 Pinterest 上的照片, 展示了我认为看起来整洁的家庭办公室的例子; 与教我隔音的音乐家朋友的谈话笔记; 以及邻居与我分享的当地承包商名单。我什至在深夜狂欢观看了 YouTube 用户参观工作室的数十个视频, 记录了他们如何将空置空间转变为功能性工作空间的细节。

Between managing the business and our household, my time was incredibly scarce as we began the remodel. Whenever I could, I would highlight and distill the last few notes I’d captured and leave my future self a brief note about where I left off. I used a series of Hemingway Bridges to string together many such windows of time that otherwise wouldn’t have been of much productive use.  
在管理企业和我们的家庭之间, 当我们开始改造时, 我的时间非常少。只要有可能, 我就会突出显示并提炼出我记录的最后几个笔记, 并给未来的自己留下简短的笔记, 说明我在哪里停下来。我使用了一系列海明威桥将许多这样的时间窗口串在一起, 否则这些时间窗口不会有太多的生产用途。

Finally, as all these thoughts and ideas and wishes and dreams began to add up, the project became quite a juggernaut. Before I knew it, our ambitions had expanded to knocking down walls, cutting through the roof for a skylight, laying down cable for superfast Internet, and redesigning the layout of the backyard to accommodate it all. We had diverged too far and needed to rein it in a bit.  
最后, 随着所有这些想法、想法、愿望和梦想开始叠加, 这个项目变得非常强大。在我不知不觉中, 我们的野心已经扩大到推倒墙壁、切开屋顶开天窗、铺设超高速互联网的电缆, 以及重新设计后院的布局以容纳这一切。我们分歧太大, 需要稍微控制一下。

This is where Dialing Down the Scope was essential: we identified the most outlandish of our plans and decided to save those for a later stage. I moved those ideas to their own “someday/maybe” section of my outline to revisit later. My wife and I also added several constraints to the project, such as the budget we were willing to spend, and a deadline to have the remodel done by a certain date. These constraints helped us reduce the scope of the project to something reasonable and manageable. As soon as we did, the next steps of finding a contractor and finalizing the floorplan became crystal clear.  
这就是缩小范围的关键所在: 我们确定了我们计划中最古怪的一个, 并决定将它们留到以后再做。我将这些想法移到了大纲中它们自己的 “某天 / 也许” 部分, 以便稍后重新访问。我和我的妻子还为该项目添加了一些限制条件, 例如我们愿意花费的预算, 以及在特定日期之前完成改造的最后期限。这些约束帮助我们将项目范围缩小到合理且可管理的范围内。一旦我们这样做了, 寻找承包商和最终确定平面图的下一步就变得非常清楚了。

### Your Turn: Move Fast and Make Things  
轮到你了: 快速行动并创造事物

If you’d like to give this approach to executing projects a try, now is the perfect time.  
如果您想尝试使用这种方法来执行项目, 现在是最佳时机。

Start by picking one project you want to move forward on. It could be one you identified in Chapter 5, when I asked you to make folders for each active project. It could alternatively be something you know you want to (or have to) get started on. The more uncertain, new, or challenging the project, the better.  
从选择一个你想继续推进的项目开始。它可能是您在第 5 章中确定的, 当时我要求您为每个活动项目创建文件夹。它也可以是你知道你想要 (或必须) 开始的事情。项目越不确定、越新或越具有挑战性, 就越好。

Make an outline with your goals, intentions, questions, and considerations for the project. Start by writing out anything already on your mind, and then peruse your PARA categories for related notes and Intermediate Packets. These could include points or takeaways from previously created notes, inspiration from models or examples you want to borrow from, or templates you can use to follow best practices.  
列出项目的目标、意图、问题和注意事项。首先写下您心中已有的任何内容, 然后仔细阅读您的 PARA 类别以查找相关的注释和中间包。这些可能包括以前创建的笔记中的要点或要点、模型或示例的灵感 你想借用, 或者你可以用来遵循最佳实践的模板。

Here are some useful questions to ask as you conduct your search:  
以下是您进行搜索时要问的一些有用问题:

*   Is there a book or article you could extract some excerpts from as inspiration?  
    有没有一本书或文章可以摘录一些作为灵感?
*   Are there websites that might have resources you could build upon?  
    是否有可能拥有您可以构建的资源的网站?
*   Are there podcasts by experts you could subscribe to and listen to while commuting or doing household chores?  
    是否有您可以在通勤或做家务时订阅和收听的专家播客?
*   Are there relevant IPs buried in other projects you’ve worked on in the past?  
    您过去从事的其他项目中是否埋藏着相关的 IP?

Some material you find will be very succinct and highly polished, while some might be quite rough. It doesn’t matter—your only goal is to get all the potentially usable material in one place. Move all the notes and IPs you might want to use into a new project folder.  
您发现的一些材料会非常简洁和高度抛光, 而有些材料可能非常粗糙。没关系——您唯一的目标是将所有可能可用的材料放在一个地方。将您可能想要使用的所有注释和 IP 移动到一个新的项目文件夹中。

Set a timer for a fixed period of time, such as fifteen or twenty minutes, and in one sitting see if you can complete a first pass on your project _using only the notes you’ve gathered in front of you_. No searching online, no browsing social media, and no opening multiple browser tabs that you swear you’re going to get to eventually. Only work with what you already have. This first pass could be a plan, an agenda, a proposal, a diagram, or some other format that turns your ideas into a tangible artifact.  
设置一个固定时间段的计时器, 例如十五或二十分钟, 然后坐下来看看您是否可以仅使用您面前收集的笔记来完成项目的第一遍。无需在线搜索, 无需浏览社交媒体, 也无需打开您发誓最终会打开的多个浏览器选项卡。只使用你已经拥有的东西。第一步可以是计划、议程、提案、图表或其他一些可以将您的想法变成有形工件的格式。

You might experience some FOMO—that inner Fear of Missing Out—that pushes you to seek out yet another morsel of information somewhere out there. You will probably be tempted to go off and “do more research,” but you are not _completing_ the entire project in one sitting. You are only creating the first iteration—a draft of your essay, a sketch of your app, a plan for your campaign. Ask yourself, “What is the smallest version of this I can produce to get useful feedback from others?”  
您可能会经历一些 FOMO(害怕错过), 这会促使您去外面的某个地方寻找另一条信息。你可能会想要离开并 “做更多的研究”, 但你并不能一口气完成整个项目。您只是在创建第一个迭代——您的论文草稿、 您的应用程序草图、您的活动计划。问问自己,“为了从他人那里获得有用的反馈, 我可以制作的最小版本是什么?”

If you find that you can’t complete the first iteration in one sitting, start by building a Hemingway Bridge to the next time you can work on it. List open questions, remaining to-dos, new avenues to explore, or people to consult. Share what you’ve produced with someone who can give you feedback while you’re away and save their comments in a new note in the same project folder. You can collect this feedback in a private conversation with a trusted colleague, or publicly on social media at full blast, or anywhere in between. Pick a venue for sharing that you feel comfortable with.  
如果您发现您无法一次完成第一次迭代, 请从建造一座海明威桥开始, 以便下次您可以继续工作。列出悬而未决的问题、待办事项、要探索的新途径或要咨询的人。与可以在您离开时为您提供反馈的人分享您制作的内容, 并将他们的评论保存在同一项目文件夹中的新笔记中。您可以通过与可信赖的同事的私下对话收集这些反馈, 也可以在社交媒体上公开收集这些反馈, 或者介于两者之间的任何地方。选择一个您觉得舒服的分享场所。

If you feel resistance to continuing with this project later, try Dialing Down the Scope. Drop the least important features, postpone the hardest decisions for later, or find someone to help you with the parts you’re least familiar with.  
如果您以后觉得不想继续这个项目, 请尝试缩小范围。放弃最不重要的功能, 推迟最艰难的决定, 或者找人帮助你处理你最不熟悉的部分。

Throughout every step of this process, be sure to keep notes on anything you learn or discover, or any new Intermediate Packets you might want to seek out. Once your biological brain is primed by this first pass through your notes, you’ll start to notice signs and clues related to it everywhere you look. Save those clues as notes as well! Once you’re finished with your first iteration, have gathered feedback, and collected a new set of notes to work with, you’ll be ready for whatever comes next.  
在这个过程的每一步中, 一定要记下你学到或发现的任何东西, 或者你可能想要寻找的任何新的中间包。一旦你的生物大脑为第一次浏览你的笔记做好准备, 你就会开始注意到与它相关的标志和线索。将这些线索也保存为笔记! 一旦您完成了第一次迭代、收集了反馈并收集了一组新的工作笔记, 您就可以为接下来的一切做好准备。

1.  [I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-008-backlink). For more insight into what I learned about the creative process from my father, I made a short documentary on his work and life called _Wayne Lacson Forte: On My Way To Me_.  
    我 。为了更深入地了解我从父亲那里学到的创作过程, 我制作了一部关于他的工作和生活的简短纪录片, 名为 Wayne Lacson Forte: 在通往我的路上。
2.  [II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-007-backlink). I first learned about the model of divergence and convergence from Design Thinking, an approach to creative problem-solving that emerged out of the Stanford Design School and was further popularized by the innovation consultancy IDEO starting in the 1980s and 1990s.  
    二。我首先从设计思维中了解到发散和融合模型, 这是一种创造性解决问题的方法, 起源于斯坦福设计学院, 并在 1980 年代和 1990 年代开始被创新咨询公司 IDEO 进一步推广。
3.  [III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-006-backlink). If your notes app syncs with your computer, this also means that you can disconnect from the Internet and still make progress, since you have all your notes saved on your hard drive.  
    三、如果您的笔记应用程序与您的计算机同步, 这也意味着您可以断开与 Internet 的连接并仍然取得进展, 因为您的所有笔记都保存在您的硬盘上。
4.  [IV](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-005-backlink). One way to think of this is to “end with the beginning in mind,” a clever rephrasing of author Stephen Covey’s classic advice to “begin with the end in mind.”  
    四、一种思考方式是 “以始为终”, 这是对作家斯蒂芬 · 柯维 (Stephen Covey) 的经典建议“以终为始” 的巧妙改写。

Chapter 9 The Essential Habits of Digital Organizers  
Chapter 9 数字组织者的基本习惯
---------------------------------------------------------------------------

> Habits reduce cognitive load and free up mental capacity, so you can allocate your attention to other tasks... It’s only by making the fundamentals of life easier that you can create the mental space needed for free thinking and creativity.  
> 习惯可以减轻认知负担并释放心智能力, 这样您就可以将注意力分配到其他任务上...... 只有让生活的基础变得更轻松, 您才能创造自由思考和创造力所需的心理空间。
> 
> —James Clear, author of _Atomic Habits_  
> ——James Clear,《原子习惯》的作者

Your Second Brain is a practical system for enhancing your productivity _and_ your creativity.  
Your Second Brain 是一个实用的系统, 可以提高您的工作效率和创造力。

While these domains are often seen as mutually exclusive opposites—one concrete and defined; the other abstract and open-ended—instead I see them as complementary. When we are organized and efficient, that creates space for creativity to arise. When we have confidence in our creative process, we don’t have to think about it as much, significantly reducing the background stress of constantly worrying whether we’re going in the right direction.  
虽然这些领域通常被视为相互排斥的对立面——一个具体而明确; 另一个是抽象的和开放式的——相反, 我认为它们是互补的。当我们井井有条且高效时, 就会为创造力的产生创造空间。当我们对自己的创作过程充满信心时, 我们就不必考虑太多, 从而大大减少了不断担心我们是否朝着正确的方向前进的背景压力。

This balance between order and creativity is something that we can build into our Second Brain intentionally. Like every system, a Second Brain needs regular maintenance. There is a certain level of organization that you want to maintain in your digital world, so that when you go there to get things done, your virtual workspaces support your productivity instead of interfering with it.  
这种秩序和创造力之间的平衡是我们可以有意构建到我们的第二大脑中的东西。像每个系统一样, a 第二大脑需要定期维护。您希望在您的数字世界中保持一定程度的组织, 这样当您去那里完成工作时, 您的虚拟工作空间可以支持您的工作效率而不是干扰它。

“Being organized” isn’t a personality trait you’re born with, nor is it merely a matter of finding the right apps or tools. Being organized is a habit—a repeated set of actions you take as you encounter, work with, and put information to use. If we’re constantly scrambling to find our notes, drafts, brainstorms, and sources, not only do we waste precious time, but we also sabotage our momentum. At each step of CODE, there are habits that can help us be more organized so that our creativity has space to arise.  
“有条理” 不是你与生俱来的人格特质, 也不仅仅是找到合适的应用程序或工具的问题。有条理是一种习惯——当你遇到、处理和使用信息时, 你会重复采取一系列行动。如果我们不断地争先恐后地寻找我们的笔记、草稿、头脑风暴和资源, 我们不仅会浪费宝贵的时间, 还会破坏我们的动力。在 CODE 的每一步, 都有一些习惯可以帮助我们更有条理, 让我们的创造力有空间出现。

### The Mise-en-Place Way to Sustainable Productivity  
可持续生产力的 Mise-en-Place 方式

Consider how chefs work in a commercial kitchen. They have incredibly high demands on both the quality _and_ quantity of their output. Every ingredient in every dish must be nearly perfect—one cold side or undercooked filet and the whole dish can be sent back, and the kitchen might have to produce hundreds of dishes on a busy night.  
想想厨师是如何在商业厨房工作的。他们对产品的质量和数量有着极高的要求。每道菜中的每一种配料都必须近乎完美——一个冷面或未煮熟的菲力牛排, 整道菜都可以送回, 而厨房在繁忙的夜晚可能不得不制作数百道菜。

This fundamental tension—between quality and quantity—is a tension we share as knowledge workers. We also must produce work to an extremely high standard, and we must do it fast, continuously, all year long. We are like sprinters who are also trying to run a marathon.  
这种基本的张力——质量和数量之间的张力——是我们作为知识工作者所共有的张力。我们还必须以极高的标准开展工作, 而且我们必须全年快速、连续地开展工作。我们就像短跑运动员一样, 也在努力跑马拉松。

Chefs have a particular system for accomplishing this daunting feat. It’s called _mise en place_, a culinary philosophy used in restaurants around the world. Developed in France starting in the late 1800s, mise en place is a step-by-step process for producing high-quality food efficiently. Chefs can never afford to stop the whole kitchen just so they can clean up. They learn to keep their workspace clean and organized _in the flow of the meals they are preparing_.  
厨师们有一个特殊的系统来完成这项艰巨的壮举。它被称为 mise en place, 这是一种在世界各地的餐厅中使用的烹饪哲学。 mise en place 从 1800 年代后期开始在法国开发, 是一个循序渐进的过程, 可以高效地生产优质食品。厨师们永远不能只 就让整个厨房停下来清理干净。他们学会在准备饭菜的过程中保持工作空间整洁有序。

In the kitchen, this means small habits like always putting the mixing spoon in the same place so they know where to find it next time; immediately wiping a knife clean after using it so it’s ready for the next cut; or laying out the ingredients in the order they’ll be used so that they serve as placeholders.  
在厨房里, 这意味着一些小习惯, 比如总是把搅拌勺放在同一个地方, 这样他们下次就知道在哪里可以找到它; 使用后立即将刀擦干净, 以便为下一次切割做好准备; 或者按照使用顺序排列成分, 以便它们充当占位符。

Chefs use mise en place—a philosophy and mindset embodied in a set of practical techniques—as their “external brain.”[1](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch09fn1) It gives them a way to externalize their thinking into their environment and automate the repetitive parts of cooking so they can focus completely on the creative parts.  
厨师们使用 mise en place——一种体现在一系列实用技术中的哲学和思维方式——作为他们的 “外部大脑”。 [1](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#ch09fn1) 它为他们提供了一种方法, 可以将他们的思维外化到他们的环境中, 并使烹饪的重复部分自动化, 这样他们就可以完全专注于创造性的部分。

We have a lot to learn as knowledge workers from the system of mise en place. We likewise have to contend with a deluge of tasks, under uncertain conditions, with tight deadlines. We also receive a constant stream of inputs and requests, have too little time to process them, and face many demands requiring simultaneous attention. For us as well, the only time we have available to maintain our systems is during the execution of our regular work.  
作为知识工作者, 我们可以从管理系统中学到很多东西。同样, 我们必须在不确定的条件下和紧迫的期限内应对大量任务。我们还收到源源不断的输入和请求, 没有足够的时间来处理它们, 并且面临许多需要同时关注的需求。对于我们来说, 我们唯一可以维护我们系统的时间是在执行我们的日常工作期间。

There’s no time that’s magically going to become available for you to stop everything and completely reorganize your digital world. It’s not likely that your manager is going to look kindly on you blocking off a whole day to “get caught up.” Your business won’t last long if you turn customers away because you’re “maintaining your systems.” It’s difficult to find the time to put the world on hold and catch your breath. We tend to notice our systems need maintenance only when they break down, which we then blame on our lack of self-discipline or our failure to be sufficiently productive.  
没有时间可以神奇地让你停止一切并彻底重组你的数字世界。你的经理不太可能会善意地看着你抽出一整天的时间 “赶上进度”。如果你因为“维护你的系统” 而拒绝客户, 你的生意就不会持续太久。很难找到时间让世界停下来喘口气。我们往往会注意到我们的系统只有在发生故障时才需要维护, 然后我们将其归咎于我们缺乏自律或我们未能足够高效。

Building a Second Brain is not just about downloading a new piece of software to get organized at one point in time; it is about adopting a dynamic, flexible system and set of habits to continually access what we need without throwing our environment (and mind) into chaos.  
构建第二大脑不仅仅是下载一个新软件来在某个时间点组织起来; 这是关于 采用一个动态、灵活的系统和一套习惯来持续访问我们需要的东西, 而不会使我们的环境 (和思想) 陷入混乱。

It’s not enough to have inner discipline. We also need to follow an _outer_ discipline—a system of principles and behaviors—to channel our energies, thoughts, and emotions productively. A system that adds some structure to the constantly changing flux of information that we interact with every day.  
仅有内在纪律是不够的。我们还需要遵循一种外在的纪律——一套原则和行为体系——来有效地引导我们的能量、思想和情感。一个为我们每天与之交互的不断变化的信息流添加一些结构的系统。

In this chapter, I will introduce you to three kinds of habits that can be integrated into your routine to ensure your Second Brain remains functional and relevant. Each of these habits creates boundaries—of time, space, and intention—around the states of mind that you want to protect and promote in your life. These boundaries tell you what you should be focusing on, and just as importantly, what you should ignore. The three habits most important to your Second Brain include:  
在本章中, 我将向您介绍三种可以融入日常生活的习惯, 以确保您的第二大脑保持功能和相关性。这些习惯中的每一个都围绕着你想要在生活中保护和促进的心态创造了时间、空间和意图的界限。这些界限告诉你应该关注什么, 同样重要的是, 你应该忽略什么。对您的第二大脑最重要的三个习惯包括:

*   **Project Checklists:** Ensure you start and finish your projects in a consistent way, making use of past work.  
    项目清单: 确保您以一致的方式开始和完成您的项目, 利用过去的工作。
*   **Weekly and Monthly Reviews:** Periodically review your work and life and decide if you want to change anything.  
    每周和每月回顾: 定期回顾你的工作和生活, 并决定是否要改变什么。
*   **Noticing Habits:** Notice small opportunities to edit, highlight, or move notes to make them more discoverable for your future self.  
    注意习惯: 注意编辑、突出显示或移动笔记的小机会, 让未来的自己更容易发现它们。

You can think of these habits as the “maintenance schedule” of your Second Brain. Just like you have a maintenance schedule for your car, which advises you to regularly change the oil, rotate the tires, and change the air filters, your Second Brain occasionally needs a tune-up to ensure it’s in good working order.  
您可以将这些习惯视为第二大脑的 “维护计划”。就像您的汽车有一个维护计划, 建议您定期更换机油、换轮胎和更换空气滤清器一样, 您的第二大脑偶尔也需要进行调整以确保其处于良好的工作状态。

Let’s explore these habits one at a time.  
让我们一次一个地探索这些习惯。

### The Project Checklist Habit: The Key to Starting Your Knowledge Flywheel  
项目清单习惯: 启动知识飞轮的关键

At the most basic level, knowledge work is about taking in information and then turning it into results. All day, every day we are consuming and then producing. You don’t need special training to perform these activities, and you certainly don’t need a Second Brain.  
在最基本的层面上, 知识工作就是获取信息, 然后将其转化为结果。我们每天都在消费, 然后生产。你不需要特殊的训练来进行这些活动, 你当然不需要第二大脑。

What most people are missing, however, is a feedback loop—a way to “recycle” the knowledge that was created as part of past efforts so it can be used in future ones as well. This is how investors think about money: they don’t get the profits from one investment and immediately spend it all. They reinvest it back into other investments, creating a flywheel so their money builds on itself over time.  
然而, 大多数人缺少的是反馈循环——一种 “回收” 作为过去努力的一部分而创造的知识的方法, 以便它也可以用于未来的努力。这就是投资者对金钱的看法: 他们不会从一项投资中获得利润并立即花光所有。他们将其重新投资到其他投资中, 形成一个飞轮, 这样他们的钱就会随着时间的推移自行积累。

This is exactly how I want you to treat your attention—as an asset that gets invested and produces a return, which in turn can be reinvested back into other ventures. This is how you can ensure your knowledge grows and compounds over time like a high-yield asset. Like investing a small amount in the stock market every month, your investments of attention can likewise compound as your knowledge grows and your ideas connect and build on each other.  
这正是我希望你对待你的注意力的方式——作为一种可以投资并产生回报的资产, 反过来又可以将其再投资回其他企业。这就是您如何确保您的知识像高收益资产一样随着时间的推移而增长和复合。就像每个月在股票市场上投资少量一样, 随着知识的增长以及想法的相互联系和相互借鉴, 您的注意力投资同样会增加。

If you look closely, there are two key moments in this process of recycling knowledge. Two places where the paths diverge, and you have the chance to do something different than you’ve done before.  
如果仔细观察, 在这个回收知识的过程中有两个关键时刻。路径分叉的两个地方, 你有机会做一些与以前不同的事情。

Those two moments are when a project starts, and when it finishes. For the former, I’ll introduce you to the Project Kickoff Checklist, and for the latter, the Project Completion Checklist.  
这两个时刻是项目开始和结束的时间。对于前者, 我将向您介绍项目启动清单, 对于后者, 我将向您介绍项目完成清单。

#### Checklist #1: Project Kickoff 清单 #1: 项目启动

Before they taxi onto the runway and take off, airline pilots run through a “preflight checklist” that tells them everything they need to check or do. It ensures they complete all the necessary steps without having to rely on their unreliable brains.  
在他们滑行到跑道并起飞之前, 航空公司飞行员会检查 “飞行前检查清单”, 告诉他们需要检查或做的一切。它确保他们完成所有必要的步骤, 而不必依赖他们不可靠的大脑。

The way most people launch projects, in contrast, can be described as “haphazardly.” They might look through their existing notes and files for any information that might be relevant, or they might not. They might talk to their colleagues about any lessons from past experience, or they might not. They might create a plan to guide their progress, or they might not. The successful start of the project is more or less left to chance.  
相比之下, 大多数人启动项目的方式可以用 “随意” 来形容。他们可能会查看现有的笔记和 文件, 寻找任何可能相关的信息, 也可能不相关。他们可能会与同事谈论过去经验中的任何教训, 也可能不会。他们可能会制定计划来指导他们的进步, 也可能不会。该项目的成功启动或多或少是偶然的。

In Chapter 5 we saw how work is becoming ever more project-centric. Every goal, collaboration, or assignment we take on can be defined as a project, which gives it shape, focus, and a sense of direction. If we consider that these projects are our biggest investments of attention, it’s worth adding a little bit of structure to how we start them. This is where the Project Kickoff Checklist comes in.  
在第 5 章中, 我们看到了工作如何变得越来越以项目为中心。我们承担的每一个目标、协作或任务都可以定义为一个项目, 它赋予它形状、重点和方向感。如果我们认为这些项目是我们最大的注意力投资, 那么在我们如何启动它们时增加一点结构是值得的。这就是项目启动清单的用武之地。

Here’s my own checklist: 这是我自己的清单:

1.  **Capture** my current thinking on the project.  
    捕捉我当前对该项目的想法。
2.  **Review** folders (or tags) that might contain relevant notes.  
    查看可能包含相关注释的文件夹 (或标签)。
3.  **Search** for related terms across all folders.  
    在所有文件夹中搜索相关术语。
4.  **Move** (or tag) relevant notes to the project folder.  
    将相关注释移动 (或标记) 到项目文件夹中。
5.  **Create** an outline of collected notes and plan the project.  
    创建收集的笔记大纲并计划项目。

1. **Capture my current thinking on the project.** I often find that the moment a project begins to form in my mind, I start to have ideas and opinions about it. I like to start by creating a blank note and doing a brainstorm of any thoughts that come to mind. This first note is then placed inside a new project folder dedicated to storing all the notes I’ll be creating related to it.  
1. 捕捉我当前对项目的想法。我经常发现, 当一个项目开始在我脑海中形成的那一刻, 我就开始对它产生想法和意见。我喜欢先创建一个空白笔记, 然后对脑海中浮现的任何想法进行头脑风暴。然后将第一个笔记放在一个新的项目文件夹中, 该文件夹专门用于存储我将创建的所有与之相关的笔记。

This step can and should be messy: I pour out all my random musings, potential approaches, links to other ideas or topics, or reminders of people to talk to.  
这一步可以而且应该是混乱的: 我会倾诉我所有的随机想法、可能的方法、与其他想法或主题的链接, 或者提醒可以与之交谈的人。

Here are some questions I use to prompt this initial brainstorm:  
以下是我用来提示这次初步头脑风暴的一些问题:

*   What do I already know about this project?  
    关于这个项目我已经知道了什么?
*   What don’t I know that I need to find out?  
    我不知道我需要找出什么?
*   What is my goal or intention?  
    我的目标或意图是什么?
*   Who can I talk to who might provide insights?  
    我可以与谁交谈, 谁可以提供见解?
*   What can I read or listen to for relevant ideas?  
    我可以阅读或收听哪些相关想法?

Anything that comes to mind from these questions I write down in my starting note. I prefer using bullet points so the information is compact and can easily be moved around.  
从这些问题中想到的任何事情, 我都会写在我的开始笔记中。我更喜欢使用项目符号, 这样信息紧凑并且可以轻松移动。

2. **Review folders (or tags) that might contain relevant notes.** Second, I look through any existing folders that might contain information relevant to the new project I’m starting, including related templates, outlines, and outtakes from previous projects. PARA and Progressive Summarization really come in handy here: I already have a variety of folders, each containing a curated set of notes, highlighted and summarized so I can rapidly recall what they’re about. I choose a handful of folders that seem most relevant to what I’m starting, including in projects, areas, resources, and archives. Then I quickly scan any notes inside that look interesting, taking care to keep the momentum going so I don’t get bogged down anywhere. Now is not the time to go on tangents that will only distract me from moving forward.  
2. 查看可能包含相关注释的文件夹 (或标签)。其次, 我查看所有可能包含与我正在开始的新项目相关的信息的现有文件夹, 包括相关模板、大纲和以前项目的摘录。 PARA 和 Progressive Summarization 在这里真的派上了用场: 我已经有各种各样的文件夹, 每个文件夹都包含一组精选的笔记, 突出显示和总结, 这样我就可以快速回忆起它们的内容。我选择了一些看起来与我开始的内容最相关的文件夹, 包括项目、区域、资源和档案。然后我快速浏览里面所有看起来有趣的笔记, 注意保持势头, 这样我就不会在任何地方陷入困境。现在不是走岔路的时候, 那只会分散我前进的注意力。

3. **Search for related terms across all folders.** The third step is to perform searches for any notes I might have missed. Sometimes there are valuable ideas buried in unexpected places, which I may not find through browsing alone.  
3. 在所有文件夹中搜索相关术语。第三步是搜索我可能遗漏的任何笔记。有时在意想不到的地方埋藏着有价值的想法, 我可能无法通过单独浏览找到它们。

This is where the Curator’s Perspective I used when I first captured the content really pays off—because each and every note in my Second Brain was deliberately chosen, I am able to search through a collection of exclusively high-quality notes free of fluff and filler. This is in stark contrast to searching the open Internet, which is full of distracting ads, misleading headlines, superficial content, and pointless controversy, all of which can throw me off track.  
这就是我在第一次捕捉内容时使用的策展人视角真正得到回报的地方——因为我的第二大脑中的每一个笔记都是有意选择的, 我能够搜索 a 完全免费的高质量笔记集合绒毛和填料。这与在开放的互联网上搜索形成鲜明对比, 互联网上充斥着分散注意力的广告、误导性的标题、肤浅的内容和毫无意义的争论, 所有这些都会让我偏离正轨。

I run a series of searches for terms related to the new project, scanning the results and quickly jumping into any note that seems relevant. Progressive Summarization helps here too, enabling me to zoom into and out of notes without having to absorb their full contents.  
我对与新项目相关的术语进行了一系列搜索, 扫描结果并快速跳转到任何看起来相关的注释。渐进式总结在这里也有帮助, 使我能够放大和缩小笔记, 而不必吸收它们的全部内容。

4. **Move (or tag) relevant notes to the project folder.** Fourth, any notes found in the previous two steps I move to the project folder, titled after the name of the new project I’m starting. Alternatively, depending on the capabilities of your notes app, you can also tag or link any relevant notes with the project, so you don’t have to move them from their original location. The important thing isn’t where a note is located, but whether you can reference it quickly while staying focused on the project at hand.  
4. 将相关注释移动 (或标记) 到项目文件夹中。第四, 我将在前两个步骤中找到的所有注释移至项目文件夹, 以我开始的新项目的名称命名。或者, 根据您的笔记应用程序的功能, 您还可以标记或链接任何与项目相关的笔记, 这样您就不必将它们从原来的位置移动。重要的不是笔记的位置, 而是您是否可以在专注于手头项目的同时快速参考它。

5. **Create an outline of collected notes and plan the project.** Finally, it’s time to pull together the material I’ve gathered and create an outline (an Archipelago of Ideas) for the project. My goal is to end up not just with a loose collection of ideas. It is to formulate a logical progression of steps that make it clear what I should do next.  
5. 创建收集的笔记大纲并计划项目。最后, 是时候整理我收集的材料并为项目创建大纲 (想法群岛) 了。我的目标是最终不只是一堆松散的想法。它是制定一个合乎逻辑的步骤进展, 使我清楚我下一步应该做什么。

The form this outline takes depends on the nature of the project. If it is a piece of writing such as an essay or report, the outline might be the main points or headings I want to include in the final piece. If it is a document outlining a collaborative project with colleagues or outside contractors, the outline might include the objectives we’re working toward and tentative responsibilities for each person. If it is a trip I’m planning to take, it might be a packing list and itinerary.  
该大纲采用的形式取决于项目的性质。如果它是一篇文章, 如论文或报告, 大纲可能是我想在最后一篇文章中包含的要点或标题。如果它是概述与同事或外部承包商的合作项目的文件, 则该大纲可能包括我们正在努力实现的目标以及每个人的暂定职责。如果这是我计划进行的旅行, 它可能是装箱单和行程单。

The important thing to remember as you move through this checklist is that you are making a plan for how to tackle the project, _not executing the project itself_. You should think of this five-step checklist as a first pass, taking no more than twenty to thirty minutes. You’re only trying to get a sense of what kind of material you already have in your Second Brain. Once you do, you’ll have a much better sense of how much time it will take, which knowledge or resources you’ll need access to, and what your challenges will likely be.  
在浏览此清单时要记住的重要一点是, 您正在制定如何处理项目的计划, 而不是执行项目本身。您应该将这个五步检查表视为第一步, 所用时间不超过 20 到 30 分钟。你只是想了解你的第二大脑中已经拥有什么样的材料。一旦你这样做了, 你就会更好地了解这需要多少时间, 你需要访问哪些知识或资源, 以及你可能面临的挑战。

I encourage you to use my kickoff checklist as a starting point and customize it over time as you understand how it fits into your own context. Depending on your profession or industry, you might need more or less formality, more or less time for a first pass, and more or fewer people involved. Here are some other options for actions you might want to include in your own version:  
我鼓励您使用我的启动清单作为起点, 并随着时间的推移对其进行自定义, 因为您了解它如何适合您自己的环境。根据您的职业或行业, 您可能需要或多或少的正式手续、或多或少的第一次通过时间以及或多或少的参与人员。以下是您可能希望包含在您自己的版本中的一些其他操作选项:

*   **Answer premortem**[I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-004) **questions:** What do you want to learn? What is the greatest source of uncertainty or most important question you want to answer? What is most likely to fail?  
    回答事前 [I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-004) 问题: 你想学什么? 最大的不确定性来源或您想回答的最重要的问题是什么? 什么最有可能失败?
*   **Communicate with stakeholders:** Explain to your manager, colleagues, clients, customers, shareholders, contractors, etc., what the project is about and why it matters.  
    与利益相关者沟通: 向您的经理、同事、客户、客户、股东、承包商等解释项目的内容及其重要性。
*   **Define success criteria:** What needs to happen for this project to be considered successful? What are the minimum results you need to achieve, or the “stretch goals” you’re striving for?  
    定义成功标准: 这个项目需要发生什么才能被认为是成功的? 您需要达到的最低结果是什么, 或者您正在努力争取的 “延伸目标” 是什么?
*   **Have an official kickoff:** Schedule check-in calls, make a budget and timeline, and write out the goals and objectives to make sure everyone is informed, aligned, and clear on what is expected of them. I find that doing an official kickoff is useful even if it’s a solo project!  
    正式启动: 安排签到电话, 制定预算和时间表, 并写下目标和目标, 以确保每个人都了解情况、保持一致并清楚他们的 期望是什么。我发现正式启动很有用, 即使它是一个单独的项目!

#### Checklist #2: Project Completion 清单 #2: 项目完成

Now let’s take a look at the Project Completion Checklist, the other side of the equation.  
现在让我们看一下项目完成清单, 等式的另一边。

The completion of a project is a very special time in a knowledge worker’s life because it’s one of the rare moments when something actually ends. Part of what makes modern work so challenging is that nothing ever seems to finish. It’s exhausting, isn’t it? Calls and meetings seem to stretch on forever, which means we rarely get to celebrate a clear-cut victory and start fresh. This is one of the best reasons to keep our projects small: so that we get to feel a fulfilling sense of completion as often as possible.  
项目的完成是知识工作者生活中非常特殊的时刻, 因为它是某些事情真正结束的罕见时刻之一。现代工作之所以如此具有挑战性, 部分原因在于似乎没有什么事情可以完成。这很累, 不是吗? 电话和会议似乎永远持续下去, 这意味着我们很少能够庆祝明确的胜利并重新开始。这是让我们的项目保持小型化的最佳理由之一: 这样我们才能尽可能多地感受到完成的成就感。

We don’t want to limit ourselves to merely celebrating the end of a project. We want to learn from the experience and document any thinking that could add value in the future. This is where the Project Completion Checklist is essential. It’s a series of steps you can take to decide if there are any reusable knowledge assets worth keeping, before archiving the rest. The only way that the Kickoff Checklist we just looked at will be feasible is if you’ve previously taken the time to save and preserve material from past projects.  
我们不想将自己局限于仅仅庆祝项目的结束。我们希望从经验中学习并记录任何可以在未来增加价值的想法。这是项目完成清单必不可少的地方。这是您可以采取的一系列步骤, 以确定是否有任何值得保留的可重用知识资产, 然后再归档其余部分。我们刚刚看到的启动清单唯一可行的方法是, 如果您以前花时间保存和保存过去项目的材料。

Here’s my checklist: 这是我的清单:

1.  **Mark** project as complete in task manager or project management app.  
    在任务管理器或项目管理应用程序中将项目标记为完成。
2.  **Cross out** the associated project goal and move to “Completed” section.  
    划掉相关的项目目标并移至 “已完成” 部分。
3.  **Review** Intermediate Packets and move them to other folders.  
    查看中间数据包并将它们移动到其他文件夹。
4.  **Move** project to archives across all platforms.  
    跨所有平台将项目移动到存档。
5.  **If project is becoming inactive:** add a current status note to the project folder before archiving.  
    If project is becoming inactive: 在归档之前将当前状态注释添加到项目文件夹。

1. **Mark project as complete in task manager or project management app.** This is the first step is making sure the project is in fact finished. Often there are a few lingering tasks needed to completely wrap it up—such as getting final approvals, filing paperwork, or disseminating the project deliverables—which is why I start by looking at my task manager. A task manager is a dedicated app for keeping track of pending actions, like a digital to-do list.[II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-003)  
1. 在任务管理器或项目管理应用程序中将项目标记为完成。这是确保项目实际完成的第一步。通常需要完成一些挥之不去的任务才能将其完全结束——例如获得最终批准、归档文件或传播项目可交付成果——这就是为什么我首先查看我的任务经理。任务管理器是一个专门用于跟踪未决操作的应用程序, 例如数字待办事项列表。 [II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-003)

If all the tasks I find there are done, I can mark it as complete and move on to the following steps.  
如果我找到的所有任务都已完成, 我可以将其标记为完成并继续执行以下步骤。

2. **Cross out the associated project goal and move to “Completed” section.** Each project I work on usually has a corresponding goal. I keep all my goals in a single digital note, sorted from short-term goals for the next year to long-term goals for years to come.  
2. 划掉相关的项目目标并转到 “已完成” 部分。我从事的每个项目通常都有一个相应的目标。我把我所有的目标都保存在一个数字笔记中, 从明年的短期目标到未来几年的长期目标。

I like to take a moment and reflect on whether the goal I initially set for this project panned out. If I successfully achieved it, what factors led to that success? How can I repeat or double down on those strengths? If I fell short, what happened? What can I learn or change to avoid making the same mistakes next time? The amount of time you spend thinking about these questions depends on the size of the project. A massive team endeavor might justify hours of in-depth analysis, while a small personal side project might deserve only a few minutes of reflection.  
我喜欢花点时间反思一下我最初为这个项目设定的目标是否实现了。如果我成功实现了它, 是什么因素导致了成功? 我怎样才能重复或加倍发挥这些优势? 如果我失败了, 会发生什么? 我可以学习或改变什么以避免下次犯同样的错误? 您花在思考这些问题上的时间取决于项目的规模。一个巨大的团队努力可能需要数小时的深入分析, 而一个小的个人项目可能只需要几分钟的思考。

I also like to cross out the goal and move it to a different section called “Completed.” Any time I need some motivation, I can look through this list and be reminded of all the meaningful goals I’ve achieved in the past. It doesn’t matter if the goal is big or small—keeping an inventory of your victories and successes is a wonderful use for your Second Brain.  
我还喜欢划掉目标并将其移至名为 “已完成” 的不同部分。每当我需要一些动力时, 我都可以浏览这份清单, 并想起我过去实现的所有有意义的目标。目标是大是小并不重要——记录你的胜利和成功对你的第二大脑来说是一个很好的用途。

3. **Review Intermediate Packets and move them to other folders.** Third, I’ll look through the folder for the completed project to identify any Intermediate Packets I created that could be repurposed in the future. This could include a web-page design to be used as a template for future sites, an agenda for a one-on-one performance review, or a series of interview questions that might come in handy for future hires.  
3. 查看中间数据包并将它们移动到其他文件夹。第三, 我将查看已完成项目的文件夹, 以确定我创建的任何可以在未来重新利用的中间数据包。这可能包括用作未来网站模板的网页设计、一对一绩效评估的议程, 或一系列可能对未来雇员派上用场的面试问题。

It takes a certain lens to see each of these documents and files not as disposable, but as tangible by-products of quality thinking. Much of our work gets repeated over time with slight variations. If you can start your thinking where you left off last time, you’ll be far ahead compared to starting from zero every time.  
需要一定的镜头才能看到这些文档和文件中的每一个都不是一次性的, 而是质量思维的有形副产品。随着时间的推移, 我们的大部分工作都会重复进行, 但会有细微的变化。如果你能从上次停下来的地方开始思考, 那么与每次都从零开始相比, 你会遥遥领先。

Any IPs I decide could be relevant to another project, I move to that project’s folder. The same goes for notes relevant to areas or resources. This is a forgiving decision, and it’s okay if you don’t catch every single one. The full contents of everything you archive away will always show up in future searches, so you don’t have to worry that anything will be lost.  
我决定的任何 IP 可能与另一个项目相关, 我移动到该项目的文件夹。与区域或资源相关的注释也是如此。这是一个宽容的决定, 如果你没有抓住每一个也没关系。您存档的所有内容的完整内容将始终显示在以后的搜索中, 因此您不必担心会丢失任何内容。

4. **Move project to archives across all platforms.** Fourth, it’s time to move the project folder to the archives in my notes app, as well as any other platforms I used during the project. For me, this usually includes my computer’s documents folder and my cloud storage drive.  
4. 将项目移动到所有平台的档案中。第四, 是时候将项目文件夹移动到我的笔记应用程序以及我在项目期间使用的任何其他平台的档案中了。对我来说, 这通常包括我电脑的文档文件夹和我的云存储驱动器。

This move ensures that your list of active projects doesn’t get cluttered with old, obsolete stuff from the past while also preserving every bit of material just in case it unexpectedly becomes relevant in the future.  
此举确保您的活动项目列表不会被过去的旧的、过时的东西弄得乱七八糟, 同时还保留每一点材料, 以防它在未来意外变得相关。

5. **If project is becoming inactive: add a current status note to the project folder before archiving.** The fifth step applies only if the project is getting canceled, postponed, or put on hold instead of completed. I still want to archive it so it’s out of sight, but in this special case, there’s one final action I take.  
5. 如果项目变为非活动状态: 在存档前将当前状态注释添加到项目文件夹。第五步仅适用于项目被取消、推迟或搁置而不是完成的情况。我仍然想将其存档以使其不可见, 但在这种特殊情况下, 我将采取最后一项措施。

I add a new note to the project folder titled “Current status,” and jot down a few comments so I can pick it back up in the future. For example, in a few bullet points I might describe the last actions I took, details on why it was postponed or canceled, who was working on it and what role they played, and any lessons or best practices learned. This Hemingway Bridge gives me the confidence to put the project on ice knowing I can bring it back to life anytime.  
我在项目文件夹中添加了一个名为 “当前状态” 的新注释, 并记下了一些评论, 以便将来可以取回它。例如, 在几个要点中, 我可能会描述我最近采取的行动、推迟或取消原因的详细信息、谁在从事这项工作以及他们扮演什么角色, 以及学到的任何教训或最佳实践。这座海明威大桥让我有信心将这个项目搁置一旁, 因为我知道我可以随时让它恢复生机。

I’ve been amazed that by being honest with myself about when a project has stalled and taking these few minutes to download my current thinking on it, I can often pick it back up months or even years later with minimal effort. It’s very empowering to realize you can put a project in “cold storage” and let go of the mental toll of having to keep it in mind. It’s tremendously comforting to know that I don’t need to make constant progress on everything all the time.  
令我惊讶的是, 通过对自己诚实地了解项目何时停滞并花几分钟下载我当前的想法, 我通常可以在数月甚至数年后以最小的努力将其恢复。意识到您可以将一个项目放入 “冷藏库” 并摆脱必须牢记它的精神损失, 这是非常有力量的。知道我不需要一直在每件事上不断取得进步, 这让我感到非常欣慰。

Here are some other items you can include on your Project Completion Checklist. I encourage you to personalize it for your own needs:  
以下是您可以包含在项目完成清单中的其他一些项目。我鼓励您根据自己的需要对其进行个性化设置:

*   **Answer postmortem questions:** What did you learn? What did you do well? What could you have done better? What can you improve for next time?  
    回答事后分析问题: 你学到了什么? 你做了什么好事? 你可以做得更好吗? 下次可以改进什么?
*   **Communicate with stakeholders:** Notify your manager, colleagues, clients, customers, shareholders, contractors, etc., that the project is complete and what the outcomes were.  
    与利益相关者沟通: 通知你的经理、同事、客户、客户、股东、承包商等, 项目已经完成, 结果如何。
*   **Evaluate success criteria:** Were the objectives of the project achieved? Why or why not? What was the return on investment?  
    评估成功标准: 项目的目标是否实现? 为什么或者为什么不? 投资回报是多少?
*   **Officially close out the project and celebrate:** Send any last emails, invoices, receipts, feedback forms, or documents, and celebrate your accomplishments with your team or collaborators so you receive the feeling of fulfillment for all the effort you put in.  
    正式结束项目并庆祝: 发送任何最后的电子邮件、发票、收据、反馈表或文件, 并与你的团队或合作者一起庆祝你的成就, 这样你就会对你付出的所有努力感到满足。

The first pass on your Project Completion Checklist should be completed in even less time than the Project Kickoff Checklist—no more than ten or fifteen minutes to grab any stand-alone materials and insights. Since you don’t know for sure that any of this material will ever be useful again, you should minimize how much extra time and attention you invest in it. Put in just enough effort that your future self will be able to decide if the material is relevant to their needs. If it is, then they can decide in that moment whether to invest the effort to further organize and distill it.  
项目完成检查表的第一遍应该比项目启动检查表的时间更短——不超过十到十五分钟来获取任何独立的材料和见解。由于您不确定这些材料中的任何一个是否会再次有用, 因此您应该尽量减少投入在其中的额外时间和注意力。付出足够的努力, 你未来的自己将能够决定这些材料是否与他们的需求相关。如果是, 那么他们可以在那一刻决定是否投入精力进一步组织和提炼它。

The purpose of using project checklists isn’t to make the way you work rigid and formulaic. It is to help you start and finish projects cleanly and decisively, so you don’t have “orphaned” commitments that linger on with no end in sight. Think of these checklists as scaffolding—a supporting structure that ensures what you’re building can stand on its own. Just as scaffolding eventually gets taken down, these habits will get absorbed into the way you think and become completely second nature. You won’t even consider starting something new without querying your Second Brain to see if there is any material you can reuse.  
使用项目清单的目的不是让你的工作方式变得死板和公式化。它是为了帮助您干净利落、果断地开始和完成项目, 这样您就不会有 “孤立的” 承诺, 这些承诺挥之不去, 看不到尽头。将这些清单视为脚手架——一种确保您正在构建的内容能够独立站立的支撑结构。正如脚手架最终会被拆除一样, 这些习惯也会被吸收到你的思维方式中, 并完全成为第二天性。如果不询问你的第二大脑, 看看是否有任何材料可以重复使用, 你甚至不会考虑开始新事物。

### The Review Habit: Why You Should Batch Process Your Notes (and How Often)  
复习习惯: 为什么你应该批处理你的笔记 (以及多久处理一次)

Next let’s talk about Weekly and Monthly Reviews.  
接下来我们来说说周评和月评。

The practice of conducting a “Weekly Review” was pioneered by executive coach and author David Allen in his influential book _Getting Things Done_.[III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-002) He described a Weekly Review as a regular check-in, performed once a week, in which you intentionally reset and review your work and life. Allen recommends using a Weekly Review to write down any new to-dos, review your active projects, and decide on priorities for the upcoming week.  
进行 “每周回顾” 的做法是由执行教练和作家大卫 · 艾伦在他颇具影响力的著作《把事情做好》中开创的。 [III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-002) 他将 Weekly Review 描述为定期检查, 每周执行一次, 您有意识地重新设置和检查您的工作和生活。艾伦建议使用每周回顾来写下任何新的待办事项, 回顾你的活动项目, 并决定下一周的优先事项。

I suggest adding one more step: review the notes you’ve created over the past week, give them succinct titles that tell you what’s inside, and sort them into the appropriate PARA folders. Most notes apps have an “inbox” of some kind where new notes collect until they’re ready to be reviewed. This “batch processing” takes only seconds per note, and you can complete it within a few minutes.  
我建议再添加一个步骤: 回顾你在过去一周创建的笔记, 给它们起一个简洁的标题, 告诉你里面有什么, 然后将它们分类到适当的 PARA 文件夹中。大多数笔记应用程序都有一个某种类型的 “收件箱”, 用于收集新笔记, 直到它们准备好接受审查。这种“批处理” 每个音符只需要几秒钟, 你可以在几分钟内完成。

Let’s dive into the details and see how Weekly and Monthly Reviews can help you maintain your Second Brain in a state of readiness for whatever arrives at your doorstep.  
让我们深入了解细节, 看看每周和每月回顾如何帮助您保持第二大脑处于准备状态, 以应对任何到达您家门口的事情。

#### A Weekly Review Template: Reset to Avoid Overwhelm  
每周回顾模板: 重置以避免不知所措

Here is my own Weekly Review Checklist, which I usually complete every three to seven days depending on how busy a given week is. The point isn’t to follow a rigid schedule, but to make it a habit to empty my inboxes and clear my digital workspaces on a regular basis to keep them from getting overwhelmed. I keep this checklist on a digital sticky note on my computer, so I can easily refer to it.  
这是我自己的每周回顾清单, 我通常每三到七天完成一次, 具体取决于给定一周的忙碌程度。关键不是要遵循严格的时间表, 而是要养成定期清空 my 收件箱并清理我的数字工作区的习惯, 以免它们变得不堪重负。我将这份清单保存在我电脑上的数字便条上, 这样我就可以很容易地参考它。

1.  Clear my email inbox. 清除我的电子邮件收件箱。
2.  Check my calendar. 检查我的日历。
3.  Clear my computer desktop. 清理我的电脑桌面。
4.  Clear my notes inbox. 清除我的笔记收件箱。
5.  Choose my tasks for the week.  
    选择我本周的任务。

1. **Clear my email inbox.** I start by clearing my email inbox of any emails lingering from the past week. I don’t usually have time to do this during the week in the rush of my other priorities, but I’ve found that if I let messages accumulate from one week to the next, it makes it hard to figure out what’s new and requires action and what’s left over from the past.  
1. 清除我的电子邮件收件箱。我首先清除我的电子邮件收件箱中过去一周遗留下来的所有电子邮件。我通常没有时间在一周内忙于处理其他优先事项, 但我发现如果我让消息从一个星期累积到下一个星期, 就很难弄清楚什么是新的和需要的行动以及过去遗留下来的东西。

Any action items I find get saved in my task manager, and any notes I capture get saved in my notes app.  
我找到的任何行动项目都会保存在我的任务管理器中, 我捕获的任何笔记都会保存在我的笔记应用程序中。

2. **Check my calendar.** Next, I check my calendar. This is the landscape of my week, showing me the meetings and appointments I need to make room for. I typically look at the last couple of weeks in case there’s anything I need to follow up on, and the upcoming couple of weeks in case there’s anything I need to prepare for.  
2. 检查我的日历。接下来, 我检查我的日历。这是我一周的风景, 向我展示了我需要腾出空间的会议和约会。我通常会查看过去几周, 以防有任何我需要跟进的事情, 以及接下来的几周, 以防有任何我需要准备的事情。

Once again, anything I need to act on gets saved in my task manager, and any notes get captured in my notes app.  
再一次, 我需要做的任何事情都会保存在我的任务管理器中, 所有笔记都会被记录在我的笔记应用程序中。

3. **Clear my computer desktop.** Next, I clear the files that have accumulated on my computer desktop. I’ve found that if I let them accumulate week after week, eventually my digital environment gets so cluttered that I can’t think straight.  
3. 清空我的电脑桌面。接下来, 我清除电脑桌面上堆积的文件。我发现, 如果我让它们一周又一周地积累, 最终我的数字环境会变得非常混乱, 以至于我无法理清思路。

Any files potentially relevant to my projects, areas, or resources get moved to the appropriate PARA folders in my computer’s file system.  
任何可能与我的项目、区域或资源相关的文件都将移动到我计算机文件系统中相应的 PARA 文件夹中。

4. **Clear my notes inbox.** By the time I get to the fourth step, the inbox in my notes app is chock-full of interesting tidbits from the previous three steps—from my email, calendar, and computer desktop. Plus all the other notes I’ve collected over the course of the preceding week, which usually totals between five and fifteen new notes in an average week.  
4. 清除我的笔记收件箱。当我进行到第四步时, 我的笔记应用程序的收件箱里塞满了前三个步骤的有趣花絮——来自我的电子邮件、日历和电脑桌面。加上我在前一周收集的所有其他笔记, 平均每周通常总共有五到十五篇新笔记。

At this point I’ll batch process them all at once, making quick, intuitive decisions about which of the PARA folders each note might be relevant to, and creating new folders as needed. There is no “correct” location for a given note, and search is incredibly effective, so I put it in the first place that occurs to me.  
此时, 我将一次性对它们进行批处理, 快速、直观地决定每个笔记可能与哪些 PARA 文件夹相关, 并根据需要创建新文件夹。给定的笔记没有 “正确” 的位置, 搜索非常有效, 所以我把它放在我想到的第一位。

You’ll notice that this is the only step in my Weekly Review that is directly related to my digital notes. It is a simple and practical process of going through my notes inbox, giving each note an informative title, and moving them into the appropriate PARA folders. I don’t highlight or summarize them. I don’t try to understand or absorb their contents. I don’t worry about all the topics they could potentially relate to.  
你会注意到这是我的每周回顾中唯一与我的数字笔记直接相关的步骤。通过我的笔记收件箱, 为每条笔记赋予一个信息丰富的标题, 并将它们移动到适当的 PARA 文件夹中, 这是一个简单实用的过程。我没有强调或总结它们。我不试图理解或吸收它们的内容。我不担心他们可能涉及的所有主题。

I want to save all that thinking for the future—for a time and place when I know what I’m trying to accomplish and am seeking a knowledge building block to help me get there faster. This weekly sorting process serves as a light reminder of the knowledge I’ve accumulated over the past week, and ensures I have a healthy flow of new ideas and insights flowing into my Second Brain.  
我想把所有的想法都留到未来——在某个时间和地点, 当我知道我正在努力完成什么, 并且正在寻找一个知识构建块来帮助我更快地到达那里时。这个每周一次的分类过程可以提醒我过去一周积累的知识, 并确保我有健康的新想法和新见解流入我的第二大脑。

5. **Choose my tasks for the week.** There’s one final step in my Weekly Review. It’s time to clear the inbox in my task manager app. By this point, there are likewise a number of tasks that I’ve captured from my email, calendar, desktop, and notes, and I take a few minutes to sort them into the appropriate projects and areas.  
5. 选择我本周的任务。我的每周回顾还有最后一步。是时候清除我的任务管理器应用程序中的收件箱了。至此, 我还从我的 电子邮件、日历、桌面和笔记中捕获了一些任务, 我花了几分钟将它们分类到适当的项目和区域中。

The final step of my Weekly Review is to select the tasks I’m committing to for the upcoming week. Because I’ve just completed a sweep of my entire digital world and taken into account every piece of potentially relevant information, I can make this decision decisively and begin my week with total confidence that I’m working on the right things.  
我的每周回顾的最后一步是选择我要为下一周承诺的任务。因为我刚刚扫描了我的整个数字世界并考虑了每一条可能相关的信息, 所以我可以果断地做出这个决定并开始新的一周, 完全相信我正在做正确的事情。

#### A Monthly Review Template: Reflect for Clarity and Control  
月度审查模板: 反映清晰度和控制力

While the Weekly Review is grounded and practical, I recommend doing a Monthly Review that is a bit more reflective and holistic. It’s a chance to evaluate the big picture and consider more fundamental changes to your goals, priorities, and systems that you might not have the chance to think about in the busyness of the day-to-day.  
虽然每周回顾是扎实和实用的, 但我建议做一个更具反思性和整体性的每月回顾。这是一个评估大局并考虑对您的目标、优先事项和系统进行更根本性改变的机会, 这些是您在忙碌的日常工作中可能没有机会考虑的。

Here’s mine: 这是我的:

1.  Review and update my goals. 回顾并更新我的目标。
2.  Review and update my project list.  
    查看并更新我的项目列表。
3.  Review my areas of responsibility.  
    审查我的职责范围。
4.  Review someday/maybe tasks. 回顾某天 / 也许任务。

1. **Review and update my goals.** I start by reviewing my goals for the quarter and the year. I ask myself questions like “What successes or accomplishments did I have?” and “What went unexpectedly and what can I learn from it?” I’ll take some time to cross off any completed goals, add any new ones that have emerged, or change the scope of goals that no longer make sense.  
1. 回顾并更新我的目标。我首先回顾我的季度和年度目标。我问自己这样的问题:“我取得了哪些成功或成就?”和 “出乎意料的事情发生了什么, 我可以从中学到什么?” 我会花一些时间划掉所有已完成的目标, 添加任何新出现的目标, 或者更改不再有意义的目标范围。

2. **Review and update my project list.** Next, I’ll review and update my project list. This includes archiving any completed or canceled projects, adding any new ones, or updating active projects to reflect how they’ve changed. I will also update the folders in my notes app to reflect these changes.  
2。查看并更新我的项目列表。接下来, 我将审查并更新我的项目列表。这包括归档任何已完成或取消的项目、添加任何新项目或更新活动项目以反映它们的变化情况。我还将更新我的笔记应用程序中的文件夹以反映这些更改。

It’s important that the project list remains a current, timely, and accurate reflection of your real-life goals and priorities. Especially since projects are the central organizing principle of your Second Brain. When you have a project folder ready and waiting, your mind is primed to notice and capture the best ideas to move it forward.  
重要的是, 项目列表始终是您现实生活目标和优先事项的最新、及时和准确的反映。特别是因为项目是你的第二大脑的中心组织原则。当您准备好并等待一个项目文件夹时, 您的头脑就会注意到并捕捉最好的想法来推动它向前发展。

3. **Review my areas of responsibility.** Now it’s time to do the same for my areas of responsibility. I’ll think about the major areas of my life, such as my health, finances, relationships, and home life, and decide if there’s anything I want to change or take action on. This reflection often generates new action items (which go into my task manager) and new notes (which get captured in my notes app).  
3. 审查我的职责范围。现在是时候对我负责的领域做同样的事情了。我会考虑我生活中的主要领域, 例如我的健康、财务、人际关系和家庭生活, 并决定是否有任何我想改变或采取行动的地方。这种反思通常会生成新的操作项 (进入我的任务管理器) 和新的笔记(在我的笔记应用程序中捕获)。

Area notebooks often contain notes that become the seeds of future projects. For example, I used an area folder called “Home” to collect photos for the home studio remodel I mentioned previously. Even before it was an active project, that broader area gave me a place to collect ideas and inspiration so it was ready and waiting the moment we decided to get started.  
区域笔记本通常包含成为未来项目种子的笔记。例如, 我使用一个名为 “Home” 的区域文件夹来收集我之前提到的家庭工作室改造的照片。甚至在它成为一个活跃的项目之前, 那个更广阔的区域给了我一个收集想法和灵感的地方, 所以它已经准备好了, 等待我们决定开始的那一刻。

4. **Review someday/maybe tasks.** “Someday/maybe” is a special category for things I’d like to get to someday, but not in the near future. Things like “Learn Mandarin” and “Plant an orchard.” These kinds of future dreams are important to keep track of, but you don’t want them cluttering your priorities today. I’ll take a few minutes to go through my “someday/maybe” tasks just in case any of them have become actionable. For example, when my wife and I settled down and became homeowners, our dream of getting a dog, which was impossible while moving from one apartment to another, suddenly came within reach. I had already saved a few notes on the kinds of dogs we should consider (athletic, hypoallergenic, good with kids, etc.), and this step in my Monthly Review reminded me to bring them to the surface.  
4. 回顾某天 / 也许的任务。 “某天 / 也许”是一个特殊的类别, 用于表示我希望某天 (但不是在不久的将来) 完成的事情。诸如 “学习普通话” 和“种植果园”之类的东西。跟踪这些未来的梦想很重要, 但你不希望它们扰乱你今天的优先事项。我将花几分钟时间完成我的 “某天 / 也许” 任务, 以防万一它们中的任何一个变成了 actionable。例如, 当我和我的妻子安顿下来并成为房主时, 我们养狗的梦想突然变得触手可及, 这在从一间公寓搬到另一间公寓时是不可能的。我已经保存了一些关于我们应该考虑的狗种类的笔记(运动型、低过敏性、适合孩子等), 我每月回顾中的这一步提醒我把它们带到表面。

5. **Reprioritize tasks.** Finally, once I’ve completed all the previous steps and have a holistic picture of my goals and projects in mind, it’s time to reprioritize my tasks. I’m often surprised just how much can change in a month. To-dos that seemed critical last month might become irrelevant this month, and vice versa.  
5. 重新确定任务的优先级。最后, 一旦我完成了前面的所有步骤并对我的目标和项目有了一个全面的了解, 就该重新确定我的任务的优先级了。我常常惊讶于一个月内可以改变多少。上个月看起来很重要的待办事项本月可能变得无关紧要, 反之亦然。

### The Noticing Habits: Using Your Second Brain to Engineer Luck  
注意习惯: 用你的第二大脑来设计运气

There’s a third category of habits that will come in handy as you start putting your Second Brain into action in the real world. It is in some ways the most important category, but also the least predictable.  
当你开始在现实世界中将你的第二大脑付诸行动时, 第三类习惯会派上用场。它在某些方面是最重要的类别, 但也是最不可预测的。

I call them “noticing” habits—taking advantage of small opportunities you notice to capture something you might otherwise skip over or to make a note more actionable or discoverable. Here are some examples:  
我称它们为 “注意” 习惯——利用你注意到的小机会来捕捉你可能会跳过的东西, 或者使笔记更易于操作或更容易被发现。这里有些例子:

*   Noticing that an idea you have in mind could potentially be valuable and capturing it instead of thinking, “Oh, it’s nothing.”  
    注意到你脑海中的一个想法可能是有价值的, 并抓住它而不是想,“哦, 这没什么。”
*   Noticing when an idea you’re reading about resonates with you and taking those extra few seconds to highlight it.  
    注意你正在阅读的想法何时与你产生共鸣, 并多花几秒钟来强调它。
*   Noticing that a note could use a better title—and changing it so it’s easier for your future self to find it.  
    注意到笔记可以使用更好的标题 - 并更改它以便未来的自己更容易找到它。
*   Noticing you could move or link a note to another project or area where it will be more useful.  
    注意你可以将笔记移动或链接到另一个项目或区域, 在那里它会更有用。
*   Noticing opportunities to combine two or more Intermediate Packets into a new, larger work so you don’t have to start it from scratch.  
    注意到将两个或多个中间包组合成一个新的、更大的作品的机会, 这样你就不必从头开始。
*   Noticing a chance to merge similar content from different notes into the same note so it’s not spread around too many places.  
    注意有机会将来自不同笔记的相似内容合并到同一个笔记中, 这样它就不会散布在太多地方。
*   Noticing when an IP that you already have could help someone else solve a problem, and sharing it with them, even if it’s not perfect.  
    注意您已经拥有的 IP 何时可以帮助其他人解决问题, 并与他们分享, 即使它并不完美。

The nice thing about notes, unlike to-dos, is that they aren’t urgent. If one important to-do gets overlooked, the results could be catastrophic. Notes, on the other hand, can easily be put on hold any time you get busy, without any negative impact. If you have the time to organize your notes each week, that’s great. If you don’t, it’s no problem. I often will wait weeks or even a month or longer before I find the time to clear my notes inbox. They remain ready and waiting there for as long as I need.  
与待办事项不同, 笔记的好处在于它们并不紧急。如果忽略了一项重要的任务, 结果可能是灾难性的。另一方面, 您可以在忙碌的任何时候轻松地搁置笔记, 而不会产生任何负面影响。如果您每周都有时间整理笔记, 那就太好了。如果你不这样做, 那没问题。我经常要等几周甚至一个月或更长时间才能找到时间清理我的笔记收件箱。只要我需要, 他们就会随时待命。

The most common misconception about organizing I see when I’m working with clients is the belief that organizing requires a heavy lift. They seem to believe that if they could just block off their calendar and get a few days free of pressing commitments, then they’d finally be able to curb the clutter and clear their head.  
当我与客户合作时, 我看到的关于组织的最常见误解是认为组织需要举重。他们似乎相信, 如果他们能把日程安排好, 腾出几天时间来处理紧迫的事情, 那么他们最终就能摆脱混乱, 理清头绪。

Even on the rare occasions I’ve seen people somehow manage to clear such a big block of time, it never seems to go very well. They tend to get bogged down in minutiae and barely make a dent in the mountain of accumulated stuff they wanted to tackle. Then they’re saddled with a feeling of guilt that they weren’t able to make progress even with so much time at their disposal. It’s not natural for humans to completely reorganize their entire world all at once. There are too many layers, too many facets of a human life, to perfectly square every little detail.  
即使在极少数情况下, 我也见过人们以某种​​方式设法清理这么长的时间, 但似乎从来都不会很顺利。他们往往陷入细枝末节的泥潭, 而几乎没有在他们想要解决的堆积如山的问题上有所作为。然后他们会感到内疚, 因为即使有那么多时间可以支配, 他们也无法取得进步。人类一下子完全重组整个世界是不自然的。 太多层, 人类生活的太多方面, 无法完美地处理每一个小细节。

It’s crucial to stay organized, but it needs to be done a little at a time in the flow of our normal lives. It needs to be done in the in-between moments of moving your projects forward as you notice small opportunities for improvement.  
保持井井有条是至关重要的, 但在我们的正常生活中需要一次完成一点。它需要在项目向前推进的中间时刻完成, 因为您注意到很小的改进机会。

Here are more specific examples of what those opportunities might look like:  
以下是这些机会的更具体示例:

*   You decide to visit Costa Rica on your next vacation, so you move a note with useful Spanish phrases from your “Languages” resource folder to a “Costa Rica” project folder to aid in your trip.  
    您决定在下一个假期访问哥斯达黎加, 因此您将带有有用西班牙语短语的注释从 “语言” 资源文件夹移动到 “哥斯达黎加” 项目文件夹以帮助您的旅行。
*   Your director of engineering leaves for another job and you need to hire a new one, so you move the folder you created last time for “Engineering hire” from archives to projects to guide your search.  
    您的工程主管去另一份工作, 而您需要雇用新的, 因此您将上次为 “工程雇用” 创建的文件夹从档案移动到项目以指导您的搜索。
*   You schedule the next in a series of workshops you are facilitating and move a PDF with workshop exercises from an area folder called “Workshops” to a new project folder for the specific workshop you’re planning.  
    您在您正在促进的一系列研讨会中安排下一个, 并将带有研讨会练习的 PDF 从名为 “Workshops” 的区域文件夹移动到您正在计划的特定研讨会的新项目文件夹。
*   You notice that you need to buy a new computer because your current one is getting too slow, so you move some articles you’ve saved from the “Computer research” resource folder to a new project folder called “Buy a new computer.”  
    您注意到您需要购买一台新计算机, 因为您当前的计算机速度太慢, 因此您将保存的一些文章从 “计算机研究” 资源文件夹移动到一个名为 “购买新计算机” 的新项目文件夹中。

All these actions take mere moments, and are made in response to changes in your priorities and goals. We should avoid doing a lot of heavy lifting up front, not only because it takes up precious time and energy, but because it locks us into a course of action that might not end up being right.  
所有这些行动只需片刻, 是为了响应您的优先事项和目标的变化而做出的。我们应该避免预先做很多繁重的工作, 不仅因为这会占用宝贵的时间和精力, 而且因为它会将我们锁定在一个最终可能不正确的行动过程中。

When you make your digital notes a _working_ environment, not just a storage environment, you end up spending a lot more time there. When you spend more time there, you’ll inevitably notice many more small opportunities for change than you expect. Over time, this will gradually produce an environment far more suited to your real needs than anything you could have planned up front. Just like professional chefs keep their environment organized with small nudges and adjustments, you can use noticing habits to “organize as you go.”  
当你把你的数字笔记变成一个工作环境, 而不仅仅是一个存储环境时, 你最终会花更多的时间在那里。当你花更多时间在那里时, 你不可避免地会注意到比你预期更多的小改变机会。随着时间的推移, 这将逐渐产生一个比您预先计划的任何环境都更适合您实际需求的环境。就像专业厨师通过微调和调整来保持环境井井有条一样, 您可以养成注意的习惯来 “边走边整理”。

### Your Turn: A Perfect System You Don’t Use Isn’t Perfect  
轮到你了: 你不使用的完美系统并不完美

Each of the three kinds of habits I’ve introduced you to—Project Kickoff and Completion Checklists, Weekly and Monthly Reviews, and Noticing Habits—are all meant to be performed quickly in the in-between spaces of your day.  
我向您介绍的三种习惯中的每一种——项目启动和完成清单、每周和每月回顾, 以及注意习惯——都应该在一天的间隙中快速养成。

They are designed to build on activities you are probably already doing in some form, adding perhaps a little bit more structure. These shouldn’t be massive feats, requiring you to set aside huge chunks of time in total Zen-like isolation. That’s not realistic, and if you wait until those perfect conditions arrive, you’ll never take even the first step.  
它们旨在建立在您可能已经以某种形式进行的活动的基础上, 并可能添加更多结构。这些不应该是巨大的壮举, 需要你在完全禅宗般的孤立中留出大量时间。这是不现实的, 如果你等到那些完美的条件到来, 你甚至都不会迈出第一步。

The checklists I’ve provided are a starting point to help you add some predictability in an environment that is often chaotic and unpredictable. They provide a regular cadence of actions for taking in, processing, and making use of digital information, without requiring you to stop everything and reorganize everything all at once.  
我提供的清单是一个起点, 可帮助您在通常混乱且不可预测的环境中增加一些可预测性。它们为接收、处理和使用数字信息提供了固定的行动节奏, 而不需要您立即停止一切并重新组织一切。

I want to remind you that the maintenance of your Second Brain is very forgiving. Unlike a car engine, nothing will explode, break down, or burst into flames if you let things slide for days, weeks, or even months. The entire point of building a Second Brain and pouring your thoughts into it is to make those thoughts less vulnerable to the passage of time. They will be ready to pick up right where you left off when you have more time or motivation.  
我想提醒你, 你的第二大脑的维护是非常宽容的。与汽车发动机不同, 如果让物体滑动数天、数周或 甚至数月, 任何东西都不会爆炸、分解或起火。构建第二大脑并将你的想法倾注其中的全部意义在于让这些想法不那么容易受到时间流逝的影响。当您有更多时间或动力时, 他们将准备好从您停下的地方继续。

To make this concrete: 为了使这个具体:

*   There’s no need to capture every idea; the best ones will always come back around eventually.  
    没有必要捕捉每一个想法; 最好的最终总会回来。
*   There’s no need to clear your inbox frequently; unlike your to-do list, there’s no negative consequence if you miss a given note.  
    无需经常清理收件箱; 与你的待办事项列表不同, 如果你错过了给定的笔记, 不会有任何负面影响。
*   There’s no need to review or summarize notes on a strict timeline; we’re not trying to memorize their contents or keep them top of mind.  
    无需在严格的时间表上复习或总结笔记; 我们并不是要记住它们的内容或将它们放在首位。
*   When organizing notes or files within PARA, it’s a very forgiving decision of where to put something, since search is so effective as a backup option.  
    在 PARA 中组织笔记或文件时, 把东西放在哪里是一个非常宽容的决定, 因为搜索作为备份选项非常有效。

The truth is, any system that must be perfect to be reliable is deeply flawed. A perfect system you don’t use because it’s too complicated and error prone isn’t a perfect system—it’s a fragile system that will fall apart as soon as you turn your attention elsewhere.  
事实是, 任何必须完美才能可靠的系统都存在严重缺陷。一个你因为太复杂和容易出错而不使用的完美系统并不是一个完美的系统——它是一个脆弱的系统, 一旦你将注意力转移到别处就会崩溃。

We have to remember that we are not building an encyclopedia of immaculately organized knowledge. We are building a _working system_. Both in the sense that it must work, and in the sense that it is a regular part of our everyday lives. For that reason, you should prefer a system that is imperfect, but that continues to be useful in the real conditions of your life.  
我们必须记住, 我们并不是在建立一个组织严密的知识百科全书。我们正在建立一个工作系统。从它必须起作用的意义上说, 从它是我们日常生活的常规部分的意义上说。出于这个原因, 你应该更喜欢一个不完美的系统, 但它在你生活的真实条件下仍然有用。

1.  [I](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-004-backlink). A premortem is a useful practice, similar to a postmortem used to analyze how a project went wrong, except performed _before_ the project starts. By asking what is likely to go wrong, you can take action to prevent it from happening in the first place.  
    我 。事前剖析是一种有用的做法, 类似于用于分析项目如何出错的事后剖析, 只是在项目开始之前进行。通过询问可能会出现什么问题, 您可以采取措施在第一时间防止它发生。
2.  [II](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-003-backlink). Although outside the scope of this book, I’ve included my recommendations for task managers on multiple operating systems in the Second Brain Resource Guide at [Buildingasecondbrain.com/resources](http://www.buildingasecondbrain.com/resources).  
    二。虽然超出了本书的范围, 但我在 Buildingasecondbrain.com/resources 的第二脑资源指南中包含了我对多操作系统任务管理器的建议。
3.  [III](https://1paragraph.app/2023-02-06T09%3A26%3A12.191Z#footnote-002-backlink). The book _Getting Things Done_, known as GTD, is a helpful counterpart to personal knowledge management, applying the same lens of “getting things off your mind” that we are using for notes to “actionable” information such as to-dos.  
    三、这本书 Getting Things Done, 被称为 GTD, 是个人知识管理的一个有用的对应物, 它应用了我们用于注释 “可操作” 信息 (如待办事项) 的相同镜头“让事情从你的脑海中消失”。

Chapter 10 The Path of Self-Expression  
第 10 章 自我表达之路
------------------------------------------------------

> An idea wants to be shared. And, in the sharing, it becomes more complex, more interesting, and more likely to work for more people.  
> 一个想法想要分享。并且, 在分享中, 它变得更复杂, 更有趣, 更可能为更多人工作。
> 
> —adrienne maree brown, writer and activist  
> ——艾德丽安 · 玛丽 · 布朗, 作家和活动家

For most of history, humanity’s challenge was how to acquire scarce information. There was hardly any good information to be found anywhere. It was locked up in difficult-to-reproduce manuscripts or stuck in the heads of scholars. Access to information was limited, but that wasn’t a problem for most people. Their lives and livelihoods didn’t require much information. Their main contribution was their physical labor, not their ideas.  
在历史的大部分时间里, 人类面临的挑战是如何获取稀缺信息。到处都找不到任何好的信息。它被锁在难以复制的手稿中或卡在学者的脑海中。获取信息的机会有限, 但这对大多数人来说不是问题。他们的生活和生计不需要太多信息。他们的主要贡献是他们的体力劳动, 而不是他们的想法。

That has all changed in just the last few decades. Historically, in the blink of an eye. Suddenly, we are all plugged into an infinite stream of data, updated continuously and delivered at light speed via a network of intelligent devices embedded in every corner of our lives.  
在过去的几十年里, 这一切都发生了变化。历史, 眨眼之间。突然间, 我们都被插入了无限的数据流中, 通过嵌入我们生活各个角落的智能设备网络不断更新并以光速传输。

Not only that, but the very nature of labor has changed. Value has shifted from the output of our muscles to the output of our brains. Our knowledge is now our most important asset and the ability to deploy our attention our most valuable skill. The tools of our trade have become abstract and immaterial: the building blocks of ideas, insights, facts, frameworks, and mental models.  
不仅如此, 劳动的本质也发生了变化。价值已经从我们肌肉的输出转移到我们大脑的输出。现在, 我们的知识是我们最重要的资产, 而集中注意力的能力是我们最宝贵的技能。我们交易的工具 已经变得抽象和非物质: 思想、见解、事实、框架和心智模型的基石。

Now our challenge isn’t to acquire more information; as we saw in the exploration of divergence and convergence, it is to find ways to close off the stream so we can get something done. Any change in how we interact with information first requires a change in how we think. In this chapter we’ll explore what it looks like and feels like to make that shift.  
现在我们的挑战不是获取更多信息, 而是获取更多信息。正如我们在发散和趋同的探索中看到的那样, 就是找到关闭流的方法, 这样我们才能完成一些事情。我们与信息互动方式的任何改变首先需要我们思考方式的改变。在本章中, 我们将探索做出这种转变的样子和感觉。

### Mindset Over Toolset—The Quest for the Perfect App  
Mindset Over Toolset——追求完美的应用程序

The majority of this book has been about acquiring a new set of tools in your relationship to information. However, over the years I’ve noticed that it is never a person’s toolset that constrains their potential, it’s their mindset.  
本书的大部分内容都是关于在您与信息的关系中获得一套新工具的。然而, 多年来我注意到, 限制一个人潜力的从来不是工具, 而是他们的心态。

You might have arrived at this book because you heard about this new field called personal knowledge management, or maybe when you were trying to find guidance in how to use a cool new notetaking app. Maybe you were drawn in by the promise of new techniques for enhancing your productivity, or perhaps it was the allure of a systematic approach to creativity.  
你可能是因为听说过这个叫做个人知识管理的新领域, 或者当你试图寻找如何使用一个很酷的新笔记应用程序的指导时才读到这本书的。也许您是被提高生产力的新技术的承诺所吸引, 或者也许是系统化的创造力方法所具有的吸引力。

Whatever you are looking for, all these paths eventually lead to the same place, if you are willing to follow: a journey of personal growth. There is no divide between our inner selves and our digital lives: the beliefs and attitudes that shape our thinking in one context inevitably show up in other contexts as well.  
无论您在寻找什么, 如果您愿意追随, 所有这些路径最终都会通向同一个地方: 个人成长之旅。我们的内在自我和我们的数字生活之间没有界限: 在一种情况下塑造我们思维的信念和态度也不可避免地会出现在其他情况下。

Underlying our struggles and challenges with productivity, creativity, and performance is our fundamental relationship to the information in our lives. That relationship was forged during your upbringing as you encountered new experiences, and was influenced by your personality, learning style, relationships, and your genes. You learned to react in a certain way when faced with new ideas. You adopted a default “blueprint” for how you treated incoming information—with anticipation, fear, excitement, self-doubt, or some complex mix of feelings that is unique to you.  
我们在生产力、创造力和绩效方面的斗争和挑战背后是我们与生活中信息的基本关系。这种关系是在你成长过程中遇到新的经历时建立起来的, 并受到 你的个性、学习方式、人际关系和你的基因的影响。你学会了在面对新想法时以某种方式做出反应。你采用了一个默认的 “蓝图” 来处理你如何处理收到的信息——带着期待、恐惧、兴奋、自我怀疑, 或者一些你独有的复杂感觉。

That default attitude to information colors every aspect of your life. It is the lens through which you studied for classes and took tests in school. It set the stage for the kinds of jobs and careers you pursued. At this very moment, as you read these words, that default attitude is working in the background. It is telling you what to think about what you’re reading—how to interpret it, how to feel about it, and how it applies to you.  
这种对信息的默认态度影响着你生活的方方面面。它是您学习课程和在学校参加考试的镜头。它为您从事的各种工作和职业奠定了基础。就在这一刻, 当你读到这些文字时, 那种默认的态度正在背后发挥作用。它告诉你如何看待你正在阅读的内容——如何解释它, 如何感受它, 以及它如何适用于你。

Our attitude toward information profoundly shapes how we see and understand the world and our place in it. Our success in the workforce depends on our ability to make use of information more effectively and to think better, smarter, faster. As society gets ever more complex, this emphasis on personal intelligence is only increasing. The quality of our thinking has become one of the central defining features of our identity, our reputation, and our quality of life. We are constantly advised that we need to know more to be able to achieve our goals and dreams.  
我们对信息的态度深刻地塑造了我们如何看待和理解世界以及我们在其中的位置。我们在职场上的成功取决于我们更有效地利用信息以及更好、更聪明、更快地思考的能力。随着社会变得越来越复杂, 对个人智慧的重视程度只会越来越高。我们的思维质量已成为我们身份、声誉和生活质量的核心定义特征之一。我们不断被告知, 我们需要了解更多才能实现我们的目标和梦想。

What would you say if I told you that isn’t true?  
如果我告诉你那不是真的, 你会怎么说?

### The Fear Our Minds Can’t Do Enough  
害怕我们的思想做不够

When it comes to accomplishing our goals, it’s not that innate intelligence isn’t valuable. I’m saying that the greater the burden you place on your biological brain to give you everything you want and need, the more it will struggle under the weight of it all. You’ll feel more stressed, anxious, like there are way too many balls in the air. The more time your brain spends striving to achieve and overcome and solve problems, the less time you have left over for imagining, creating, and simply enjoying the life you’re living. The brain can solve problems, but that isn’t its sole purpose. Your mind was meant for much more.  
当谈到实现我们的目标时, 并不是说先天的智慧没有价值。我是说, 为了给你想要和需要的一切, 你给你的生物大脑施加的负担越大, 它就越会在这一切的重压下挣扎。你会感到压力更大、更焦虑, 就像空气中有太多的球一样。你的大脑花在努力实现和克服以及 解决问题上的时间越多, 你留给想象、创造和享受你现在生活的时间就越少。大脑可以解决问题, 但这不是它的唯一目的。你的思想意味着更多。

It is this fundamental attitude toward information that will start to change as you integrate your Second Brain into your life. You will begin to see connections you didn’t know you could make. Ideas about business, psychology, and technology will connect and spawn new revelations you’ve never consciously considered. Lessons from art, philosophy, and history will intermingle to give you epiphanies about how the world works. You will naturally start to combine these ideas to form new perspectives, new theories, and new strategies. You will be filled with a sense of awe at the elegance of the system you’ve created, and how it works in almost mysterious ways to bring to your attention the information you need.  
当您将第二大脑融入您的生活时, 这种对信息的基本态度将开始发生变化。您将开始看到您不知道可以建立的联系。关于商业、心理学和技术的想法将联系起来并产生你从未有意识地考虑过的新启示。艺术、哲学和历史的教训将交织在一起, 让你顿悟世界是如何运作的。你会很自然地开始将这些想法结合起来, 形成新的视角、新的理论和新的策略。您将惊叹于您所创建的系统的优雅, 以及它如何以近乎神秘的方式工作以引起您的注意您需要的信息。

Maybe you don’t see yourself as a writer, creator, or expert. I certainly didn’t when I first started taking notes on my health problems. Once you start seeing even your biggest ambitions in terms of the smaller chunks of information they are made up of, you’ll begin to realize that any experience or passing insight can be valuable. Your fears, doubts, mistakes, missteps, failures, and self-criticism—it’s all just information to be taken in, processed, and made sense of. All of it is part of a larger, ever-evolving whole.  
也许您不认为自己是作家、创作者或专家。当我第一次开始记录我的健康问题时, 我当然没有。一旦你开始从组成小块信息的角度看待你最大的抱负, 你就会开始意识到任何经验或短暂的洞察力都是有价值的。你的恐惧、怀疑、错误、失误、失败和自我批评——这些都只是需要接受、处理和理解的信息。所有这些都是一个更大的、不断发展的整体的一部分。

A participant in one of my courses named Amelia recently told me that starting to build her Second Brain had caused her to make a 180-degree change in her relationship to the Internet. She had seen it as “sensationalistic and offensive” and, as a result, hadn’t wanted to engage with the online world at all. Once she had a place where she could curate the best of the Internet while ignoring everything that didn’t serve her, she told me she began to see it in a completely new light. Amelia is a skilled leadership coach who runs a clinic teaching leaders how to manage their nervous systems to improve their well-being and effectiveness. Imagine how many more people she can reach with her expertise now that she sees the Internet as a source of wisdom and connection, not just noise.  
我的一门课程的参与者 Amelia 最近告诉我, 开始构建她的第二大脑使她与互联网的关系发生了 180 度的转变。她认为这是 “煽情和冒犯性的”, 因此根本不想参与网络世界。一旦她有了一个地方, 在那里她可以挑选最好的互联网, 同时忽略一切对她不利的东西, 她告诉我她开始以全新的眼光看待它。 Amelia 是一位技术娴熟的领导力教练, 她经营一家诊所, 教授 leaders 如何管理他们的神经系统以提高他们的幸福感和效率。想象一下, 既然她将互联网视为智慧和联系的源泉, 而不仅仅是噪音, 她可以用她的专业知识接触到更多的人。

How does such a dramatic change happen? Amelia didn’t necessarily learn a new fact that she didn’t know before. She took on a new perspective. She chose to look at the world through a different lens—the lens of appreciation and abundance. We can’t always control what happens to us, but we can choose the lens we look through. This is the basic choice we have in creating our own experience—which aspects to nourish or starve, using only the magnifying power of our attention.  
如此戏剧性的变化是如何发生的? 阿米莉亚不一定了解她以前不知道的新事实。她采用了新的视角。她选择通过一个不同的镜头——欣赏和丰富的镜头来看待这个世界。我们不能总是控制发生在我们身上的事情, 但我们可以选择我们看的镜头。这是我们在创造自己的体验时所拥有的基本选择——滋养或饥饿哪些方面, 只使用我们注意力的放大力量。

As you build a Second Brain, your biological brain will inevitably change. It will start to adapt to the presence of this new technological appendage, treating it as an extension of itself. Your mind will become calmer, knowing that every idea is being tracked. It will become more focused, knowing it can put thoughts on hold and access them later. I often hear that people start to feel a tremendous sense of conviction—for their goals, their dreams, and the things they want to change or influence in the world—because they know they have a powerful system behind them amplifying every move they make.  
当你建立第二大脑时, 你的生物大脑将不可避免地发生变化。它将开始适应这种新技术附属物的存在, 将其视为自身的延伸。你的头脑会变得更加平静, 因为你知道每个想法都在被追踪。它会变得更加专注, 知道它可以搁置想法并在以后访问它们。我经常听到人们开始对他们的目标、梦想以及他们想要改变或影响世界的事物产生强烈的信念感, 因为他们知道他们背后有一个强大的系统在放大他们所做的每一个动作。

### Giving Your First Brain a New Job  
给你的第一个大脑一份新工作

Instead of trying to optimize your mind so that it can manage every tiny detail of your life, it’s time to fire your biological brain from that job and give it a new one: as the CEO of your life, orchestrating and managing the process of turning information into results. We’re asking your biological brain to hand over the job of remembering to an external system, and by doing so, freeing it to absorb and integrate new knowledge in more creative ways.
